Received 
      11th December 2020
    First published on 12th May 2021AbstractDespite the rapid development of perovskite solar cells (PSCs) over the past few years, the conversion of solar energy into electricity is not efficient enough or cost-competitive yet. The principal energy loss in the conversion of solar energy to electricity fundamentally originates from the non-absorption of low-energy photons ascribed to Shockley–Queisser limits and thermalization losses of high-energy photons. Enhancing the light-harvesting efficiency of the perovskite photoactive layer by developing efficient photo management strategies with functional materials and arrays remains a long-standing challenge. Here, we briefly review the historical research trials and future research trends to overcome the fundamental loss mechanisms in PSCs, including upconversion, downconversion, scattering, tandem/graded structures, texturing, anti-reflection, and luminescent solar concentrators. We will deeply emphasize the availability and analyze the importance of a fine device structure, fluorescence efficiency, material proportion, and integration position for performance improvement. The unique energy level structure arising from the 4fn inner shell configuration of the trivalent rare-earth ions gives multifarious options for efficient light-harvesting by upconversion and downconversion. Tandem or graded PSCs by combining a series of subcells with varying bandgaps seek to rectify the spectral mismatch. Plasmonic nanostructures function as a secondary light source to augment the light-trapping within the perovskite layer and carrier transporting layer, enabling enhanced carrier generation. Texturing the interior using controllable micro/nanoarrays can realize light–matter interactions. Anti-reflective coatings on the top glass cover of the PSCs bring about better transmission and glare reduction. Photon concentration through perovskite-based luminescent solar concentrators offers a path to increase efficiency at reduced cost and plays a role in building-integrated photovoltaics. Distinct from other published reviews, we here systematically and hierarchically present all of the photon management strategies in PSCs by presenting the theoretical possibilities and summarizing the experimental results, expecting to inspire future research in the field of photovoltaics, phototransistors, photoelectrochemical sensors, photocatalysis, and especially light-emitting diodes. We further assess the overall possibilities of the strategies based on ultimate efficiency prospects, material requirements, and developmental outlook.
Cong Chen
Cong Chen received his PhD degree from Jilin University in June 2019. Previously, he studied and did scientific research at Jilin University focused on perovskites, and quantum dots sensitized solar cells under the supervision of Prof. Hongwei Song. In July 2019, he joined Hebei University of technology as an associate professor. He is the youngest special-term “Yuanguang scholar” in Hebei University of Technology. His main topic of interest is the design of solar cells having high efficiency.
Hongwei Song
Hongwei Song received his PhD degree in Condensed Material Physics from Changchun Institute of Physics, Chinese Academy of Science (CAS) in 1996. From 1996 to 2000, he worked as a postdoctoral researcher in the Institute of Physics, CAS, Nagoya Institute of Technology, and University of California at Berkeley in turn. Since 2007 he has worked at Jilin University as a full professor. He is currently an editorial advisory board member of Scientific Reports. He has published over 300 scientific papers and two book chapters. His research interests have been focused on the spectral physics of rare-earth ions and their optoelectronic applications.
1. Introduction
Dealing with the energy crisis is an urgent task for current human needs. Among a variety of emerging renewable energy sources (biomass energy, wind energy, hydroelectric power, geothermal energy, and nuclear energy), solar energy has been considered the most abundant, renewable, and environment-friendly energy form.1–5 If 0.1% of the sunlight that reaches the earth's surface could be converted by photovoltaics with an average power conversion efficiency (PCE) of 10%, the converted electrical energy can fully meet our energy needs.6 Photovoltaic technologies produce electricity from sunlight using solar cells and have the potency to meet a considerable fraction of future energy demands and deal with the energy crisis and environmental issues.7 Creutzig et al. concluded that photovoltaics could supply 30–50% of electricity in competitive markets.8 To date, silicon (Si) based solar cells represent about 90% of the world's total photovoltaic production.9–11 Latterly, organic and inorganic hybrid perovskite-based solar cells (PSCs) have undergone rapid development and shown great potential as next-generation photovoltaics due to their high PCEs and low-cost solution process.12–14 Hybrid perovskites form a large group of crystals that all share the same type of crystal structure based on CaTiO3, namely ABX3 (Fig. 1a), with A cations, such as methylammonium (MA+) and cesium (Cs+); B cations such as tin (Sn2+) and lead (Pb2+); and halides, such as iodide (I−), bromide (Br−), and chloride (Cl−), as the X anion (Fig. 1b).4,15,16 Some derivatives “A2BB′X6” double perovskite materials have also been studied.17–22 In 2009, Kojima et al. applied MAPbBr3 and MAPbI3 as an absorber for the first time in dye-sensitized solar cells (DSSCs) and achieved a PCE of 3.8%.23 Currently, PSCs have reached PCEs above 25%, outperforming Cu(In,Ga)(Se,S)2, CdTe, and Si-based solar cells.24–29 This expeditious and unexampled progress in PCE is due in part to intense efforts to develop novel device architectures that are energetically favorable for carrier generation and extraction, coupled with regulating the crystallization process of perovskite photoactive films.30–32 However, the record efficiency of 25.5% is still below the theoretical limit of a single-junction photovoltaic device due to the energy loss from the intrinsic properties of the perovskite photoactive material.33–35 In detail, the energy loss is related to low energy photons that cannot be absorbed while high energy photons are not used efficiently. In the optical aspect, there is still much room to further improve the absorption of PSCs by reducing the reflection loss and parasitic absorption.36–39
 Fig. 1  (a) The crystalline structure of the (a-1) typical ABX3 and (a-2) double A2BB′X6 perovskite; (b) potential A-site cations, metals, and halides for perovskite structure, and (c) bandgaps (left) and best PCEs (right) of variously studied perovskites; suitable materials for solar cells should have direct bandgaps of around 1.1 to 2.0 eV. (d) Record PCEs for single-junction solar cells plotted as a function of perovskite bandgap and compared to the Shockley–Queisser limit. Reprinted with permission from ref. 60, copyright 2016, AAAS. 
For the low energy photon loss, the photons’ energy is not enough to excite the semiconductor and generate electron–hole pairs.40,41 As we know, AM 1.5G sunlight extends over a wide range of usable wavelengths of 280–2500 nm (∼0.5–4.4 eV).30,42 The bandgap and best-recorded PCEs of various typical perovskite materials (Fig. 1c) indicate the inefficient use of the solar spectrum. Fortunately, there are some low bandgap constituents with perovskite structures that have attractive properties.43–45 The representative Sn or Sn/Pb-based halides, MASnI3, FASnI3, and CsSnI3 could exhibit direct bandgaps of around 1.20, 1.41, and 1.3 eV, respectively, which are even narrower and more attractive than the pure Pb-based or Bi/Sb-based analogues.46–51 However, the Sn-based perovskite layer tends to degrade due to the natural oxidization of Sn2+ to Sn4+, which deteriorates the semiconducting properties. The morphology of ASnX3 perovskite will also result in reduced photovoltaic behavior and stability.52–56 Generally, the spectral response range of PSCs is mainly concentrated in the visible region.57–59 The PCE of PSCs is limited by the Shockley–Queisser limit and could only achieve about 31% for non-concentrated sunlight irradiation with an optimized bandgap (Fig. 1d).60 From the perspective of the total energy losses, previous studies demonstrated that the below bandgap loss is the dominant loss process for PSCs with a bandgap of 1.55 eV, which accounts for 41.1%.61–64 This brings a major issue for enhancing the light-harvesting and reducing the energy loss of PSCs since most of the solar irradiation lies in the UV and IR regions.65 Given the 1.6 eV bandgap perovskite, the corresponding PSCs could utilize 300–775 nm region incident photons, while wasting the sunlight region from 775 nm to 2500 nm,66,67 indicating that a considerable part of the energy over the near-infrared (NIR) region cannot be utilized by the most studied perovskite layer. Obviously, insufficient spectral utilization has been a big obstacle to improve further the short-circuit current densities (JSC) and PCE of PSCs.50,52,68–72
The inefficiently used high-energy photons are usually related to the thermalization of carriers and optical loss.60,73–77 As for the thermalization of carriers, a high-energy photon can only generate one electron–hole pair. When a photon with an energy Eph exceeding the perovskite bandgap Egap is absorbed, the excess energy Eph − Egap is typically lost to phonon emission, i.e., heat.78–80 As for the optical loss, it also originated from the short light path in the perovskite layer, the absorption losses in the transparent conductive oxide (ITO, FTO, etc.) layer, ETL, HTL, and back electrode (Au, Ag, Cu, etc.) due to their non-zero extinction coefficient, and the reflections at the air/glass interface.37,67,81–83 From the perspective of theory and experiment, previous results show that the dominant optical loss can reach 25% for experimentally optimized PSCs with the power conversion efficiency of 19%, while the optical loss climbs up to 40% for a thin-active-layer design.84 With the rapid development of the photovoltaic industry over the past few decades, there is an urgent need for enhancing the light absorption capacity of perovskite materials and broadening the optical response range of devices by utilizing the UV and NIR parts of sunlight efficiently.85 The present review highlights the latest advances to reduce the spectral mismatch losses and broaden the optical response range of PSCs in improving the light-harvesting of PSCs.
An alternative approach to increase the theoretical efficiency beyond the Shockley–Queisser limit is to adapt the solar spectrum through upconversion (UC).67,86,87 The original research on UC can be dated back to the early work in 1959 by Bloembergen, who introduced rare-earth ions (REs)-doped materials as NIR quantum counters.88 Today, UC is a rapidly developing research field in bioimaging, medical therapy, lasers, detector, sensors, and solar energy harvesting.89–92 It is a fascinating nonlinear optical phenomenon, which enables to turn low-energy photon into higher-energy luminescence emissions.93–95 In 1983, Saxena firstly proposed the application of UC in solar cells.96 Based on the maximum efficiency of single-junction solar cells, Trupke and Green studied the theory of UC enhancement and revealed that a configuration consisting of a solar cell with a bandgap of 2 eV and an ideal upconverter system attached to its rear could achieve a maximum PCE of 47.6% under constant sunlight and 62.18% under concentrated sunlight.97 Latterly, Johnson and Conibeer calculated that the limited theoretical PCE of a 1.2 eV Si-based solar cell with an additional ideal upconverter illuminated by nonconcentrated light is 40%, higher than that of 33.25% for a radiative limit efficiency.67 Therefore, integrating the UC material layer inside or outside of a solar cell, especially for the most efficient but wide-bandgap Pb-based PSCs, will realize the effective use of low energy photon. Benefiting from the UC mechanism, breaking through the intrinsic flaw limitation of the bandgap for PSCs is possible.98–100 Recently, researchers put intense research efforts into RE-doped UC nanomaterials to reduce the sub-bandgap energy loss in PSCs. We here will focus on the UC to broaden the response in the NIR spectrum and enhance the efficiency of PSCs.
In the case of downconversion (DC), it is the optical process of converting high-energy photons into lower-energy ones, which can be efficiently absorbed by the solar cell.101–104 The primary DC materials include RE doped compounds, semiconductor oxides, organic dyes, and quantum dots. DC has shown the potential to improve the JSC for various solar cell technologies.103,105–107 Previously, R. Rothemund presented an optical model to investigate the positive effect of DC layers on photovoltaic module performance.108 In another investigation, Lipovšek et al. used 3-D ray tracing and Mie scattering theory to predict that solar cells could exhibit an increased JSC by 6% from a DC layer.109,110 The model accounted for several parameters, such as layer thickness, phosphor size distribution, and volume concentration. Attempting to extend the spectral response range of PSCs to the UV region represents a fundamental approach to increasing the UV light utilization and, thus, the efficiency of PSCs. In addition to the spectrum utilization, it has been shown that the PSCs suffer from stability problems associated with incident UV radiation on TiO2, which is the typical electron-transporting material but exhibits photocatalytic features for the degradation of perovskite. DC materials applied to PSCs could exhibit the function of UV protection but not UV blocking.62,111,112 It can also be applied to enhance the spectral response of PSCs to lower wavelengths (190–400 nm).113 In this regard, the UC strategy enabling simultaneous improvement in the UV-light absorption and stability could be in high demand.
Considering the below-bandgap absorption loss and the thermal-relaxation loss of hot carrier, the PCE of single-junction solar cells cannot exceed the Shockley–Quiesser limit.114,115 From the perspective of device structure design, the concept of tandem cells can be introduced to broaden the spectral response of photovoltaic devices through complementary absorption.116,117 Tandem or graded cells, which are usually connected either mechanically or monolithically into a single configuration, seek to rectify the spectral mismatch by combining a series of subcells with varying bandgaps.118–120 The concept of tandem cells was initially proposed by Wolf in 1960.40,121 Recently, the national renewable energy laboratory (NREL) has refreshed a best PCE of 32.9% of double junction (not concentrated) thin-film solar cells.24,25 In double-junction tandem solar cells, a “top cell” with a large bandgap absorbs high-energy photons but permits lower-energy photons to transmit. Then the transmitted photon is then utilized by the “bottom cell” which has a small bandgap. In such a situation, the higher-energy photons generate a high open voltage (VOC) to reduce the thermalization loss in the top cell while the transmitted low-energy photons are absorbed in the bottom cell, leading to broader harvesting of the solar spectrum. In the PSCs configuration, several cells with different bandgaps can be monolithically connected in series, which is a promising strategy for improving the PCE of single-junction solar cells beyond the S–Q limit. Several distinct advantages illustrated that the PSCs could serve as the candidate for tandem solar cell application. The first and most important, the bandgap of the perovskite layer can be tuned in the range of 1.17–3.10 eV through compositional engineering.11,122 Secondly, PSCs possess a higher and more stabilized PCE, compared to traditional organic solar cells and DSSCs.123–127 Third, the perovskite materials are earth-abundant, low-cost, and low-temperature processing.128,129 Almansouri et al. have predicted that a practical PCE of ≈30% in two-terminal (2T) perovskite/Si tandem solar cell is achievable.33,130 In 2020, the NREL announced that the latest certified efficiency of the 2T perovskite/Si tandem solar cell is 29.5%, achieved by Oxford photovoltaic.25 The existing review papers have made a very systematic summary of the tandem cell of perovskite/Si, perovskite/CIGS, and perovskite/CdTe-based photovoltaics.114,117,120,131–134 We here will focus on the review of perovskite/perovskite, perovskite/organic, perovskite/dye, and perovskite/quantum dots tandem solar cells, which could extend the spectral response of PSCs.
In addition to the conversion and broadening of the spectrum, it is also an essential factor for improving the performance of PSCs to adequately respond to the response wavelength range (300–800 nm). Plasmonic effects, which is the interaction of incident light photons with the conduction electrons of metal nanostructures, have been considered as an efficient route for increasing light absorption.135,136 The optical properties of metal nanostructures have been studied since the time of Faraday.137 In recent years, the research interest in metal nanoparticles has intensified, in particular, due to the discovery of Raman scattering.138–140 Plasmonic metal nanostructures (such as nanoparticles, nanorods, nanoshells, nanostars, etc.) have novel optical properties like surface plasmon resonance (SPR), which arise due to the collective oscillation of conduction electrons at the metal surface induced by the incident electromagnetic field.137,141,142 Until now, there has been a great deal of work in the fields of optical communication, surface-enhanced spectroscopy, and solar cells. Especially in thin-film solar cells, substantial local field enhancement around metal nanoparticles can increase the light absorption of solar cells. As a matter of fact, the introduction of plasmonic nanoparticles into photovoltaics applications has led to an emerging research area, usually called plasmon-enhanced solar cells. By employing plasmonic effects such as strong scattering, generation of hot electrons, and amplified near fields in photovoltaic devices, light-harvesting in the visible and NIR regions can be enhanced.143–145 Previously, Schaadt et al. deposited Au nanoparticles on Si-based solar cells and obtained an enhanced response of up to 80% at wavelengths around 500 nm.146 Pillai et al. deposited Ag particles on 1.25 μm thick and planar Si-based solar cells and obtained an increased photocurrent by 33% and 19%, respectively.147 Enhancements from plasmons have also been observed in other semiconductor solar cells.148–152 Currently, the reported metal nanoparticles in PSCs are generally utilized by being incorporated into the solution of the interfacial carrier modification layer or photoactive layer to increase light-harvesting.87,90,153,154 From another point of view, due to the toxicity of Pb in perovskite materials, researchers believe that the thickness of the perovskite layer should be further reduced on the existing basis to decrease the usage and pollution of Pb. This implies that light-harvesting by metal nanoparticles may be crucial for perovskite films to generate the required photocurrent.
In addition to generating highly efficient electricity, PSCs have a unique use as a translucent window for buildings. To improve the transparency of PSCs, most of the approaches are focused on developing transparent electrodes, such as ultrathin Au electrodes, Ag nanowires, transparent polymers, and transparent metal oxides, and the perovskite layer itself. According to existing studies, the thickness of the perovskite photoactive layer required must be at least 370 nm to completely capture light with photonic energy efficiently. However, both efficiency and transparency depend on the thickness of the perovskite layer and are contradictory. On the one hand, increasing the thickness of the perovskite layer will reduce the transparency of PSCs. On the other hand, reducing the thickness of the perovskite layer to increase transparency leads to decreased efficiency, as previously demonstrated in the manufacture of ultra-thin PSCs. To overcome this dilemma, a promising texturing method, which can simultaneously enhance light absorption and transmission beyond the wavelength range of the perovskite photoactive layer, has proved to be a feasible solution for enhancing the light-trapping capability of PSCs.155,156 In an ergodic optical medium, the light intensity can be up to n2 times the incident light intensity, with n being the local index of refraction or two times that if a diffusively reflective surface is placed behind.157 Through the optimization of the structure, the PCE of the device can be maintained as far as possible while ensuring the transmission. Xiao's group simulated two different sets of textures of cylindrical nanopillars and an array of cylindrical nanoholes by FDTD for MAPbI3 based PSCs.158 They found that an optimized textured structure could induce the PCE enhancement of 30.48%, indicating that the light trapping structure is essential in thin-film PSCs.
Similar to traditional thin-film solar cells, FTO/glass or PEN/ITO must be used as a transparent conductive substrate in the device structure of PSCs, which makes the incident light must pass through the transparent conductive substrate before reaching the perovskite photoactive layer.159 Fresnel reflection at the air–substrate interface of a solar cell inevitably occurs, causing an optical loss in photovoltaic performance.160–162 According to classical electromagnetic theory, reflection is born out of a transition in the medium in which light is traveling.163 Generally, the reflectivity determined by the difference of the diffraction index between the conductive substrate and air and incident angle is inevitable, especially for long-wavelength light.114,164 Trapping the incident light in the perovskite layers by fabricating an anti-reflection layer has been an effective route to reduce the reflection loss. For a solar cell fabricated on a planar glass substrate, 4% of the incident light at normal incidence will be reflected at the air–glass interface. This value can go up to 30% as all incident angles are taken into consideration.163 To reduce the reflection, it is well known that introducing an interface modification layer or anti-reflection layer at the substrate/air interface or inside the device structure can reduce reflection losses. Directly, attaching a haze layer in front of the PSCs could be a straightforward and practical approach to reduce the reflection loss and improve the light absorption over a broad optical spectrum without affecting the device fabrication process.
Solar concentration technology represents a practical approach to reduce the cost of photovoltaic by decreasing their active area used for generating the same amount of electrical power.165,166 A luminescent solar concentrator (LSC), which is one of the most striking photonic devices that relies on the so-called Stokes shift engineering launcher, has been proposed as a useful complement for photovoltaic modules to realize the building of integrated photovoltaic devices, such as translucent solar windows.167,168 The earliest LSC typed configuration appeared as a grant proposal in 1973 by Lerner.169–171 To date, a higher PCE of 14.5% has been achieved using a tandem planar solar concentrator by employing a mixture of dyes combined with CIGS solar cells.166 The LSC has again become the interest of many groups around the world.166 The fluorescence quantum efficiency, a key feature for LSCs, is the ratio of photons emitted from the panel edges to the total number of absorbed photons.172 To date, various types of fluorophores have been used as light converters for LSCs, including organic dyes, quantum dots, and RE doped compounds. Today, the optical quantum efficiency has been exceeded 20% for the device with active areas of 100 cm2 for Stokes shift-engineered CdSe, CdSe/CdTe, CdSe/ZnS, and carbon quantum dots.173 We conclude these performances can be further improved by enhancing the quantum yield of semiconductor nanocrystals. Perovskite quantum dots (CsPbBr3, Cs2PbBr4, Cs2AgBiBr6, etc.) have absolute advantages for LSC application, including an absorption band that spans a large portion of the solar spectrum, high quantum yields higher 90%, large Stokes shift, strong luminescence matching well with the attached solar cells and a refractive index of 2.5, indicating the possibility of more efficient confinement for the LSC application.167,174 Here, we will focus on the influence of perovskite nanocrystals as an absorption light source on LSC, and we also look forward to their future application potential combined with efficient PSCs.
Distinct from other published reviews, we here systematically and hierarchically presented all of the photon management strategies to reduce energy loss in PSCs. This paper is organized as follows: in Section 2, a brief overview of the fundamental aspects of UC and its application in every structural part of PSCs is presented. In Section 3, we discussed the theoretical framework and types of DC luminescent materials for PSCs. In Section 4, the tandem or graded PSCs are discussed. In Section 5, a brief overview of the mechanisms of SPR and its function in PSCs is presented. In Section 6, we introduce the texturing method in the internal structure of PSCs for light-trapping. In Section 7, we overview the anti-reflection for photon recycling by reducing the surface reflection loss in PSCs. In Section 8, we discuss the LSC of perovskite nanocrystals for enhancing the photovoltaic performance of traditional Si-based solar cells and PSCs. In every section, the mechanism, device structure, high-resolution characterization, theoretical simulation, preparation method, manufacturing technique, and efficient photovoltaic applications are discussed, as is organized in Fig. 2. The photoelectric characteristics and the enhanced factor are summarized in the corresponding tables. Finally, this review ends with a summary of the perspectives and challenges related to the use of different functions in PSCs to harness a broader range of the solar spectrum, attaining higher efficiencies.67 This work guides experimentalists and theorists toward and demonstrates an approach to reduce the energy loss of perovskite-based devices, including photovoltaics, photodetector, and photoelectrochemical cells. This review will also guide research in luminescence and related biomedical imaging fields.
 Fig. 2  The overall distribution of this review, including UC, DC, tandem, SPR, texturing, anti-reflection, and LSC effect. Specifically, we will summarize the photon management strategies from the perspective of working mechanism, device structure, high-resolution characterization, theoretical simulation, preparation method, manufacturing technique, and their future application. 2. Upconversion for NIR light-harvesting
As we all know, efficient PSCs should respond in a broad spectral range, for example, from the visible light region to the NIR region (∼190–2500 nm), and effectively convert the incident light into carriers.180,181 Although the PSCs can absorb the visible light part of sunlight effectively, there exists a significant energy loss for the sub-bandgap photon transmission in the NIR spectral parts of sunlight.182,183 With nearly 40% of the energy wasted owing to sub-bandgap losses in solar cells, utilizing the NIR part of sunlight for photovoltaic improvement is tremendous.184 A practical approach to decrease the sub-bandgap loss in PSCs is applying the UC materials that absorb NIR light and upconvert it to higher visible energy photons via a non-linear optical process.99,185–187 UC is anti-Stokes radiation by a two-step excitation process.188 Individually, the material is lifted from the ground state to the high-energy state by absorbing two NIR photons through the intermediate state. This is followed by the emission of high excited energy states. Generally, UC materials consist of a host material (e.g., NaYF4 and NaGdF4) and RE dopants (typically, Yb3+/Ho3+, Yb3+/Er3+, or Yb3+/Tm3+ couples).67,189,190 In general, REs refer to the lanthanide elements in the atomic sequence 57 (lanthanum)–71 (lutetium) in the periodic table of elements, as well as the scandium and yttrium of the third subgroup (17 items in total), which possess rich energy levels arising from the 4f configurations.191–195 As for the most typical configuration of NaYF4:Yb3+,Er3+ based UC materials, Yb3+ acts as an NIR sensitizer and Er3+ acts as a visible photon emitter. RE doped UC materials can give rise to various emissions with wavelengths covering the UV, visible, and NIR.196 Given UC application in PSCs, the UC materials are required to satisfy the following demands: (1) excellent spectral matching between the photovoltaic response of PSCs and the emission spectrum of UC materials. Precisely, the excitation range (absorption range) of UC materials needs to cover an as broad as possible NIR range in which the PSCs have no photovoltaic response, whereas the emitted light should be within the optimal photoelectric response range of PSCs (300–800 nm). (2) Effective UC luminescence yield and lower UC threshold. Usually, the power density of the sunlight within 100 nm in the NIR range is less than 10 mW cm−2, and this demands that the UC materials could emit visible light efficiently at lower excitation power. (3) The UC material needs to have good transmittance in the photovoltaic response wavelength range of the solar cell, thereby avoiding the absorption loss of visible light by the UC layer.85 Here, we systematically list the research on UC effect in PSCs, and summarize the relevant photovoltaic characteristics data in the table. To show the superiority of device structure and straighten up the enhancement of UC effect in PSCs, we classify the application of UC materials from every functional structure perspective.
2.1 Upconversion in electron transporting layers
Compared to traditional thin-film photovoltaics, PSCs exhibit strong compatibility in the mesoporous layer part, which can accommodate the introduction of many dielectric nanomaterials, such as Al2O3 and ZrO2, etc.197–199 This allows researchers to place dielectric UC materials inside the device structure, rather than the outside structures of the solar cell such as Si and GaAs. There are several works that reported the incorporation of the UC layer in the mesoporous or scaffold layer of PSCs.190,200–203 Early on, He et al. applied monodisperse NaYF4:Yb3+,Er3+ nanoparticles as the mesoporous layer for PSCs applications.200 The UC layers can convert the NIR into 408, 523, 542, and 655 nm emissions, which are associated with the 2H9/2–4I15/2, 2H11/2–4I15/2, 4S3/2–4I15/2, and 4F9/2–4I15/2 transitions of Er3+ (Fig. 3a). From the device structure in Fig. 3b, it can be seen the converted light was effective utilization by perovskite photoactive layer, leading to an enhanced PCE from 16.8% to 17.8%. Further characterization showed that a stable JSC could be increased from 19.5 mA cm−2 under standard sunlight to 19.9 mA cm−2 with an additional 980 nm laser, demonstrating the positive role of the UC in enhancing the PCE of PSCs. Similarly, Jongmin Roh et al. also found that the hexagonal β-NaYF4:Yb3+,Er3+ nanoprism modified PSCs in the mesoporous layer could exhibit an overall enhancement of 13.74% compared to bare TiO2-based PSCs.190 By examining the J–V characteristics (JSC = 0.027 mA cm−2, VOC = 0.374 V) under 980 nm laser irradiation, they attributed the enhanced PCE to the active UC luminescence properties of the NaYF4:Yb3+,Er3+ nanoprisms. Que et al. synthesized and applied β-NaYF4:Yb3+,Tm3+/NaYF4 core–shell them into the electron transporting layers (ETLs) of PSCs to enhance the photocurrent densities and PCE, which is attributed to the UC effect of the UC nanoparticles by the FDTD simulation results.85 Recently, Guo et al. synthesized β-NaYF4:Yb3+,Er3+,Sc3+@NaYF4 nanoparticles with additional Sc3+ to enhance the luminescence efficiency,202 leading to the efficient PSCs with PCE of 20.19%, higher than that of 17.44% for control devices with a single TiO2 mesoporous layer. Li et al. revealed that incorporating the NaYbF4:Ho3+ nanoparticles into ZrO2 as the scaffold layer could achieve a PCE of 14.32%, which is increased by 28.8% compared with the bare ZrO2 based PSCs (11.12%).203 The enhanced IPCE response (Fig. 3c) over a broad range of 400–600 nm indicates that incorporating nanoparticles will boost the light-harvesting and lower carrier recombination. Additionally, a small response peaking at approximately 970 nm reveals the UC effects for spectrum widening. It should be noted that the JSC reached 25.16 mA cm−2 at 40% concentration, which is higher than the value of most studies. Although the luminescence yield of UC nanomaterials is limited at the current time, they possess the advantage and possibility of being combined inside with concepts such as photonics and plasmonics for enhancing the device performance of PSCs.106 Liang et al. synthesized the small-sized β-NaYF4:Yb3+,Tm3+@TiO2 core–shell nanoparticles and incorporated them into the TiO2 mesoporous layer.201 The proposed energy transfer from the core–shell nanoparticles to MAPbI3 induces the optimized PCE of 16.27%, higher than 13.98% for the control devices. The above findings mainly concentrate on the devices with only one sensitizer to absorb NIR photons at 980 nm. Wang et al. prepared dual-sensitized β-NaYF4:Nd3+,Yb3+,Er3+ to absorb NIR photons at 980 nm and 808 nm for PSC applications.204 Compared with the control device, the PSCs with expanded absorption range could exhibit a best PCE of 19.23%, which improved by 7.13% compared with the control device. The enhanced PCE was found to be due to UC, light scattering, and effective electron injection by characterizing time-resolved UC fluorescence spectra and optical transmission and reflectance spectra.
 Fig. 3  (a) The UC fluorescence spectrum of NaYF4:Yb3+,Er3+ nanoparticles under a 980 nm laser excitation. The corresponding energy transitions are shown as an inset. Although the RE doped NaYF4 nanoparticles exhibit practical UC effect, previous studies have found that pure RE doped NaYF4 nanoparticles could induce electron trapping in PSCs due to the limitation of carrier transport. Recently, researchers found that SiO2 nanoparticles could exhibit insulating properties as a shell coating on the RE doped NaYF4 nanoparticles to overcome the electron trapping. Hu et al. incorporated a highly uniform core/shell-structured hexagonal β-NaYF4:Yb3+,Er3+@SiO2 into PSCs to achieve enhanced PCE.205 They attributed the PCE enhancement to the scattering centers for increasing light capture and the additional photocurrent generated by UC. Similarly, Zhang et al. applied NaYF4:Yb3+,Tm3+@SiO2 nanoprisms for enhancing the photovoltaic parameters of carbon-based PSCs.65 They concluded that the enhanced JSC is attributed to broadened light response to the NIR region and the improved light-scattering effect, while the higher FFs are related to the reduced carrier recombination at the interface of perovskite/TiO2 through the passivation effect of the SiO2 capping layer. Qi et al. designed a novel bilayer electron-transport scaffold, including SiO2/NaYF4:Yb3+,Er3+@SiO2 modified TiO2 nanorods to improve the performance of PSCs.206 It is concluded that the 1D nanorods structure underneath the TiO2 scaffold can facilitate carrier transportation. As a result, the modified PSCs exhibit the best PCE of 14.52%, higher than that of 14.21% for pure SiO2 modified TiO2 nanorods-based PSCs. Under a 980 nm laser irradiation (300 mW), the SiO2/NaYF4:Yb3+,Er3+@SiO2 modified TiO2 nanorod-based PSCs exhibit a PCE of 0.12%, while that of the SiO2 modified TiO2 nanorod based cell is 0.00097%, indicating the utilization of the NIR light.
In addition to applying NaYF4 matrix-based UC materials, Zhang et al. synthesized Er3+-doped TiO2 nanorod arrays as UC materials for PSC applications with an optimized PCE of 10.6%.207 The Er3+-doped TiO2 nanorods exhibit a broad absorption from 710 to 1200 nm and upconvert it into visible green light. They ascribed the enhanced performance to the UC effect and enhanced electron-injection efficiency. They also prepared Er3+, Yb3+, Li+, and Ho3+, Yb3+, Mg2+ tri-doped TiO2 with an enhanced UC emission for PSCs applications.208,209 Wang et al. proved that TiO2:Yb3+,Er3+ nanorod arrays with the UC luminescence spectra could boost the PCE to 13.4%, which is 20.8% higher than that of control PSCs.210 The above results indicate that incorporating RE doped TiO2 based ETLs could provide a promising route to reducing the energy loss in PSCs.
2.2 Upconversion in hole transporting layers
Although most of the studies have placed UC materials in the ETLs (the mesoporous or scaffold layer), some recent studies have placed them in the hole transporting layers (HTLs). Deng et al. prepared the mono-disperse, single tetragonal-phase Li(Gd,Y)F4:Yb3+,Er3+ nanoparticles (Fig. 3d) with green luminescence (Fig. 3e) and integrated them into the HTLs of PSCs.63 The optimized UC nanocrystals could produce an increased average PCE by over 25%, with a steady JSC of 22.90 mA cm−2. Furthermore, they also observed an increased JSC value of 23.39 mA cm−2 when the 980 nm laser was applied (Fig. 3f), indicating the utilization of low energy loss. In order to quantify the UC contribution, Sebag et al. performed the light-beam-induced current/fluorescence mapping technique to correlate UC emission and the contributed photocurrent, together with the morphology of the devices under the excitation of a NIR laser at 975 nm for Yb3+,Er3+ co-doped KY7F22 nanoparticles based PSCs (Fig. 3g).211 When the laser spot irradiates from the part without nanoparticles to the part with nanoparticles, there was a ∼3-fold increase in photocurrent, which was attributed to the UC contribution. The decreased ratio in the fluorescence spectrum of green/red light is related to the better response of the perovskite photoactive layer to green light (Fig. 3h and i). This work directly proves that the UC fluorescence and perovskite collect the generated fluorescence, highlighting the role of UC in improving the performance of PSC devices.
The core–shell structured UC nanoparticles were also embedded in HTL for device optimization. Liang et al. synthesized core–shell-structured NaLuF4:Yb3+,Er3+@NaLuF4 nanoparticles and integrated them into the HTL for improving PCE in γ-CsPbI3 based PSCs.212 Compared with the control devices, the JSC and PCE of the modified device reached up to 19.17 mA cm−2 and 15.86%, respectively, which is increased by as much as 1.91% and 2.26% than that of the control device. Although NaLuF4:Yb3+,Er3+@NaLuF4 nanoparticles possess UC fluorescence properties, they identified that the limited fluorescence quantum efficiency (<1%) makes it play a minimal role in devices (Fig. 3j). Further characterization proved that the nanoparticles mainly served as scattering centers, which extended the sunlight optical path by combining with scattering and reflecting effects (Fig. 3k). Such research reviews are also recognized by many researchers, who believe that scattering and reflection effects play a more dominant role in photovoltaic devices.213–215
2.3 Upconversion in perovskite photoactive layers
In addition to the configuration in the carrier transport layers, some studies have also placed UC in the perovskite photoactive layer of the PSCs. By overcoming the severe compatibility issue with the help of a ligand-exchange strategy, Meng et al. demonstrated in situ growth of β-NaYF4:Yb3+,Er3+ nanocrystals with bright UC fluorescence and embedded them into the perovskite film for PSC applications (Fig. 3l).216 Specifically, the ligand-exchange processed β-NaYF4:Yb3+,Er3+ nanocrystals could serve as nucleation sites for the crystallization growth of perovskite films. The PCE difference indicates that β-NaYF4:Yb3+,Er3+ based PSCs could achieve a PCE of 19.70%, while the bare β-NaYF4 based devices achieve a PCE of 19.49% (Fig. 3m), indicating the contribution of UC. Under concentrated irradiation of 980 nm laser with a power density of 3 W cm−2, the PSCs also exhibited a PCE of 0.37%, 740-fold enhanced to the pristine PSC (η = 0.0005%).
Despite the enormous potential of nanoparticles for photovoltaic applications, the low efficiency of UC luminescence has severely hampered their practical use in photovoltaic applications. Many strategies have recently been explored to enhance the luminescence intensity of RE-doped nanoparticles (such as host lattice modification, doping concentration optimization, core/shell nanostructure design, and surface plasmon coupling),217 but the quantum yield of the most efficient nanoparticles remains very low (<10%). Recently, the discovery of localized surface plasmon resonance (LSPR) in semiconductor nanoparticles has opened a new regime in plasmonics and could provide a strategy for enhancing the UC luminescence of RE-doped UC nanoparticles.87,193,218 Usually, the collective oscillation behavior of free electrons in nanostructures oscillating with the incident light field is called LSPR (which we will introduce in detail in Section 5). Recent studies revealed that some semiconductor nanocrystals could exhibit strong LSPR absorption. More importantly, the LSPR absorption band of semiconductor nanocrystals is located in the NIR region, which can make full use of the abundant NIR energy. Therefore, the combination of semiconductor and UC nanostructures can effectively increase the fluorescence intensity of UC. Previously, our group firstly demonstrated the distinctive photonic and photoelectric properties of Cu2−xS semiconductor plasmon nanoparticles and their unique interactions for enhancing the UC photoluminescence of NaYF4:Yb3+,Er3+ nanoparticles. With the addition of a MoO3 spacer, the UC luminescence intensity of NaYF4:Yb3+,Er3+ nanoparticles can be improved and exhibited extraordinary power-dependent behavior, as shown in Fig. 4a. Specifically, The UC luminescence enhancement is mainly induced by the scattering of plasmon Cu2−xS nanoparticles at lower excitation power. We proposed a model to explain the mechanism of interaction between Cu2−xS plasmon nanoparticles, NaYF4:Yb3+,Er3+ nanoparticles and enhanced UC luminescence, that is, the plasmon scattering effect at lower excitation power and electron diffusion via a two-photon interband transition of Cu2−xS at higher excitation power (Fig. 4b). Differently, when the 980 nm excitation power is higher than 0.7 W mm−2, the electrons arising from the valence band of Cu2−xS can be excited into the direct conduction band of Cu2−xS through a two-photon process (Fig. 4c). These findings provide insights into the LSPR in semiconductors for applications in photoelectric devices. Then, we demonstrate that the broadband semiconductor plasmon (800–1600 nm) of Cu2−xS could serve as an antenna to sensitize UC of Er2O3 nanoparticles (Fig. 4d and e).193 The overall UC luminescence of the Cu2−xS@SiO2@Er2O3 composites can be enhanced by a factor of ∼1000, with a maximal quantum efficiency of 14.3% under excitation, leading to the expanded NIR response and considerably improved PCE (Fig. 4f). Under AM 1.5G irradiation, the composites in PSCs enhanced the photocurrent via electron transfer from oxygen defects to the conduction band of TiO2. The PCE of the best-performing solar cell reached 18.8% at a concentration of 15 suns (Fig. 4g), which is consistent with the high quantum efficiency and effective electronic transition. This result indicates that the electron transfer and re-absorption of UC luminescence both contributed to the enhancement of PCE under irradiation of 15 suns. Recently, Xu et al. found that the LSPR could help the UC nanoparticles (NaCsWO3@NaYF4@NaYF4:Yb3+,Er3+) exhibit an enhanced UCL intensity by more than 124 times for widening the spectral response range and then improving the photocurrent of PSCs.219 More importantly, the combination of semiconductor and UC nanostructures gives researchers an excellent reference to improve the NIR response of PSCs by increasing the UCL intensity with the LSPR effect in the traditional UC materials to reduce the energy loss.
 Fig. 4  (a) Comparison of the enhanced factors of UC fluorescence. (b) Schematic illustration for the UC fluorescence enhancement at high excitation power and low excitation power in the Cu2−xS–MoO3–NaYF4:Yb3+,Er3+ composites. (c) Schematic illustration of the electron injection process for Cu2−xS–MoO3–NaYF4:Yb3+,Er3+. Reprinted with permission from ref. 89, copyright 2016, American Chemistry Society; (d) fabrication process of the nanocomposites and their application in PSCs. (e) Schematic of the mechanism of UC broadband emission in the nanocomposites. (f) IPCE curves of the best-performing PSC. (g) J–V curves of the best-performing PSC with different light intensities. Reprinted with permission from ref. 193, copyright 2017, Royal Society of Chemistry; (h) broadband NIR sunlight-harvesting for PSCs, (i) the absorption spectra of the perovskite, IR806, and UC nanoparticles, and the emission spectrum of IR806. (j) PL spectra of UC nanoparticles in DMF excited by a laser, inset is the photographs of the UC nanoparticles before and after ligand exchange treatment. Reprinted with permission from ref. 223, copyright 2017, Elsevier; (j) AM 1.5G spectrum shows the fraction absorbed by PSCs and the spectral regions that can be utilized through the UC process. (k) Schematic of UC populating for the UC nanoparticles/IR-783 dye/Au nanorods composite film at simulated sunlight excitation. Reprinted with permission from ref. 224, copyright 2020, American Chemistry Society. 
Despite, confined by the lower quantum yield and smaller absorption cross-section of the traditional RE doped UC nanoparticles, its application is still a significant challenge. To further increase the fluorescence intensity and fluorescence efficiency, a novel class of UC nanomaterials with broadband, low-power NIR excitation, and high overall luminescence quantum yield were developed by combining an organic NIR dye as a sensitizer and traditional RE doped NaYF4 nanoparticles, which makes them highly promising as a spectral converter for applications in solar cells.196 The dye-sensitization on UC nanomaterials can effectively enhance UC fluorescence intensity and efficiency.220 For instance, Zou et al. developed a strategy to enhance the UC luminescence of NaYF4:Yb,Er nanoparticles, in which IR-806 dye molecules acted as an antenna to absorb NIR photons at a broad wavelength range, and then transferred the energy to Yb3+.182,221,222 Lai et al. reported incorporating IR806 dye-sensitized UC nanocrystals modified PSCs could exhibit a PCE of 17.49%, higher than that of 13.52% for the control device (Fig. 4h).223 Three characteristic Er3+ emission bands at 510–530 nm, 530–570 nm, and 630–680 nm, corresponding to 2H11/2 → 4I15/2, the 4S3/2 → 4I15/2 and the 4F9/2 → 4I15/2 transitions are observed in the PL spectrum of IR806-UC nanoparticles (Fig. 4i). This strategy provides a feasible way to enable the most efficient harvesting of NIR sunlight for PSCs. Until now, there is no literature on improving the performance of PSCs by using dye and metal-enhanced UC nanoparticles. Very recently, our group enhanced the UC luminescence by synthesizing the IR-783 dye-sensitized core/shell NaYF4:Yb3+,Er3+@NaYF4:Yb3+,Nd3+ UC nanoparticles.224Fig. 4j reveals the schematic diagram of a solar spectrum conversion process by a UC nanoparticles/IR-783 dye/Au nanorods composite film, and the energy-level diagram and the UC processes under the excitation of around 808 nm are shown in Fig. 4k. The luminescence intensity was increased by 120 times under the sensitization of dye molecules and the LSPR effect of Au nanorods. As a result, the rationally designed UC nanoparticles were assembled on the SnO2 layer of the PSCs, resulting in a high PCE of 20.5%. As far as we know, this is the highest PCE for the PSCs based on UC. Under the 1 W cm−2 irradiation, the PCE can be further enhanced to 21.1%. This work provides an effective method for increasing the fluorescence intensity and luminescent quantum yield utilized in the PSCs and is of considerable significance to the future concentrating PSCs. The results show that the enhanced PCE is due to the combination of the NIR spectrum and the enhanced light collection induced by the scattering effect of the rationally designed UC nanoparticles. We conclude that this previous work could provide a strategy for enhancing the luminescent quantum yield of UC nanoparticles and further applied in the PSCs, which is of great significance for future PSCs that use sunlight concentrators.
Very recently, the RE ions doped semiconductor nanocrystals (CsPbF3:Zn2+,Yb3+,Tm3+) have been proved to absorb two or more NIR photons and convert them into a high-frequency emission photon via multi-step energy transfer, which surpasses the bandgap energy of the semiconductor, thus inducing photoelectric response.220,225–227 Our group demonstrated a novel class of sensitive CsPbF3:Zn2+,Yb3+,Tm3+ based perovskite UC nanocrystal, which could exhibit excellent responsivity at 980 nm NIR region and external quantum efficiency (EQE) of 135%.228 For future research, to effectively avoid the influence of dielectric materials on the internal charge transfer of perovskites and achieve better lattice matching characteristics with a perovskite photoactive layer in PSCs, we believe that RE doped semiconductor-based UC materials, especially for the RE doped perovskite UC nanocrystals (CsPbBr3, CsPbCl3, etc.), have great potential in achieving effective UC and reducing light loss.
There are also some problems that need to be clarified for researchers. Although the above result demonstrates that the RE based UC materials applied inside PSCs could provide additional photocurrent and a potential application in the field of NIR light-sensitive response photodetectors for expanding the effective absorption spectrum, almost none of these works mentions or avoids mentioning the fluorescence quantum efficiency of UC nanomaterials. In other words, the fluorescence quantum efficiency of UC nanomaterials at this stage is very low, and it also needs very high energy to excite, such as a high-power 980 nm laser. Therefore, the photovoltaic performance improvement (PCE, JSC, FF, and VOC) of PSCs is primarily attributed to enhanced scattering, improved carrier transport, and suppressed carrier recombination.
2.4 Upconversion out of PSCs
Although the fluorescence intensity and efficiency of UC materials can be enhanced by the plasmonic effect of semiconductors and the compound effect of dyes, integrating RE doped NaYF4 nanoparticles inside of the PSC device still faces a challenge of carrier recombination effects because of the dielectric properties. The upconverting nanoparticles usually act as severe recombination centers, leading to photocurrent loss due to the direct contact between the UC nanoparticles and the perovskite. In the process of introducing the UC materials into the device structure, special attention to the size, thickness, and uniformity of UC material must be paid for researchers. More important, high internal UC quantum yield values at low irradiance suitable for photovoltaics have been reported only for monocrystalline and multi-crystalline materials.229 According to a model from earlier research from Si and CIGS-based solar cells, the UC layer is usually placed at the rear side of a solar cell to capture the transmitted photons to enhance photovoltaic response independently.230,231 This has relatively large requirements for photovoltaic devices. The significant advantage of the rear side strategy is that the UC layer has no influence on the existing device structure. Researchers need to optimize the fluorescence characteristics of the UC material itself and the photoelectric efficiency of the PSCs independently.
Based on the above independent development strategy, our group firstly demonstrated the application of the transparent LiYF4:2% Yb3+,1% Er3+ single crystal with an internal quantum efficiency of 5.72% as an independent luminescent upconverter in front of PSCs (Fig. 5a).232 As is illustrated in Fig. 5b, we can see that the PCE can be enhanced by 7.9% under the irradiation of simulated sunlight by 7 suns, illustrating the positive effect of UC for PSCs under multiple solar light constants. The power-dependent enhancement efficiencies of the PSCs with LiYF4:2% Yb3+,1% Er3+ crystals reveal that the UC effect can play a very positive role under high power light. This work was the first to use UC single crystals in the research of PSCs, and to directly improve the performance of the device under the excitation of sunlight rather than laser light. Also, on the front of the PSCs, Tang et al. utilized a Yb3+ and Er3+ co-doped TACN based fluorotellurite glass as a UC converter.233 Under 980 nm irradiation with a power density of 10 W cm−2, the PSC could achieve a PCE of 0.18% (Fig. 5c). The above results show that applying UC on the front side of PSCs has an essential effect on improving the utilization of the solar spectrum.
 Fig. 5  (a) Schematic of the J–V curves of the PSCs under simulated sunlight excitation. (b) Enhanced PCE of the PSCs and Si-based solar cells with LiYF4:2% Yb3+,1% Er3+ crystals dependent on the excitation power density. Reprinted with permission from ref. 232, copyright 2016, American Chemistry Society; (c) the J–V curves of MAPbI3−xClx-based PSCs using TACN-1.0 glass under 980 nm laser irradiation with different power supply. Reprinted with permission from ref. 233, copyright 2018, American Chemistry Society; (d) cross-sectional SEM image of the complete UC modified PSCs. (e) J–V curves of four optimized cells with and without the UC layer under 1.5 W cm−2. The inset figure shows the emission images of the corresponding UC layer. (f) PCEs are changing as a function of power density. (g) AM 1.5G spectrum shows the fraction response by PSCs through the UC and DC process. Reprinted with permission from ref. 234, copyright 2018, Elsevier. 
In contrast to the UC front side strategies, a UC material layer is preferentially placed on the backside of a PSC, where it takes up the sub-bandgap part of the solar spectrum and re-emits visible light, which now is resonant to the bandgap of the PSCs. Based on a semi-transparent electrode, we develop a NaYF4:Yb3+,Er3+/NaYF4:Yb3+,Tm3+/Ag composite based UC layer (Fig. 5d) on the backside of the PSCs.234 This design enables multi-functional effects, including harvesting NIR light, plasmonic scattering, reflection, and luminescent enhancement. For testing the spectrum widening effect on device performance, we introduced the NaYF4:Yb3+,Er3+, NaYF4:Yb3+,Tm3+, and NaYF4:Yb3+,Er3+/NaYF4:Yb3+,Tm3+ composite layer, respectively, to modify PSCs under 1.5 W cm−2 illumination. The J–V results reveal that the relative gain of PCE is 6.8%, 4.3%, and 11.8%, indicating that the NaYF4:Yb3+,Er3+/NaYF4:Yb3+,Tm3+ composite layer could exhibit the best UC effects for NIR utilization (Fig. 5e). Further depositing an Ag LSPR layer, the NaYF4:Yb3+,Er3+/NaYF4:Yb3+,Tm3+ composite layer-based device exhibits an optimized PCE of 19.2% (Fig. 5f), increased by about 20% under 1.5 W cm−2 illumination, suggesting the great potential for application in the spectral broadening of PSCs (Fig. 5g). Very recently, Kinoshita et al. obtained a photocurrent under excitation at 938 nm for PSCs by attaching the UC layer behind with sensitizing a rubrene triplet with an osmium complex donor having singlet-to-triplet absorption in the NIR range.235 Above all, the UC layer covered the backside of PSCs could absorb the transmitted photons with low-energy and re-emitted visible light with high energy, which can contribute to the formation of extra electron–hole pairs in PSCs with higher photocurrent.
In the above, although PSCs have a higher tolerance in a device structure than traditional organic and DSSCs, the thickness of the entire device needs to be controlled within 1 μm, which places high requirements on the size and thickness of the UC material. In the last part of this section, the introduction of UC single-crystals outside of the PSCs can effectively avoid carrier recombination and thus enhance the electrical performance. Simultaneously, a UC single crystal with high efficiency and high fluorescence intensity can be used to place on the outside of the PSCs, and the performance of the device can be further enhanced through optimization of the structure, such as the LSPR and photonic crystal structure for UC luminescence enhancement.87,94 It should also be noted that the light transmission of the UC single-crystal for visible light also needs to be considered when placed outside the device.
In the section summary, from the current point of view, the UC mechanism is the only way to break through the limited efficiency of the Shockley–Queisser limit of a perovskite-based single-junction solar cell. Most of the UC strategies to improve the spectral response of PSCs have been positively promoted. Of course, this improvement in photovoltaic performance may be the synergistic result of multiple strategies, such as UC, scattering, and promoted crystallization of perovskite. Nevertheless, under the excitation of a 980 nm laser or multiple sunlight, it is unquestionable whether the UC-based PSCs could exhibit an extra eye-catching photocurrent, which is not possible with traditional PSCs. At present, most of the research is focused on RE doped UC materials, especially RE doped NaYF4 and LiYF4 materials, which are mainly concentrated in some trivalent REs with matching intermediate energy levels, such as Er3+, Tm3+, Ho3+, etc. (by using Yb3+ as a sensitizer). Although REs can provide efficient UC coupling, the massive gains in PCE could not be achieved as theoretically predicted, and the practical application of UC materials in solar cells still faces enormous challenges. The main reason is that the f–f absorption band of REs is relatively narrow, which is the key to UC luminescence to achieve broadband UC absorption/excitation, leading to the luminous efficiency and absorption cross-section of the existing UC materials. The trivalent REs have the common issue of small absorption cross-section and narrow excitation band. Simultaneously, the REs that can produce UC luminescence often have richer and denser intermediate state energy levels, which enhances the coupling between the excited state electrons and the lattice, that is, the electron–phonon coupling, making its UC fluorescence efficiency less than ideal. Currently recognized as the most efficient UC luminescent material RE doped NaYF4 system, their luminescence quantum efficiency is still less than 10%, while the nanomaterials have a lower quantum yield. Thus, realizing UC absorption in a broadband is of decisive significance for the UC of the whole solar spectrum in the future (Table 1).
