3. Downconversion/shifting for UV utilization
3.1 Theoretical study of downconversion improved solar cells
Extending the spectral response in the UV region and enhancing the UV-light stability are two remaining challenges for PSCs.62,237–239 The conversion process from high energy photon to low energy photon usually includes two different ways: DC and downshifting.101 DC is the process of converting high-energy photons into two low-energy photons, which is also called quantum clipping.240,241 There is a similar mechanism called downshifting, which is the typical photoluminescence process by converting one high-energy photon into another photon with lower energy. Downshifting can be regarded as a subsection of DC. Although the two processes are similar, their quantum efficiency is different. In the downshifting process, quantum efficiency will undoubtedly be less than 100%.242 Due to the slight thermal loss of DC, its quantum efficiency is equal to or greater than 100%, so it has better potential to improve the performance of solar cells than downshifting. Fig. 6a shows a schematic diagram of downshifting and DC processing. Once the photons are continuously emitted and absorbed, two photons with lower energy can be obtained in DC processing.243 If the wavelength is equal to the wavelength associated with the bandgap of the perovskite, the DC material can be used to improve the efficiency of PSCs. A similar process can occur in downshifting, but as mentioned before, only one photon can be produced for each absorbed photon. Hovel et al. first explored the method to improve the performance of solar cells using the DC layer.244 Since then, theoretical and experimental studies have been carried out on DC materials.105,107,245,246 The traditional materials for the DC layer in solar cells are luminescent organic dyes, quantum dots, organic metal complexes, and so on.171 The efficiency of different solar cells has been improved, such as crystalline Si,243,247,248 CdTe,249,250 CIGS solar cells,251 organic solar cells,252 and DSSCs.67,253 While DC phosphors have been investigated for decades in the lighting industry, the possibility of DC converted sunlight to improve the performance of solar cells was first treated theoretically by Trupke et al.104 They demonstrated that a Si-based solar cell with an ideal DC layer could exhibit an optimized PCE of 38.6%, which is in comparison to the limiting efficiency of 30.9% for the same solar cell without a DC layer present.104 According to studies on the concept of the DC effect from Rothemund, the DC-induced PCE enhancement in solar cells depends on several parameters, including the refractive indices and extinction coefficients of the materials, the film thickness, the particle volume, and size distribution (Fig. 6b).108 Benjamin Lipovšek illustrated that a simulated JSC of 10.62 mA cm−2 and a boost of 6.3% could be obtained by optimizing the DC layer in organic solar cells.109,110 Introducing DC materials can change the absorption curve (Fig. 6c), which reveals the obvious regions of absorption and emission of the DC materials. Fig. 6d shows the DC effect of the violet dye for λ < 400 nm, and of the yellow dye for λ > 400 nm for CIGS application. The yellow dye absorbs nearly all the emission of the violet dye and efficiently reemits down-shifted photons centered around 540 nm. Under AM 1.5G illumination, it can be seen that the introduction of fluorescent dyes can effectively improve the spectral EQE response of photovoltaic devices in the UV region (Fig. 6e).
 Fig. 6  (a) Energy diagram shows different behaviors schematically among a DC and a downshifting material used to harvest the photo in a solar cell. (b) Optical processes and losses for a downshifting layer placed on top of a solar cell. Absorbed and downshifted photons hit the underlying solar cell directly via total reflection or re-absorption and re-emission. Non-absorbed photons are transmitted without a change of wavelength into the solar cell. Optical losses are due to reflection at the front side and interfaces. Due to isotropic emission, individual fractions of down-shifted photons escape through the front side escape cone and the side of the downshifting layer. Reprinted with permission from ref. 108, copyright 2014, Elsevier; (c) simulated absorbance in the typical organic solar cell without (dashed line) and with (full line) the downshifting layer applied to the front side of the cell. Reprinted with permission from ref. 110, copyright 2015, Elsevier; (d) downshifting for CIGS-based solar cells with a mixture of two organic dyes: (d) the violet organic dye absorbs strongly in the low-wavelength region, the yellow organic dye between 400 nm and 500 nm. (e) Fitting the EQE spectrum with a constant LDS efficiency results in small errors because of a change of ηPL for the short-λ two-step absorption and re-emission process. A wavelength-depended LDS efficiency perfectly fits the experimental data. Reprinted with permission from ref. 108, copyright 2014, Elsevier; (f) schematic of the solar cell structures considered in the simulations DC enhanced UV utilization. (g) and (h) Stand for the results for the structure of the solar cell with nBackground of 1.5. Panels (g) and (h) are the absorption profiles calculated for the PSCs with thicknesses of 250 nm and 500 nm, respectively. Reprinted with permission from ref. 113, copyright 2018, Run. 
Integrating DC phosphors into PSCs can be a feasible approach because it could remove a load of spectral matching from the halide perovskite semiconductor and minimizes the thermalization losses in the devices. In order to obtain a more efficient DC effect, the researchers need to carefully design and synthesize the efficient DC materials for PSCs application. For example, the DC material must have high emission efficiency, and its light absorption band should not overlap with the absorption of the active layer in the device, so a light-emitting material whose central absorption peak is in the UV region of the solar spectrum can be selected. In addition to the expansion of the spectrum, DC materials can also effectively reduce the performance degradation of PSCs caused by high-energy UV light. Whilst the same role could be fulfilled by a UV filter, the DC layer does not block the incident UV light but ‘recycles’ it to be used for photocurrent generation in PSCs. Miguel Alexandre et al. focuses on the calculation of the photocurrent gains resulting from the DC effect in PSCs from a theoretical point of view, as well as the reduced effects of UV-induced stability on the devices. The planar structure of PSCs is exhibited in Fig. 6f, in which the DC encapsulant layer was modeled as the front of the incident light layer. In the simulation process, they chose a Gaussian profile to emulate the absorption profiles and then multiplied with the solar photon flux, based on the ASTM G-173 global irradiance spectra, to calculate the hypothetical absorption flux in a thick DC layer. Fig. 6g and h reveal the absorption profiles calculated for the PSCs with perovskite thickness of 250 and 500 nm, respectively. Their results revealed that the optimized DC layer could achieve a marginal increase by 2% (∼0.6 mA cm−2) in photocurrent.113
It has been proved that RE-doped phosphors were able to take up one high-energy incident photon and convert it into two or more low-energy photons. RE-doped phosphors, organic dyes, complexes, and quantum dots have all been suggested to be utilized for achieving the DC effect for PSCs.98,100,254 There are some key characteristics for DC materials, including broadband absorption, high absorption coefficient, and quantum efficiency, high transmittance in the response region, large Stokes shift to minimize self-absorption losses, and excellent stability.101,196 Nanomaterials and bulk materials, as the source of DC fluorescence, have pervasive research work to improve the PCE of PSCs. At the current time, nanomaterials can show excellent fluorescence characteristics, even more than 100% quantum cutting efficiency.241,255–259 At the same time, there are many kinds of DC materials, including RE doped materials, semiconductor quantum dot materials, silicon nanocrystals, as well as carbon-based materials and metal complexes, which can show excellent fluorescence characteristics.260–262 Therefore, we mainly classify and summarize the DC effect on PSCs according to the types of DC materials, which is convenient for readers to research progress and prospects of DC materials for PSCs.
3.2 REs-based downconversion phosphors
Due to their specific 4f electronic structure, the RE doped DC phosphors are ideal candidates for extending the response wavelength of PSCs to the UV region.263 Hence, applying the RE based DC effect to PSCs is a practical approach to improve the photovoltaic performance of PSCs.264 Some previous research using RE luminescence has been proposed to improve the photovoltaic performance of PSCs, including Y2O3, NaGdF4, Sr2CeO4, YVO4:Eu3+, ZnGa2O4:Eu3+, or CeO2:Eu3+, etc.265–267 Jia et al. deposited NaYF4:Eu3+ on the nonconducting side of the conducting glass to extend the spectral response range for PSCs and alleviate the photocatalytic activity of TiO2.265 NaYF4:Eu3+ absorbed the UV light and exhibited emission peaks located at 595 nm, 614 nm, 650 nm, and 700 nm, assigned to the typical 5D0–7F1, 5D0–7F2, 5D0–7F3, 5D0–7F4 transition of Eu3+, respectively (Fig. 7a). As a result, the NaYF4:Eu3+ modified PSCs exhibited a PCE of 19.89% and a JSC of 23.06 mA cm−2, higher than that of 16.99% and 20.65 mA cm−2 for the control device (Fig. 7b). N. Chander et al. found that YVO4:Eu3+ modified PSCs could exhibit improved light stability and provide an 8.5% enhancement in JSC due to the DC effect.266 Chen et al. found that the PSC device with the CeO2:Eu3+–TiO2 composite electrode could exhibit a PCE of 10.8%, higher than that of 10.1% for bare TiO2 based PSCs and 8.0% for undoped CeO2 based PSCs.267
 Fig. 7  (a) Photoluminescence emission spectra of the NaYF4:Eu3+ powder excited at 375 nm. (b) The IPCE spectra of l PSCs with or without NaYF4:Eu3+. Reprinted with permission from ref. 265, copyright 2019, Royal Society of Chemistry; (c) the device structure of the doped YVO4 based PSCs. (d) The excitation and emission spectrum of YVO4:Eu3+ and YVO4:Eu3+,Bi3+ (λem = 343 nm, λex = 621 nm). Reprinted with permission from ref. 268, copyright 2018, American Chemistry Society; (e) schematic configuration of SrAl2O4:Eu2+,Dy3+ based PSCs. (f) Representative J–V curves of PSCs with and without SrAl2O4:Eu2+,Dy3+ under darkness, and AM 1.5G illumination. (g) The IPCE curves of PSCs with and without SrAl2O4:Eu2+,Dy3+. The inset shows the excitation and emission spectra of the SAED film. (h) UV stability for control, inside and outside of the SrAl2O4:Eu2+,Dy3+ based PSCs under 365 nm UV-light irradiation. (i) Optical energy storage effect for the devices with and without SrAl2O4:Eu2+,Dy3+ after simulated light was turned off. Reprinted with permission from ref. 62, copyright 2017, Wiley-VCH. 
It is efficient to utilize UV light compared to the bare TiO2 based PSC. Our group proposed a similar way to optimize the mesoporous TiO2 (m-TiO2) with DC materials.268 We incorporated the YVO4:Eu3+,Bi3+ with intensive luminescence into the m-TiO2 as a luminescent DC layer in PSCs (Fig. 7c). Fig. 7d presents the excitation and emission spectrum of YVO4:Eu3+,Bi3+ nanoparticles. It can be seen that the modified PSCs could exhibit an optimized PCE of 17.9%, which shows an improvement of 9.8% compared with the control device. This work also reveals that the YVO4:Eu3+,Bi3+ modified PSCs could exhibit enhanced UV stability, maintaining 70% initial efficiency after 60 h of UV light irradiation. Rahman et al. found that the Eu3+ based DC nanomaterial, Sr2CeO4:Eu3+, could achieve a photocurrent of 23.70 mA cm−2 and a PCE of 18.95% due to the harvesting and re-emitting of UV-light in the visible range through the DC effect.269 They also reported that the application of Sr2CeO4:Sm3+ modified PSCs could exhibit an enhancement of 11.4% in photocurrent and 16.2% in PCE.270 Moreover, the Sr2CeO4:Eu3+ and Sr2CeO4:Sm3+ based PSCs could exhibit enhanced UV stability. Kim et al. introduced Au@Y2O3:Eu3+ nanoparticles with a quantum yield above 80% to enhance the PCE of PSCs.271 The Au layer on the Y2O3:Eu3+ nanoparticle could amplify the converted visible light by up to 170%, resulting in an enhanced PCE of 16.1%, as compared to the value of 15.2% for control PSCs. Hun et al. found a mixing Y3Al5O12:Ce3+ phosphor layer with TiO2 could help to achieve higher PCE than of PSCs based pure TiO2 nanoparticles due to the effective utilization of light via the DC effect made by the YAG:Ce3+ nanoparticles.272 Hou et al. synthesized and incorporated a ZnGa2O4:Eu3+ nanophosphor m-TiO2 layer of PSCs.273 They obtained the highest PCE of 14.34% with a JSC of 25.02 mA cm−2, much higher than that of 10.67% and JSC of 20.2 mA cm−2 for control PSCs. Tawalare et al. found that ZnB2O4:Mn2+ and Ba9Sc2Si6O24:Eu2+ phosphors by the solid-state reaction can protect PSC from UV radiations and thus decrease the degradation.274 These results revealed that RE doped phosphors as efficient DC materials not only improve the effective utilization of UV light but also enhance the light stability of PSCs.
Although much research work at this stage has proved the practical DC effect of RE-based nanomaterials, the efficient DC materials with uniform and dispersed nanocrystals are difficultly synthesized because of the few appropriate precursor complexes, the high crystalline temperatures, and the agglomeration of RE nanocrystals.275 Meanwhile, there are many organic functional groups on the surface of the nanoparticles prepared by the oil phase method, causing severe recombination of carriers at the interface. There is an urgent need to develop a method capable of large-scale and uniform preparation of DC nanomaterials. Recently, our group developed the pulsed laser deposition approach to preparing the SrAl2O4:Eu2+,Dy3+ photon DC layer for PSC applications (Fig. 7e).62 We found that the SrAl2O4:Eu2+,Dy3+ modification could depress the light-induced deep trap states in the perovskite photoactive layer, achieving an optimized PCE of 17.8% (Fig. 7f and g). The devices with SAED exhibited significantly improved UV light stability by the remaining 92% of the initial PCE values after UV irradiation (Fig. 7h). More importantly, we additionally obtained a solar energy storage effect (Fig. 7i) after light illumination is turned off, due to the long-persistent luminescence of SrAl2O4:Eu2+,Dy3+.276,277 Very recently, we also introduced another long-lasting phosphor layer, Y2O2S:Eu3+,Ti4+,Mg2+, to achieve DC effects and energy-storage function.278 The Y2O2S:Eu3+,Ti4+,Mg2+ modified devices could exhibit a considerably improved PCE, suppressed hysteretic effects, and highly prolonged light stability. Above, introducing DC material with long-lasting effect in PSCs not only reduces energy loss in UV and light-induced instability by photoconversion from UV to visible light but also represents a new strategy for solar energy storage. Based on the same matrix of Sr4Al14O25, Cui et al. found that the Sr4Al14O25:Mn4+,Mg2+ modified PSCs could exhibit a 7.7% increase in PCE as compared with that of the control devices.279 Above all, the phosphor layer coating can achieve the function of UV-protection and waterproof capability, resulting in a reduced moisture-degradation of perovskite films.
Although the above studies indicate that DC can effectively improve UV light utilization in PSCs,101,104,107 various loss mechanisms, including the reflection losses, need to be considered. Roh et al. tried to embed a composite of green Lu3Al5O12:Ce3+ and orange α-SiAlON:Eu2+ in a glass as a DC converter.280 As for the stability, the DC converter-based PSCs exhibit a PCE of 440% larger than that of conventional PSCs after 100 h long UV-irradiation, revealing the enhanced UV stability. Not consistent with previous studies, they found that the JSC of PSCs slightly decreases from 21.33 mA cm−2 to 17.29 mA cm−2 with the introduction of the DC converter. They attributed the reduced PCE and JSC to the reflectance effect at the interface. This work gives an ample warning to the application of DC for PSCs. That is, the DC material has prominent scattering and reflection effects, which will, in turn, affect the transmittance of visible light. Therefore, when introducing DC materials, it is necessary to consider the thickness and the size of the material and avoid blocking the spectrum in the effective response range of PSCs.
3.3 Semiconductor oxides
Although the above-mentioned RE doped DCs have been used as active fluorescent DC layers, the dielectric properties of the phosphors need to be fine-tuned. As is proved, these phosphors have no conductive properties, and their role inside of PSCs is mainly a stent effect, which cannot promote the separation of excitons and the transport of photogenerated carriers. Inspired by the luminescent DC effects of RE dopants, our group tried to prepared the Sm3+ and Eu3+ co-doped TiO2 as ETLs to convert UV light into visible light for PSC applications.281 The PL spectra and energy transfer pathway for Sm3+ and Eu3+ co-doped TiO2 films are exhibited in Fig. 8a and b. Furthermore, the PSCs can sustain at least 80% of their initial PCE over 25 days of full sunlight irradiation or UV irradiation. Very recently, Chen et al. show that the Eu3+ doped TiO2 could effectively convert damaging UV photons into useful visible luminescence for reducing energy loss in the UV region (Fig. 8c).282 The optimized bandgap alignment at the Eu–TiO2/perovskite interface could facilitate efficient carrier extraction, leading to an enhanced PCE of 21.40%, with increased JSC and VOC. More importantly, the Eu3+ doped TiO2 modified PSCs could exhibit remarkably improved UV stability, retaining 75% of the initial PCE after exposure to UV illumination for 500 h. Jiang et al. also found that the 1.0 wt% Eu3+ doped TiO2 based PSCs could exhibit an enhanced PCE of 15.79% and IPCE value (Fig. 8d), which is related to the photoluminescence of the TiO2:Eu3+ nanocrystals.239 Some other types of RE doped TiO2 nanostructures have also been explored to reduce the energy loss and improve the photovoltaic characteristics of PSCs.283 As we all know, PSCs using TiO2 as ETLs have achieved high efficiency but tend to degrade under full spectral illumination due to UV photocatalysis. To avoid the photocatalytic properties of the TiO2 matrix, some researchers replaced TiO2 with other semiconductor oxides. Roose et al. found that Ga3+ doped SnO2 could exhibit favorable stability under full solar spectrum illumination.284 Lee et al. found that UV irradiation on the ZnGa2O4 film could result in an additional current of 110 μA cm−2, confirming the efficient UV utilization (Fig. 8e).285 Hwang et al. introduced a core/shell-structured CdS/TiO2 electrode to enhance the UV utilization and light stability of PSCs.286 The CdS shell can suppress the intrinsic trap, suppress the decomposition caused by defect catalysis, and then exhibit highly improved light stability.287 In addition to CdS, Ito et al. introduced an Sb2S3 surface blocking layer with a suitable energy band position between TiO2 and perovskite to improve the photovoltaic performance by reducing the carrier recombination in TiO2 but also promote the light stability of an unencapsulated device (Fig. 8f).288 Yin et al. demonstrated that applying 2D-TiS2 nanosheets as an effective ETL in planar PSCs could help to exhibit a PCE of 17.37% and excellent UV stability.289 Shin et al. proved that La–BaSnO3 could improve the stability of PSCs, with a steady-state PCE of 21.2%.290 Luo et al. designed Fe2O3 nanoisland that could induce a promising PCE of 18.2%, with improved UV light stability.291
 Fig. 8  (a) PL spectra of TiO2:Sm3+,Eu3+, and TiO2:Eu3+ films excited by 405 nm and 395 nm, respectively. (b) The schematic diagram of energy transfer pathway from Sm3+ to Eu3+ ions in TiO2 film. Reprinted with permission from ref. 281, copyright 2019, Elsevier; (c) schematic illustrations of the device configuration of n–i–p planar PSCs employing Eu3+ doped TiO2 as ETLs. Reprinted with permission from ref. 282, copyright 2020, Elsevier; (d) IPCE curves of PSCs with different TiO2:Eu3+. Reprinted with permission from ref. 239, copyright 2018, American Chemistry Society; (e) absorbance curves of TiO2/ZnGa2O4 composites. Reprinted with permission from ref. 285, copyright 2019, Japanese Journal of Applied Physics; (f) variation of PCE with the time of PSCs during light exposure without encapsulation in the air for 12 h. The inset pictures are the photographs of PSCs after the light exposure. Reprinted with permission from ref. 288, copyright 2014, Royal Society of Chemistry. 
3.4 Organic fluorescent materials
Besides the traditional RE dopants and semiconductors, organic materials with efficient fluorescence were developed to improve the light utilization of PSCs.5,292 Bella et al. showed that fluorinated photopolymer V570 coatings on the front side of PSCs (Fig. 9a) could induce the enhanced UV utilization and light stability, with obviously enhanced IPCE response (Fig. 9b).293 Meanwhile, the coatings could form a significant hydrophobic barrier toward environmental moisture on the rear side. Same using V570 in PSCs, Stefan Schlisske et al. report colorful PSCs by combining Violet 570, Yellow 083, and Red 300 by digital inkjet-printing processes.294 The perceived color of the devices can be tuned independently from the shape of the device. Unlike the improved PCE results from Bella et al., Stefan Schlisske et al. found that colored PSCs exhibited decreased PCE due to inevitable absorption of the dye with excessive concentration or excessive thickness (Fig. 9c). Inspired by nature compounds, Jing Cao et al. tuned the interfacial chemical interactions (Fig. 9d) and reduced the electron recombination by using the sinapoyl malate interlayer (Fig. 9e) at the ETL/perovskite interface for the UV-resistant PSCs, thus improving PCE up to 20.5%.295 Tsai et al. proved that the biaxially-extended octithiophene-based conjugated polymers (Fig. 9f) as a HTL could improve UV-photostability under continuous irradiation by a 365 nm UV irradiation for 200 min.296 Gheno et al. found that the S-tetrazine molecule allowed to reduce the loss of JSC and improved the stability of PSCs.297 Also, the conjugated side-chain groups (Fig. 9g) of polymers could facilitate its UV absorption function when applied in the structure of HTL in PSCs. Recently, studies focused on applying Kremer fluorescent blue, Eu-4,7-diphenyl-1,10-phenanthroline, bathophenanthroline, 2,2′-methylenebis(4-tert-octyl-6-benzotriazole phenol) were also used to improve the spectral response and photostability of PSCs, leading to an increased photocurrent density.68,111,298,299 Very recently, Hwang et al. developed a water-repellent platinum(II) complex, Pt–F, as a luminescent DC layer for PSCs application, obtaining an improved PCE from 21.4% to 22.0%.254 The enhanced photovoltaic can be attributed to the long-range photon DC effect of Pt–F. In addition, the introduction of Pt–F films remarkably enhanced the stability of PSCs under UV irradiation as well as humid conditions, owing to fluoroalkyl functional groups for moisture resistance.
 Fig. 9  (a) Digital photograph of a V570 coated PSC when exposed to UV light. (b) IPCE curves of the coated and uncoated devices. Reprinted with permission from ref. 293, copyright 2016, AAAS; (c) graph of the EQE (normalized to 700 nm) of a PSC coated with up to four red down-shifting layers. Left inset is the photograph of a bare inkjet-printed PSCs with no LDS layer present, and right is a photograph of inkjet-printed LDS layers with different thicknesses placed on top of PSCs while illuminated by a solar simulator. Reprinted with permission from ref. 294, copyright 2019, American Chemistry Society; (d) structure of sinapoyl malate. (e) Schematic illustration of Sinapoyl malate assembled at the interface between TiO2 and perovskite. Reprinted with permission from ref. 295, copyright 2018, Wiley-VCH; (f) chemical structures of the studied polymeric HTLs. Reprinted with permission from ref. 296, copyright 2018, Royal Society of Chemistry; (g) S-tetrazine molecule and associated photophysical steps. Reprinted with permission from ref. 297, copyright 2018, Elsevier; (h) the chemical formula of a water-repellent platinum(II) complex, Pt–F, with DC effect. Reprinted with permission from ref. 254, copyright 2020, Wiley-VCH; (i) stability of the control and ETPM modified PSCs. Reprinted with permission from ref. 300, copyright 2019, American Chemistry Society. 
Although the above organic materials exhibited significant results in terms of DC effects, there are still two issues of their low quantum efficiency and the low stability that need to be considered. Prolonged light irradiation will cause the decomposition of organic materials. In order to solve the above two problems, our group recently developed that the REs complexes for application in PSCs. The REs complexes possess a broadband absorption in the UV region ascribed to the 5D0 to 7FJ (J = 1, 2, 3, and 4) transitions of Eu3+, which can effectively transfer the absorbed UV energy to REs, and help PSCs to achieve higher photocurrent and stability. We demonstrate that the Eu(TTA)2(Phen)MAA can improve the PCE from 17.00 to 19.07% for effective utilization of incident UV light and reduced carrier recombination (Fig. 9h).300 Meanwhile, Eu(TTA)2(Phen)MAA could enhance the light stability of the device (Fig. 9i). In another work, we also introduced Eu(TTA)2(Phen)MAA to further improve the absorption of devices in the UV region, giving rise to the highest photovoltaic performance of 19.5% and photocurrent of 27.1 mA cm−2 among the reported transparent PSCs, indicating the great potential for application in the spectral broadening of PSCs.234 Above, the organic material-based DC layer can function as an efficient photon DC layer for reducing energy loss in the UV region and act as a “chemical edge sealant” that enhances the environmental stability of PSCs.
3.5 Quantum dots
Recently, semiconductor quantum dots have been considered to be an excellent way to achieve DC and spectral expansion in PSCs and other typical photovoltaics.4,301–307 Our group proved that the CsPbBr3 quantum dot (Fig. 10a) modification could boost the vapor phase (Fig. 10b) and solution-processed PSCs with a PCE of 16.4% and 20.8% by the DC effect in the UV region and suppresses carrier recombination (Fig. 10c).237 Besides, we observed a “colorful” phenomenon due to the luminescence properties of quantum dots (Fig. 10d). The conversion of the UV light eliminated a significant loss mechanism that deteriorates perovskite stability. Wang et al. found that the Mn2+ doping in CsPbCl3 quantum dots could induce an intermediate quantum state and boost the higher quantum yield of ∼60% (Fig. 10e).308 The CsPbCl3:Mn2+ quantum dots could improve PCE and EQE (Fig. 10f) accompanied by enhanced stability for PSCs. They also enhance the PCE of organic and Si-based solar cells of 3.21% and 2.98%, respectively, by insulating the CsPbCl3:Mn2+ quantum dots. Because of the excellent DC effect, perovskite nanocrystals are also widely used in photodetector to achieve better response and stability.309 Zou et al. applied CsPbBr3 quantum dots acting as an efficient DC layer to convert UV light into 510 nm light and a protective layer to prevent degradation of the hybrid perovskite for achieving a higher detectivity to UV-C, with an increased value to 1.4 mA W−1 and 2.4 × 1011 jones, respectively. Tong et al. also prepared a dual-phase CsPbBr3–Cs4PbBr6 material on the rear side to achieve faster, highly sensitive photodetection.310 As a result, the modified photodetector could exhibit a fast response time and high responsivity. In addition to perovskite nanocrystals, silicon quantum dots are fascinating materials for photovoltaics because of their superior properties, including high quantum-confined photoluminescence, nontoxicity, and earth-abundant nature.311–313 More importantly, silicon quantum dots exhibit an efficient DC effect for converting shorter wavelength excitation light (300–530nm) into the visible region light (550–800nm). Very recently, Wang et al. incorporated the silicon quantum dots inside of PSCs (Fig. 10g) to extend external quantum efficiencies over a wide wavelength range of 360–760nm.262 By converting short wavelength (300–530 nm) light to a perovskite-operating visible band (380–775 nm), the silicon quantum dots modified PSCs could yield an enhanced photocurrent density of 21.2mA cm−2 and PCE of 19.3%. Wang et al. introduced typical traditional ZnSe quantum dots on the rear side of PSCs for yielding an increased PCE from 16.6% to 17.3%, with delayed destruction speed under the UV irradiation.314 These works provide a successful strategy toward efficient PSCs and perovskite-based photodetectors by quantum dots for effective UV light utilization and thus energy loss reduction.

(b) Schematic of CsPbBr3 modified PSC devices by vapor deposition technology with organic MAI and inorganic PbI2 source. (c) J–V characteristics of the control and CsPbBr3 coated PSCs. (d) The luminescence of the CsPbBr3 nanocrystal modified devices varies as a function of time. 3.6 Carbon materials
It is always an exciting topic to build low-cost DC materials with high quantum yield. Due to high electrical conductivity, chemical stability, low cost, multiformity, and high efficiency in terms of electrical and fluorescent properties, carbon materials have made undeniable contributions. Some typical and famous carbon materials, especially graphite, carbon black, carbon nanotube, graphene, and graphdiyne, possess characteristics of DC fluorescence.315–320 Carbon materials with different dimensions were formed by different strategies of atom bonding, leading to the carrier transport behaviors with clear distinction.321,322 Generally, 0D carbon materials, fullerene, and its derivatives have good solubility and high electron density, which are considered ideal electron transport materials in PSCs.322–324 From the perspective of theoretical simulation, Hosseini et al. applied luminescent graphene quantum dots on top of a PSC to build a simple optical model for improving the light-harvesting efficiency and stability.325 Their calculated results revealed that a graphene quantum dot layer with 94% quantum yield could induce a remarkable enhancement in IPCE and thereby an enhanced photocurrent by more than 40% in the spectral range of 300–400 nm (Fig. 11a–c). They predicted that the graphene quantum dots could offer a definite possibility of achieving highly photo-stable PSCs under full spectral illumination without the requirement of an additional UV filter. Based on existing theoretical research, our results indicate that the introduction of fluorescent carbon dots in the m-TiO2 layer (Fig. 11d and e) could convert UV to blue light for efficient PSC applications.326 We revealed that the fluorescent carbon dot modified PSCs could exhibit an improved PCE of 16.4% and maintain nearly 70% of their initial PCE after 12 h of full sunlight illumination (Fig. 11f and g). Our results further proved that the DC effect of fluorescent carbon dots is a novel and practical approach to improving the PCE and photostability of PSCs. Similarly, Wang et al. revealed that introducing carbon dots (Fig. 11h) into SnO2 could reduce the trap-state density and increase the mobility in SnO2 films, leading to PSCs with a high PCE of over 20% with better UV stability after exposure to UV illumination.327 Bian et al. developed N-doped graphene quantum dots with a quantum yield of 80% to convert UV light into visible photons for γ-CsPbI3 based PSCs applications.328 Photographs taken under visible light and 365 nm UV light of samples with a different layer of N-doped graphene quantum dots deposited on FTO glass is shown in Fig. 11i. The N-doped graphene quantum dots could serve as an active DC layer to harvest the UV light, leading to the optimized PSCs with an increased JSC of 19.15 mA cm−2 and a PCE of 16.02%, compared with that of 18.67 mA cm−2 and 15.53% for control devices, which proved the effective strategy in improving the stability by suppressing the UV light from entering the device. Zhang et al. used γ-GD quantum dots in PSCs as a surface modifier to enhance UV and thermal stability.112 Li et al. report a solution-processed carbon quantum dots could help to achieve higher PCE, VOC and JSC.329 Xie et al. demonstrate that the addition of C60 to PCBM can effectively improve the device performance of 19.31% and retain 91.3% of its initial PCE over 1056 hours of continuous illumination.330 Wang et al. found that μGR crosslinking could result in an increased PCE by 11.40% for CsPbI3 based PSCs by suppressing the thermal/moisture-induced agglomeration.331 Zhou et al. used polyethylenimine modified cross-stacked super aligned carbon nanotube film to enhance the photovoltaic performance of PSCs.332 As a result, the modified PSCs exhibit an optimized PCE of ∼11% with negligible hysteresis. Moreover, PSCs based cross-stacked super aligned carbon nanotube show superior durability in comparison to the standard Ag-based devices. Above all, it can be found that that the carbon materials with higher quantum yield could offer a pronounced possibility in achieving highly photo-stable PSCs under full spectral illumination.
 Fig. 11  (a) Normalized absorption and emission spectra of graphene quantum dots-EDA and graphene quantum dots-G and the IPCE curves of a bare PSC. (b) Calculated IPCE for PSCs without and with graphene quantum dots-G. (c) Calculated total IPCE spectra for PSCs without and with graphene quantum dots-EDA. Reprinted with permission from ref. 325, copyright 2018, Royal Society of Chemistry; (d) and (e) stand for the schematic illustration of the energy levels and cross-sectional view in PSCs. (f) J–V curves based on different concentrations of carbon dots. (g) Stability of PSCs with various concentrations of carbon dots under light exposure. Reprinted with permission from ref. 328, copyright 2019, Royal Society of Chemistry. 
From the above research, we can conclude that the DC materials play a decisive role in efficient PSCs by effectively utilizing the UV light for reduced energy loss. In contrast to the UC process, the effect of the DC process in PSCs is more prominent. In order to maximize the spectrum utilization of PSCs through DC, the bandgap of perovskite and device structures of PSCs must be considered in the selection of DC materials. In addition to the luminescence properties of DC materials, the reliability, including light stability and environmental stability, must be taken into account for future commercial applications in PSCs. DC material is a more beneficial strategy for improving the light stability of PSCs. At the same time, the introduction of a new DC layer inside or outside the PSCs means an increase in the total cost, which depends on the type of material and the deposition method. Most importantly, in order to evaluate the best DC materials, it is necessary to measure the fluorescence efficiency of DC materials and the PCE enhancement of PSCs accurately. Most of the studied DC materials are based on RE doping because of the unique fluorescence characteristics, and this requires the host material to have high transmittance and low scattering. Defects in the surface and crystal structure of the host material have an important influence on the fluorescence characteristics, which will cause the energy to be absorbed directly in the host rather than transferred to the activated ions. Also, the host material also needs to have good chemical stability in terms of humidity and temperature. If placed inside of the PSCs, the DC materials cannot react with the light-absorbing material or electrode. In recent years, semiconductor nanoparticles, especially perovskite nanocrystals, have also been used as potential DC materials, which could exhibit nearly 100% fluorescence efficiency. However, when placing the semiconductor nanoparticles inside of the PSCs, there will be an ineluctable problem of fluorescence efficiency reduction caused by aggregation. There is another issue of poor humidity stability of the water-sensitive DC materials, which could be accompanied by packaging technology. The fluorescence intensity has a great relationship with the thickness, while the efficient PSCs are very demanding in thickness. Above all, taking the DC mechanism as the goal to reduce energy loss and further improve the PCE of PSCs, then more research attention should be paid to developing new DC materials possessing a compromise between optical characteristics (fluorescence efficiency, transmittance, absorption wavelength) and electrical performance (Table 2).

4. Tandem or graded structures for optical complementarity
As is discussed in the previous section, tandem solar cells composed of two or more independent solar cells connected in series or parallel can broaden the spectral response range of solar cells and maximize the conversion of light energy into electrical energy.333–337 The solar light spectrum will be divided into several continuous bands, and the devices are made of semiconductors with the best matching bandwidth.12,28,35,117 Generally, there are two main types of tandem solar cells. First is the 4T structured device, two subcells are independent and are not electrically connected (Fig. 12a). The only requirement for the 4T is that the top cells should be semi-transparent to ensure that the light needed for the bottom subcells can be sufficiently transmitted. The sum of the output PCE of the 4T device is obtained by simple manual calculation from the top subcell and bottom subcell under the same light conditions. The other is the 2T structured device, which needs to be completed in the whole process of complex device preparation (Fig. 12b). In order to deliver optimum performance in a 2T structured device, specific requirements, including bandgap matching and solvent dissolution, need to be considered. What is needed to be noticed is that the subcells are necessary to be connected with an ideal carrier transporting layer with high transparency and high conductivity.132 Above all, compared to the 2T tandem device, the 4T configuration is much easier to fabricate for the unnecessary precise current matching while exhibiting relatively higher tolerance of bandgap matching.338,339 Nevertheless, the 2T configuration is preferred over 4T because it has minimized parasitic absorption and higher practical efficiency.340Fig. 12c and d depicts plots of theoretical PCEs of the double-junction tandem solar cell versus bottom and top cell bandgaps in both 2T and 4T configurations along with the bandgap combinations.117 The black dotted line represents the ∼1.1 eV c-Si and the best performing CIGS solar cells. These could be ideally paired with PSCs in the 1.6–1.75 eV range to yield a PCE of ∼44%.47 From recent results, the perovskite/Si tandem solar cell has been the focus of a great deal of research and has recently reached a PCE of 29.5% in 2T configurations, exceeding the record PCE of 26.7% for single-junction Si-based solar cells.24
 Fig. 12  Schematic pictures of tandem solar cells in (a) 4T and (b) 2T tandem structures; (c) the theoretical PCE provided for 4T tandems. The photocurrent density was calculated by integrating the AM 1.5G spectrum, assuming 100% EQE above the bandgap. (d) Theoretical efficiency limit for 2T solar cells, calculated with different subcell thicknesses. Reprinted with permission from ref. 117, copyright 2018, Springer Nature; (e) and (f) efficiency limits for 4T and 2T all-perovskite tandem solar cells. Reprinted with permission from ref. 340, copyright 2017, Wiley-VCH. 
Traditional tandem solar cells based on the dye, quantum dots, or inorganic thin films (CuInSeS) usually are treated to be fabricated at low-costs,341,342 but their PCEs are still much inferior to the perovskite-based tandem solar cells,39 which exhibits bandgap tenability and low-cost processing ability. Adjustable bandgaps making perovskites an ideal candidate for preparing all-perovskite tandem solar cells with a maximum prediction PCE.343,344 All-perovskite tandem solar cells have a substantial potential to outperform the Shockley Queisser limit of single-junction solar cells,345,346 which is an alternative approach with some inherent advantages, such as cost-effective and low-temperature monolithic fabrication processes.164,339,347 Selecting perovskite photoactive materials with complementary bandgaps is essential to enhance the efficiency of all-perovskite tandem solar cells.348,349 Hossain et al. recently investigated the optical and electrical properties for planar all-perovskite tandem solar cells and conducted a systematic investigation for selecting absorber materials, where CsyFA1−yPb(IxBr1−x)3 with a wide bandgap of 1.72 eV acts as a top cell, while MASnxPb1−xI3 with a low bandgap of 1.16 eV acts as a bottom cell, to obtain a maximum theoretical PCE of more than 35%.350 Regardless of the existing developed perovskite materials, Fig. 12e presents the efficiency map of all-perovskite tandem solar cells containing two ideal perovskite-based subcells with different bandgaps in 2T configuration, while Fig. 12f stands for the 4T configuration. We can learn that all-perovskite tandems could also offer a route to high efficiency exceeding 38% with optimized bandgaps for top cells and bottom subcells.340
To realize an efficient all-perovskite based tandem solar cell, fabricating semi-transparent top-cells and highly efficient perovskite subcells is critical. Currently, wide bandgap can be accomplished with CsyFA1−yPb(IxBr1−x)3 or MASnBr3 based perovskite. The most studied Sn–Pb binary mixed perovskites were usually utilized for the bottom cell due to the feasibility of bandgap tunability from 1.6 eV to 1.2 eV, making them suitable candidate photoactive absorber as the low-bandgap subcells in tandem devices. Replacing Pb with Sn can shift the bandgap down to ∼1.3 eV for MASnI3,47,351 while the optical bandgap could further lower down to 1.17 eV for MAPb0.5Sn0.5I3. Even though there some breakthroughs in mixed Sn/Pb perovskite for all-perovskite tandem solar cells in the current time, it is still far from being optimum since the suitable low-bandgap mixed Sn–Pb top cells are not air-stable enough.47,351–354 From previous studies, we believe that a perovskite/Si tandem solar cell is the first step in the commercialization for future efficient devices, while the all-perovskite tandem may be the ultimate goal for its attractive opportunities in the thin-film photovoltaic industry, including wide-band response, low cost and the use of flexible and lightweight substrates. What needs to be clarified is that the review paper mainly discusses the strategies of spectral broadening of PSCs; we will not review the strategies of perovskite/Si and perovskite/CIGS, in which the perovskite films are used as an auxiliary means to improve the spectral range of other devices.338,355,356 Readers can learn more by reading the recent literature on tandem solar cells.171 We conclude with a complete summary of perovskite/perovskite, perovskite/organic, perovskite/quantum dots, or perovskite/dyes based tandem solar cells in the following, in which the wide-bandgap perovskite, organic materials, quantum dots, and dyes were used to complement the spectral response range of perovskite in the UV and NIR regions.
4.1 All-perovskite tandem solar cell
As we mentioned before, mechanical stacking of a wide bandgap PSC as the top-cell on a low bandgap subcell for a 4T tandem device allows processing diversity.357–359 Since the top-cell and the subcell are fabricated independently, each subcell can be fabricated using their optimized processes without any additional processing restrictions.359–361 Early all-perovskite tandem solar cells mainly focused on the optimization of the composition of perovskite materials (and thus changing the optical bandgap), specifically including wide bandgap perovskite materials and low bandgap perovskite materials.358,362–365 In 2016, Li et al. demonstrated a PCE of ∼9.5% with VOC of 2.24 V by combining the MAPbBr3 based PSCs as top cell and MAPbI3 as the bottom cell in 4T configuration.366 Zhao et al. used stacked a semi-transparent wide-bandgap FA0.8Cs0.2Pb(I0.7Br0.3)3 top cells (1.75 eV) and a low-bandgap (FASnI3)0.6(MAPbI3)0.4 subcells (1.25 eV) (Fig. 13a) to obtain a 4T all-perovskite tandem solar cells with a PCE of 23.1% (Fig. 13b).367 By employing a combined compositional process and interfacial engineering, Yang et al. demonstrated a 4T all-perovskite tandem solar cell with a high PCE of 19.08% by combining a MAPbI3 based top cell (1.55 eV) and a MA0.5FA0.5Pb0.75Sn0.25I3 based bottom cell (1.33 eV). The transmittance spectrum of the semi-transparent MAPbI3 based PSCs and the EQE for MAPb1−xSnxI3 (x = 0.25, 0.5, 0.75, and 1.00) based PSCs are shown in Fig. 13c and d, respectively.355 Rajagopal et al. utilized an indene-C60 bis-adduct to achieve optimized interfacial contact for improving the VOC to 1.98 V in all-perovskite tandem solar cells.340 This strategy also unlocks opportunities for solar water splitting using hybrid perovskites with solar-to-hydrogen efficiencies beyond 15%. Eperon et al. obtain a PCE of 20.3% by combining FA0.75Cs0.25Sn0.5Pb0.5I3 (1.2 eV) with a wide bandgap FA0.83Cs0.17Pb(I0.5Br0.5)3 for 4T tandem application.353 Zhao et al. developed a low-bandgap perovskite (FASnI3)0.6(MAPbI3)0.4 (∼1.25 eV) based PSC with over 70% EQE in the range of 700–900 nm to deliver a PCE of 21.0% when mechanically stacked with a MA0.7FA0.3PbI3 (∼1.58 eV) bandgap perovskite top cell to form the 4T tandem solar cell.368
 Fig. 13  (a) Schematic diagram of the device structure of the 4-T tandem perovskite by using a low-Eg 1.25 eV and a wide-Eg 1.75 eV subcells. (b) J–V curves of the 1.75 eV perovskite-based top cell illuminated from the glass/FTO side and the 1.25 eV perovskite-based bottom cell with and without the semi-transparent 1.75 eV top cell. Reprinted with permission from ref. 367, copyright 2018, American Chemistry Society; (c) the transmittance spectrum and image of the semi-transparent MAPbI3 based PSCs with sputtered top ITO electrode. (d) EQE spectra of the MAPb1−xSnxI3 based PSCs. Reprinted with permission from ref. 355, copyright 2016, Wiley-VCH; (e) SEM images of perovskite films prepared with 7% GuaSCN additive. Inset shows the HRTEM image of the grain boundary region with 7% GuaSCN as additive. (f) MPP tracking of the semi-transparent front PSC, filtered bottom PSC, and the 4-T tandem device under AM 1.5G illumination. Reprinted with permission from ref. 369, copyright 2019, AAAS. (g) Schematic illustration of the vacuum-assisted growth control method for processing high-quality low-bandgap Sn–Pb perovskite. (h) Stabilized PCE of the best performing solar cell under maximum-power-point tracking conditions. In the inset, a top surface SEM image of the Sn–Pb perovskite thin film. (i) J–V curves of the two kinds of single-junction solar cells. Reprinted with permission from ref. 371, copyright 2020, Wiley-VCH. 
In addition to the optimization of the components of the perovskite materials, the optimization of crystallinity and carrier transporting characteristics can also promote the photovoltaic characteristic of the low-bandgap perovskite and the PCE of the corresponding PSCs. Tong et al. found that introducing 7% GuaSCN additives could reduce the densities of small grains and pinholes in low-bandgap (FASnI3)0.6(MAPbI3)0.4 films (∼1.25 eV) (Fig. 13e), resulting in carrier lifetimes of greater than 1 μs and diffusion lengths of 2.5 μm.369 When combined the GuaSCN modified (FASnI3)0.6(MAPbI3)0.4 based PSCs with Cs0.05FA0.8MA0.15PbI2.55Br0.45 based PSCs to a 4T tandem PSCs, they obtained an efficient PCE of 25% with higher stability than that of the control cells over time (Fig. 13f). Above all, we can conclude that the NIR absorbing Sn–Pb mixed perovskite materials will enable all-perovskite tandem solar cells to reach the highest efficiencies at the lowest costs. Moghadamzadeh et al. demonstrated that introducing Cs+ into the FA0.8MA0.2Sn0.5Pb0.5I3 perovskite could improve the photo-stability of the PSCs by reducing the aggregation of residual nanosized SnyPb(1−y)I2.370 The preparation method will also have an important impact on the crystallinity and photovoltaic performance of the perovskite. Nejand et al. reported a vacuum-assisted growth control (Fig. 13g) of solution-processed low-bandgap Sn–Pb perovskite with large columnar crystals.371 In comparison with the well-established processing of Sn–Pb perovskite via traditional antisolvent deposition, the vacuum-assisted growth control approach produced Sn–Pb perovskite could exhibit a significantly enhanced charge-carrier lifetime. The resulting low-bandgap PSCs showed a remarkable PCE of 18.2% and stable PCE of 17.1% (Fig. 13h), and helped a 4T all-perovskite tandem solar cell achieve a PCE of 23% (Fig. 13i). Optimizing the carrier transport layer is also a pivotal breakthrough to achieve high-efficiency tandem solar cells. Yao et al. found that using diluted PEDOT:PSS as HTL in Sn–Pb based PSCs can decrease the energy loss at the interface and inhibit charge recombination.372 Then, they constructed a 4T all-perovskite tandem solar cell using an optical splitting system with the diluted PEDOT:PSS modified Sn–Pb based PSCs as the bottom cell and a wide bandgap PSC as the top cell to achieve an optimized PCE of 23.26%.
For 2T all-perovskite tandem solar cells, the most critical component is the interlayer that not only connects two subcells but also protects the bottom subcell from any damages caused by the solution-process of the top cell.373 Palmstrom et al. developed a strategy for optimized interconnection layers for 2T all-perovskite tandem solar cells by incorporating a nucleation surface by atomic layer deposition (Fig. 14a), with the normalized EQEs for the subcells plotted to calibrate the efficiency measurements in Fig. 14b.175 As a result, they obtained PCE of 23.1% on rigid substrates and 21.3% on flexible plastic substrates (Fig. 14c). Jiang et al. demonstrate a bottom-up solution-processed 2T all-perovskite tandem solar cell with a PCE of 7% via developing a novel interlayer, Spiro-OMeTAD/PEDOT:PSS/PEI/PCBM:PEI, to bridge two perovskite subcells and protect the bottom perovskite film during the top perovskite film deposition.374 Heo et al. fabricated all-perovskite tandem solar cells with VOC of 2.25 V and PCE of 10.4% by physically sandwiching two separated MAPbBr3 and MAPbI3 cells together.375 However, due to the relatively large bandgap of the MAPbI3 (1.55 V) and the physically connecting interface, they obtained a smaller JSC value of only 8.3 mA cm−2. This work revealed that the ohmic contact for the interconnecting layer is essential to improve the performance of the device. Forgács et al. used doped organic semiconductors as carrier transporting layers to combine the Cs0.15FA0.85Pb(I0.3Br0.7)3 as the top cell and MAPbI3 as the bottom cells for 2T tandem solar cell applications.132 As a result, the all-perovskite tandem cells delivered a PCE of 18%, which benefited from the reduced carrier recombination at the perovskite interfaces. Fundamentally, thermionic emission plays a critical role in the electron transport process in the interconnecting layer. Li et al. demonstrated a new thermionic emission-based interconnecting layer by introducing a fluoride silane-incorporated polyethylenimine ethoxylated hybrid system to passivate the surface defects to reduce electrical loss, leading to the PCE of 17.9%.376 The interconnection layers control carrier collection and recombination from subcells. Yu et al. reported an interconnection layer in all-perovskite tandem cells consisting merely of a I− doped C60 layer and a SnO1.76 layer (Fig. 14d).377 Energy diagrams before and after the Fermi level alignment reveal that there is nearly no band bending at the interface of the SnO1.76 and the low bandgap perovskite owing to their very similar work function (Fig. 14e). The C60/SnO1.76 based interconnection layer boosts the PCE of small-area tandem PSCs (5.9 mm2) and large-area tandem PSCs (1.15 cm2) to 24.4% and 22.2% (Fig. 14f), respectively. Chang et al. demonstrated a solution-processed interlayer consisting of cross-linked p-doped PTAA by introducing (TPE-MN3) and Mo(tfdCOCF3)3 for all-perovskite tandem solar cells, as is depicted in Fig. 14g.347 Owing to the excellent carrier conductivity of the interfacial layers, the tandem solar cells could exhibit a promising PCE up to 16.20%.
 Fig. 14  (A) Cross-sectional SEM image of the prepared all-perovskite tandem solar cells. (b) Normalized EQE for the subcells of the same tandem solar cells. (c) Photo of flexible tandem device. (e) Energy diagram for the C60/SnO1.76/perovskite/C60 layers in all-perovskite tandem solar cells. (f) J–V curves with scanning in reverse and forward directions for a large-area (1.15 cm2) tandem solar cell. The inset photo shows the front side (left) and backside (right) of the large-area tandem device. Reprinted with permission from ref. 377, copyright 2020, Springer Nature; (g) (g-1) schematic illustration of the device architecture and the molecular structures of materials, (g-2) the equivalent circuit model of the proposed tandem device, and (g-3) the schematic of cross-linking of PTAA by TPE-MN3 via C–H insertion of nitrene radicals under UV illumination. Reprinted with permission from ref. 347, copyright 2019, Elsevier; (h) J–V characteristics and (i) EQE for a FA0.83Cs0.17Pb(Br0.7I0.3)3/MAPbI3/FA0.6MA0.4Pb0.4Sn0.6I3 triple-junction architecture. Reprinted with permission from ref. 378, copyright 2019, Elsevier. 
McMeekin et al. employ an acetonitrile/methylamine composite solvent to enable the sequential processing of perovskite layers upon bottom perovskite-based subcells.378 Furthermore, they showed that the SnF2 modified MAPb0.75Sn0.25I3 perovskite could be processed via the acetonitrile/methylamine solvent system, and extend the light absorption of the multi-junction cell to 925 nm. More importantly, they established a model of triple-junction PSCs by using 1.94/1.57/1.24 eV perovskite materials, presenting a VOC of 2.83 V. Fig. 14h and i reveal the J–V performance and optimized response curves of the triple-junction tandem all-PSCs with optimized total thicknesses of 1382 nm, indicating the possibility of achieving a PCE of 26.7% with corresponding VOC of 3.16 V. By optimizing the perovskite photoactive layer with the help of methylammonium chloride vapor, Leijtens et al. used micron-sized FA0.75Cs0.25Sn0.5Pb0.5I3 to fabricate an all-perovskite tandem solar cell with a stabilized PCE of 19.1%.343 Moreover, the unencapsulated Sn–Pb based PSCs could maintain their full performance after 150 hours at 85 °C in air. Tong et al. found that combining GuaSCN modified (FASnI3)0.6(MAPbI3)0.4 with Cs0.05FA0.8MA0.15PbI2.55Br0.45 as 2T tandem PSCs could achieve a PCE of 25%.369 By using an additive vacuum-deposition protocol, Ávila et al. report an optimized MAPbI3–MAPbI3 tandem solar cell with VOC of 2.30 V.379 Zhao et al. report a bulk-passivation strategy via incorporating chlorine to enlarge the grains and reduce electronic disorder of the (FASnI3)0.6(MAPbI3)0.4 layers.380 As a result, they fabricated efficient low-bandgap PSCs using thick absorber layers of ∼750nm, enabling a champion PCE of 21% and steady-state efficiency of 20.7% for the 2T all-perovskite tandem solar cells. Approaches to mixed Pb–Sn perovskites must overcome the drawbacks of Sn2+ oxidation. Very recently, Tan's group reported simultaneous enhancements in the efficiency, uniformity, and stability of narrow-bandgap subcells using strongly reductive surface-anchoring zwitterionic molecules.381 Then, they obtained a best PCE of 25.6% and a certified PCE of 24.2% for all-perovskite tandem solar cells with an active area of 0.049cm2 and 1cm2, respectively.
In addition to the widely studied all-perovskite tandem solar cells stacked with two junctions, all-perovskite triple-junction solar cells can feasibly provide even higher efficiency than that available by tandem solar cells.339,382–384 Hörantner et al. evaluated that an all-perovskite triple-junction solar cell could achieve PCE up to ∼37% using perovskites with ideal bandgaps.384 McMeekin et al. first reported the fabrication of the proof-of-concept all-perovskite triple-junction solar cell with a PCE of only 6.7%.378 Recently, by optimizing the interconnection layers of C60/SALD-SnO2/Au/PEDOT:PSS, Wang et al. demonstrated efficient and reproducible all-perovskite triple-junction solar cells with a very promising PCE of 16.8% by combining 1.73eV, 1.57eV, and 1.23eV photoactive layers.385 Xiao et al. demonstrated the solution processing of efficient all-perovskite triple-junction solar cells using an optimal-bandgap of 1.99, 1.60, and 1.22 eV for the front, middle, and back subcells, respectively. By introducing the NiO/PTAA bilayer as the HTL for the middle subcell, they obtained monolithic all-perovskite triple-junction solar cells with a champion PCE of 20.1%, together with a high FF of 81.1% and a VOC of 2.8 V.339 Above, the high-performance 2T structured two-junction and triple-junction all-perovskite tandem solar cells have the universality of preparation methods, and can be manufactured by common solution processing techniques or vacuum vapor deposition methods. Most importantly, the interconnection layer control for effective transport of carriers will always be the research focus of the 2T all-perovskite tandem solar cells.
Another way to realize all PSCs and enhance solar spectrum utilization of PSCs is through the compositional grading strategy, which has been widely exploited in efficient Cu(In,Ga)Se2, CdTe, GaAs, and quantum dot based solar cells.386 In contrast to traditional tandem solar cells, complex interconnections and current coupling in the graded configuration are unnecessary, while the electron–hole collection efficiency can be enhanced considerably, resulting in an acceptable output voltage and a considerable output current. However, realizing and maintaining compositionally graded perovskite photoactive from solution processing is still challenging. In 2016, Ergen et al. firstly demonstrated a graded bandgap PSC based on MASnI3 and MAPbI3−xBrx by incorporating GaN, monolayer hexagonal boron nitride, and graphene aerogel inside of devices to achieve an optimized PCE of 21.7%387 (Fig. 15a). The EQE data indicate that the hexagonal boron nitride and graphene aerogel modified PSCs could exhibit improved spectral response at long wavelengths (Fig. 15b). After that, Bian et al. designed a graded bandgap CsPbI2+xBr1−x based PSC (Fig. 15c) by using multi-graded heterojunction inorganic CsPbBrI2 and CsPbI3 quantum dots as component cells for covering most of the visible solar spectrum from 300 nm to 700 nm.388 From the well-matched energy-level diagram as well as the proposed carrier generation and transport mechanism for the graded device shown in Fig. 15d, we can conclude that the interface could induce favorable band bending at the valence-/conduction-band edge for improved carrier transport. Under the optimization of extended-spectrum response, high carrier mobility, and well-matched energy levels. Li et al. designed a PSC architecture by introducing CsPbBrxI3−x layers as HTLs for the grade adjustment to reach an optimal energy alignment (Fig. 15e) of the valence and conduction band between MAPbI3 and CsPbBrxI3−x.389 For further increasing the grade to 2, 3, and 4graded, the multi-grade type heterojunction could provide more energy barriers that prevent the generation of reverse photocurrent (Fig. 15f). As a result, the CsPbBrxI3−x modified PSCs (Fig. 15g) could exhibit an improved PCE by nearly 40% to that of control devices. To further enhance PCE of graded PSCs, Cho et al. developed a “more graded” PSCs by constructing an additional FAPbBr3−xIx layer to improve carrier collection and reduce carrier recombination at the rear interface between the perovskite layer and the HTL for boosting the PSC efficiency.390
 Fig. 15  (a) Schematic figure of a graded bandgap PSC; the GaN, monolayer hexagonal boron nitride, and graphene aerogel are vital components of an efficient cell architecture. (b) EQE spectra for typical graded bandgap PSCs with and without h-BN and graphene aerogel components. The cells without graphene aerogel exhibit compositional fluctuation due to ionic motion. Reprinted with permission from ref. 387, copyright 2016, Springer Nature; (c) schematic structure and (d) bandgap diagram to exhibit the collection process of photogenerated charge carriers in graded PSCs. Reprinted with permission from ref. 388, copyright 2018, Elsevier; (e) schematic energy level at the interface for MAPbI3/CsPbBrxI3−x films. (f) Schematic figures of carrier transport mechanism in (f-1) the neat HTL-free PSCs, (f-2) the HTL-free PSCs for 1-grade, and (f-3) the HTL-free device for multigrade. (g) Cross-section SEM image of graded CsPbBrxI3−x films based PSCs. Reprinted with permission from ref. 389, copyright 2017, Wiley-VCH. 
4.2 Perovskite/organic tandem solar cell
In addition to the all-perovskite based tandem solar cells, organic materials, and semiconductor quantum dots with an excellent light response in the NIR region can also broaden the spectrum response of PSCs.391 From another point of view, the photovoltaic performance of all-perovskite tandem solar cells is susceptible to the solution fabrication process of the subcells, where the wide-bandgap perovskite layer should be cautiously deposited to prevent the solvent erosion (DMF, DMSO, NMP etc.) of the top perovskite layer.380 Although researchers have developed dense transparent conductive film (such as SnO2/Au, SnO1.76/C60) as an interconnection layer, it is still a huge problem for the stability and reproducibility of all-perovskite tandem solar cells.377,381 The merits of the intrinsic orthogonal solvents adopted to dissolve the organic–inorganic perovskite and organic materials make the fabrication of perovskite/organic tandem devices more attractive.392,393 More importantly, the organic solar cells exhibit higher stability and bandgap tunability compared with the Sn-based perovskite counterparts, making them be potential candidates as the bottom cell of the tandem solar cells. For example, Liu demonstrates a 2T tandem perovskite/polymer bulk-heterojunction solar cell (Fig. 16a) that combining a ∼90 nm thick perovskite top cell with a ∼100 nm thick polymer: fullerene blend back subcell by a zwitterionic fullerene, Ag, and MoO3 as a recombination layer.394 The energy band diagram in Fig. 16b highlights the ability of both C60-SB and C60-N to generate large negative interfacial dipoles on silver, resulting in a PCE of 16.0%, which is 75% greater than that of ∼90 nm perovskite-based single-junction PSCs and 65% greater than that of a polymer device. This strategy can broaden the spectral response range of PSCs and significantly reduce the lead content in devices. Chen et al. present a 2-D perovskite/polymer tandem solar cell by utilizing a new IR-sensitive block copolymer, PBSeDTEG8, with photosensitivity up to 950 nm.395 The optimized thickness is adjusted to be around 90–105 nm for the polymer as the bottom subcell and 180–190 nm for the perovskite as the top subcell, achieving the highest PCE of 10.2% (Fig. 16c). To broaden the photoresponse of PSCs, our group integrated a novel PBDB-TF:BTP-4Cl organic layer on the PSCs to extend the photoresponse of the device to 950 nm.396 A maximum IPCE response of 60% at 810 nm revealed that the PBDB-TF:BTP-4Cl can provide high current output in the range beyond the absorption edge of perovskite. Furthermore, the PBDB-TF:BTP-4Cl layer could extract holes efficiently from the perovskite film due to the suitable energy level matching and then transport holes to the Au electrode. Liu et al. present a tandem solar cell with a nanostructured perovskite and PBDTT-DPP:PC70BM layer as the photoactive layer and a PFN/doped MoO3/MoO3 structure as the interconnecting carrier transporting layer.397 The optical constants measured by fitting ellipsometry data (Fig. 16d) revealed that the perovskite film exhibits a strong extinction capability in the short wavelength range while the PBDTT-DPP:PC70BM layer is sensitive to long-wavelength photons. The 3D mesh plot of the simulated photocurrent versus the thickness (Fig. 16e) revealed that the maximum photocurrent of tandem solar cells is usually achieved when the thickness of the organic subcell is at its interference peaks (100 nm, 200 nm, and 360 nm) while the thickness of the perovskite layer is limited to the range of 90 to 150 nm. Guo et al. demonstrate an efficient perovskite/organic tandem solar cell by integrating a low bandgap PBDTTT-E-T:IEICO on MAPbI3 layer to achieve a maximum wavelength of 930 nm for light-harvesting.123 The corresponding EQE curves and integrated JSC values (Fig. 16f) illustrated that the EQE of the tandem device could exceed 50% in the NIR range, resulting in an enhanced JSC of over 24 mA cm−2. Li et al. developed efficient perovskite/organic tandem solar cells by integrating the PMABr passivated perovskite (1.74 eV) and low bandgap organic PBDB-T:SN6IC-4F (1.30 eV) as photoactive layers (Fig. 16g).398 The resulting perovskite–organic tandem solar cells could exhibit a remarkable PCE of 15.13% and 13.61% with high VOC exceeding 1.8 V on a rigid substrate and flexible substrate, respectively. To make use of the obtained high VOC, they assembled the tandem solar cells into a solar-to-hydrogen system combining with dual electrocatalysis to achieve a solar-to-hydrogen efficiency of 12.30% (Fig. 16h). Lang et al. constructed a tandem solar cell with inorganic CsPbI2Br perovskite and organic conjugated molecules PTB7-Th:IEICO-4F bulk-heterojunction, exhibiting a remarkably PCE of 17.24% compared to that of 12.09% and 10.89% for the single junction CsPbI2Br based PSCs and PTB7-Th:IEICO-4F based organic solar cells.399 Wu et al. developed a perovskite/organic with PCE of 18.06% by integrating tandem solar cells with inorganic CsPbI2.1Br0.9 and a narrow bandgap organic PM6:Y6 serving as top and bottom cells, respectively.400 Xie et al. reported a perovskite/organic tandem solar cell with PCE exceeding 18% by integrating CsPbI2Br inorganic perovskite as top cell and organic PM6:Y6 or PTB7-Th:O6T-4F (the complementary absorption spectra is exhibited Fig. 16i) as bottom solar cells.401 Very recently, Chen et al. developed perovskite/organic tandem solar cells with a landmark PCE of 20.6% (certified as 19.54%), benefiting from the complementary bandgaps and orthogonal processing solvents.402 They used the orthogonal solvents for processing front and rear subcells and Ag nanoparticle-based interconnecting layers to reduce optical loss. The obtained efficiency is among the best performing PCE with the device structure of perovskite/organic tandem. Above all, the recent studies reveal that a perovskite/organic tandem structure is a promising strategy to extend and enhance sunlight utilization for PSCs. These studies provided a critical starting point for future advances in solution-processed perovskite tandem solar cells with complementary absorption.
 Fig. 16  (a) Device structure and cross-sectional SEM image of perovskite/polymer tandem solar cells. (b) Energy level diagram of the tandem device. Reprinted with permission from ref. 394, copyright 2016, American Chemistry Society; (c) photon absorption rate profile for each subcell inside the hybrid tandem solar cell. Reprinted with permission from ref. 395, copyright 2015, Royal Society of Chemistry; (d) the optical constants (n and k) of the perovskite layer (blue) and the PBDTT-DPP:PC70BM layer (red), (e) the 3-D mesh plot of simulated photocurrents of hybrid tandem solar cells as a function of the thickness combination of the perovskite layer and the organic layer. (f) EQE and Jint curves of the PBDTTT-E-T:IEICO and MAPbI3based tandem solar cell. Reprinted with permission from ref. 123, copyright 2018, Royal Society of Chemistry; (g) energy level diagram of the PMABr passivated perovskite and organic PBDB-T:SN6IC-4F based 2T perovskite/organic tandem solar cell. (h) The J–V curves of tandem solar cell and NiFe LDH electrodes in a two-electrode system used for water splitting. Reprinted with permission from ref. 398, copyright 2020, Wiley-VCH. (i) Absorption spectra of the different materials used in the tandem cell. Reprinted with permission from ref. 401, copyright 2018, Elsevier. 
So far, the best PCE obtained by the perovskite/organic tandem solar cells is ∼21%,402–404 which is much lower than the PCE value obtained from all-perovskite tandem devices. Since the only difference between these two tandem devices is the composition of the bottom subcell, the low PCE of the perovskite/organic tandem device is mainly due to the relatively low photovoltaic performance of the organic solar cells. At this stage, the highest PCE of a single-junction organic solar cell is 18%.405 Therefore, future research can start from the following aspects: (1) study the working principle of perovskite/organic tandem devices in detail, especially the photoelectric loss mechanism of the interface, and develop an interconnection layer with high conductivity and high stability. (2) Develop organic photovoltaic sub-devices with excellent light response only in the UV and NIR regions to realize complementary absorption with perovskite. (3) Develop organic/perovskite heterostructure devices, such as using Sn–Pb binary perovskite nanocrystals to be dissolved in a solvent of organic materials to achieve one-step forming and crystallization.
4.3 Perovskite/quantum dots or dyes tandem solar cell
Except for the organic materials that respond in the NIR, quantum dots or dyes with low-bandgaps can provide more complementary absorption in the NIR region and reduce the energy loss (Fig. 17a).406,407 Among the most studied NIR quantum dots, PbS quantum dots appear as an attractive alternative for silicon and perovskite due to the ease of fabrication, low-cost processing, and bandgap tunability, and excellent air-stability.408–410 More importantly, controlling the size of PbS quantum dots could effectively tune the bandgap for complementary absorption. Thus, extensive efforts over time are required to achieve such high PCE for perovskite/PbS quantum dots tandem solar cells. Since the fabrication process of 2T tandem devices is challenging, researchers prefer to fabricate 4T tandem solar cells by mechanical attachment of both subcells. Manekkathodi et al. reported 4T perovskite/PbS quantum dots tandem device with a best PCE of 20.55% and stabilized PCE of 20.2% by fabricating the semi-transparent PSCs with MoO3/Au/Ag/MoO3 as a top electrode.411 Later, Chen et al. improved the design of perovskite/PbS quantum dots 4T tandem solar cells and obtained a PCE of 24%.122 Very recently, 2T integrated devices have also been thoroughly studied for simplified preparation processes and large-scale production. Karani et al. designed a 2T perovskite/quantum dots tandem solar cells (Fig. 17b) based on a PSC as the top subcell and chalcogenide colloidal quantum dots as the bottom subcell.406 Combining the tandem model with experimental data, they expected that the postulated PCE of 29.7% could be achieved for the tandem device based on a PSC (1.55 eV) as the top cell and PbS quantum dot solar cells as the bottom cell (1 eV) in Fig. 17c. Zhang et al. also introduced PbS colloidal quantum dots (Fig. 17d and e) to extend the response range of PSCs and simultaneously deliver excellent device performance with an optimal PCE of 11.03% (Fig. 17f and g).412 Besides, the introduction of a PbS subcell can effectively eliminate the hysteresis and result in high stability. Similarly, Tavakoli et al. reported a highly efficient and stable 2T and 4T perovskite/PbS tandem solar cell with stabilized PCEs of 17.1% and 21.1%, respectively, by CdCl2 surface passivation.413 Very recently, our group developed a novel Cu2CdZn1−xSnS4 to complement the absorption of the typical MAPbI3 based PSCs.414 The IPCE spectra (750–900 nm) of various PSCs revealed that the Cu2CdZn1−xSnS4 layer could broaden the IPCE response to 900 nm, with a remarkably increased band around 830 nm. Furthermore, the well-matched bandgap alignment between Cu2CdZn1−xSnS4 and MAPbI3 can extract holes from the perovskite film, demonstrating a remarkably increased PCE from 19.30% to 21.11% and suggesting that a perovskite/inorganic-integrated structure is a promising strategy to broaden and boost the NIR response of the PSCs.
 Fig. 17  (a) AM 1.5G solar spectrum shows the limit of photocurrent generation by a typical PSC with a 1.55 eV bandgap and the lower-energy photons that could be captured by low-bandgap quantum dots (Eg ≥ 0.65 eV). (b) Layer-by-layer device architecture of a monolithic perovskite/quantum dots tandem solar cell. (c) J–V curves and projected tandem cell performance based on the state-of-the-art experimental results. Reprinted with permission from ref. 406, copyright 2018, American Chemistry Society; (d) device structure of PbS quantum dots/perovskite tandem solar cells, (e) cross-sectional SEM images of tandem devices, (f) EQE spectrum of subcells and tandem solar cells. (g) J–V characteristics of single-junction solar cells and PbS quantum dots/perovskite tandem solar. Reprinted with permission from ref. 412, copyright 2018, Royal Society of Chemistry; (h) chemical structure of DX3, (i) device structure of the hybridized cell using a dichroic spectrum splitter, (j) IPCE, and integrated current spectra of DSSCs made of DX3 and PSC. Reprinted with permission from ref. 416, copyright 2015, Springer Nature. 
Breaking the traditional tandem structure design in a straight direction, the spectral splitting system constructed using dichroic mirrors at an angle of 45° (Fig. 17h) proved to be a new type of light complementary structure. This is not a typical tandem solar cell with compositional grading within one material, as in the case of PSCs.415 Kinoshita et al. employed panchromatic ruthenium complexes-DX3 (Fig. 17i) in a PSC/DSSC tandem solar cell.416 The DX3-sensitized solar cell could exhibit wide-ranging light absorption to 1100 nm with high JSC exceeding 30 mA cm−2. As a result, the hybridized mesoscopic photovoltaic could achieve an optimized PCE of 21.5% (Fig. 17j), which is almost twice as much as that of individual DSSC, and also higher than that of PSCs. This spectral splitting system structure does not need to consider the transmissivity of the two subcells, but pay attention to the spectral response of different subcells. More importantly, it is applicable to almost all combinations of photovoltaic devices.
Above all, the recent studies reveal that the tandem solar cells of perovskite/perovskite, perovskite/organic, perovskite/quantum dots, and perovskite/dye are promising strategies to extend and enhance sunlight utilization for wide bandgap or low bandgap based single-junction PSCs. These published studies provided a critical starting point for future advances in solution-processed perovskite tandem solar cells with complementary absorption. Currently, the PCE of perovskite-based tandem solar cells has been dramatically improved. Compared with the widely studied perovskite/Si tandem solar cells at this stage, the advantages are that these tandem solar cells borrow the characteristics of flexibility, tunability, and low-cost solution processing of perovskite and organic materials. However, what should be particularly mentioned is that the absorption coefficient of the commonly used organic materials, quantum dots, and dye is much smaller than that of the perovskite. Researchers need to weigh specific complementary absorbers and their optimized thickness (Table 3).
