5. Surface plasmon resonance for light-harvesting
5.1 Basic mechanisms in surface plasmon resonance
Plasmonic nanostructures may be particularly applicable for PSCs, as they can be fabricated using solution processing methods with wide photoactive layer thickness adjustment properties.417,418 Multiple reports have used simulations to assess the viability of using plasmonic light-trapping schemes for PSCs.142,419,420 Before concluding this section about the effect of plasmonic nanostructures on the performance of PSCs, we first look back on the theoretical fundamentals of SPR and primary enhancement mechanisms in solar cells. As displayed in Fig. 18a, the most representative examples of LSPRs are metal nanoparticles. Accordingly, metal nanoparticles are strong light scatterers at wavelengths near the plasmon resonance due to a collective oscillation of the conduction electrons. The particle plasmons are excited when the frequency of the incident photons matches the resonance frequency of the nanoparticles. From the quasistatic approximation, the polarizability (α) of a spherical particle can be expressed as: (1)where a is the diameter of the nanoparticles, εp is the dielectric function of the nanoparticle, and εm is the dielectric function of the embedding medium, respectively. We can conclude that the value of particle polarizability α reaches its maximum when εm = −2εp, then producing a resonance condition. Because the excited plasmons are localized and cannot propagate within the nanostructure, this process is known as LSPR. For the particles with diameters below the wavelength of light, that is a ≪ λ, the point dipole model can accurately and vividly describe the absorption and scattering of light. The scattering and absorption cross-sections are given by:421 (2) (3)where k (k = 2π/λ) is the wavenumber of the light, λ is the wavelength of the incident photon, and V is the particle volume. Furthermore, the scattering efficiency (Qscat) can be defined as: (4)Three main basic mechanisms have been proposed to explain device performance enhancement by SPRs, and the schemes are summarized as a function of time in Fig. 18b. In particular, the SPR process includes (1) far-field light scattering (Fig. 18c), (2) near-field enhancement (Fig. 18d), and (3) plasmon-induced carrier separation (Fig. 18e).143 The far-field light scattering and near-field enhancement are induced until the LSPRs are dephased (several to 20 fs). Plasmon-induced carrier separation is a relatively long process compared to light scattering and near-field enhancement because of the subsequent electron–hole pair generation process.37 These effects have been widely used for increased absorption in the optical absorber layer of PSCs. To make these enhancements effective, the contribution of each mechanism depended mostly on the nature, size, shape, localization of the nanostructures and the dielectric parameters of the surrounding environment in the devices should be strictly controlled.137,147,148,421
 Fig. 18  (a) LSPR confined in metal spheres. The metal spheres are excited by the electric field of the incident light of a wavelength in metal spherical. (b) Different time domains in which LSPRs and each mechanism in a plasmonic solar cell. Reprinted with permission from ref. 426, copyright 2018, Royal Society of Chemistry; (c)–(e) show the schematic illustration of the influence of the size of a nanostructure on the corresponding plasmonic enhancement mechanism. (c) A light scattering of a ∼120 nm Au nanoparticle. (e) The near-field plasmonic effect from a ∼45 nm Au nanoparticle, (d) the photo-charging effect from a ∼5 nm Au nanoparticle, referenced from the result of Rizia Bardhan et al.,37 and we redraw it. (f)–(h) Structure designs of plasmonic-enhanced PSCs based on (c)–(e). (f) Nanostructures act as scattering centers, and the incident photons are scattered mostly into the material having a higher dielectric constant at the thin film surface. (g) Embedded nanoparticles are positioned in perovskite to enhance the near-field in PSCs. (h) Electrical effects from SPRs. 

5.1.1 Far-field scattering effect. 
As mentioned above, the SPR process includes three aspects. For the first far-field scattering effect (Fig. 18f), Erwin et al. have calculated absorption and scattering cross-sections of spherical Au nanoparticles to demonstrate the size effect.37 Their result revealed that the amount of light scattered from nanoparticles monotonically increases with size while the amount of light absorption decreases. At the crossover size of ∼90 nm for spherical Au nanoparticles, the amount of light absorbed and scattered is equivalent, indicating that the nanoparticle size can be tuned to achieve desired photoactive and scattering properties for maximum light-harvesting. The red-shift and broadening of the resonance with increased particle size would be advantageous to photovoltaics applications.422 Since σscat scales with a6, metal nanoparticles with sizes larger than 30 nm are ideal for coupling far-field scattered light into the perovskite photoactive layer.423 Beyond the size of metal nanostructures, the shape also controls the far-field scattered light attributed to the enhanced polarizability of incoming light.424–426 The effect of polarizability is particularly dominant for non-spherical nanostructures with sharp edges, corner including cubes and layered concentric nanostructures, such as pyramids and octahedrons.148,427,428 The disk-like shaped metal structure that have a large fraction of their volume can lead to a high fraction of light scattered into the substrate.148,427–429 Furthermore, far-field scattering characteristics are affected by the composition of metal nanostructures, where Ag has more robust radiative properties due to less ohmic losses than Au. The material characteristics can also affect the resonance frequency. For example, for a particle size corresponding to the same deposited mass thickness of Au and Ag, Au nanoparticles have a lower frequency resonance than Ag nanoparticles.430 The resonance can also be adjusted by using nanoshells that have a dielectric as the core and metal as the shell.431 The red-shifting of resonance at longer wavelengths would be beneficial for increasing the response of solar cells. Finally, far-field effects in PSCs may be tuned as a function of the permittivity of the medium (εm) surrounding the metal nanostructures. A higher εm could red-shift the LSPR frequency, enabling the capture of photons where the perovskite photoactive layer poorly absorbs.37,432 The resonance frequency also gets red-shifted when the dielectric function of the surrounding medium increases. For example, overcoating the metal particles on a substrate can change the resonance.147,433,434 From a theoretical point of view, Liang et al. introduced a SiO2 nanophotonic structure in PSCs by electromagnetic theory to increase the light absorption.435 The geometrical parameters of SiO2 nanophotonic structure were optimized by an FDTD method combined with the particle swarm optimization algorithm to obtain the light absorption enhancement. With the introduction of SiO2, a 200 nm perovskite layer-based PSC can generate the common photocurrent as the 300 nm perovskite layer-based planar PSC, which has the ability to reduce the toxic lead elements in PSCs.


5.1.2 Near-field enhancement. 
The interaction between the plasmonic nanostructures and incident light will cause the electromagnetic field in the vicinity of the nanostructures to increase locally, which is usually called the “near field” (Fig. 18g).426,436 Metal nanostructures integrated into PSCs act as concentrators to focus the incident light on the metal surface in a small mode volume.437 The intensity of the gathered light field is several orders of magnitude higher than that of the incident light. Therefore, we can compare the nanostructure inside of the device structure to the incident, so as to increase the photon flux. The perovskite photoactive layer near the nanostructure will be coupled with the strong near-field, and the resulting plasmon molecular coupling will increase the generation of electron–hole pairs. Then, higher density carriers can be used to transfer from the conduction band of the photosensitive material to the conduction band of ETL, thus improving the overall photocurrent density of PSCs. The FDTD simulation reveals that the morphology and composition of nanostructures affect the local electromagnetic field intensity during plasmon resonance.438,439 Due to the different dielectric functions of metal materials, the near-field effect is also different for Ag relative to Au. Generally, light scattering and near-field enhancement belong to radiation effects. In this process, the metal nanostructures with the LSPR effect will produce scattering effects and then re-radiate the light into the perovskite photoactive layer, or the metal nanostructure will be used as an auxiliary light source to enhance the local electric field. These effects can be useful for increasing the absorption of PSCs. To make these enhancements useful, it is crucial to choose the nature, size, shape, and localization of the nanostructures properly applied in PSCs.83


5.1.3 Plasmon resonant energy transfer. 
In addition to the radiation effects above-mentioned, plasmonic nanostructures can also positively affect the photoelectric properties of PSCs through non-radiation effects. The plasma can transfer its energy non-radiatively to the surrounding medium, which is the so-called “plasma resonance energy transfer” process (Fig. 18h). Moreover, after the femtosecond plasmons decay into electron–hole pairs, the so-called “hot electrons” can be extracted into the surrounding semiconductor medium. Plasma-induced carrier separation is part of LSPR, and energy is then transferred to nearby semiconductors, thereby enhancing the photocurrent.83 In the plasma non-radiation process, the metal nanostructure essentially acts as the direct photoactive materials.37
In addition to the metal nanoparticles, employing periodic corrugated structures, such as gratings, at the interface will boost the light-trapping effect, which is the most common approach used in organic solar cells and Si-based solar cells. In the next section, we will discuss some typical periodic nanostructures that have successfully improved the performance of PSCs in detail. In this section, we will focus on the plasmonic effect of nanoparticles to enhance light absorption in the PSCs devices. Based on our survey of the literature with regard to plasmonic PSCs, there are many device configurations in which metallic nanostructured electrodes can be placed, such as within the HTL, ETL, or is blended in the perovskite photoactive layer. In these configurations, each mechanism has different preparation requirements for devices, and the absorption efficiency, spectral response range and the PCE of PSCs can be improved with different control strategies.440 Plasmonic nanostructures with different nature, size, shape, and concentration have been incorporated into the different layers of PSCs, we will give a detailed summary in the following sections. In order to distinguish different kinds of SPR materials, we take the type of materials as the basis, rather than the position in the device structure, to summarize the SPR effect of different materials on PSCs. We will focus on the SPR effect of metal particles and dielectric particles to improve the light utilization of PSCs, including single metal nanoparticles, metal alloys, nanoparticles combined with dielectric composite structure, and some typical dielectric nanoparticles.

5.2 Metals nanostructures
Metal nanoparticles with subwavelength dimensions can couple incident photons with conduction electrons and give rise to LSPRs.441–443 Ag, Au, Cu, and Au–Ag alloy nanoparticles or nanostructures with different sizes and morphology could exhibit an LSPR and have been the most commonly used in PSCs to enhance the device performance.444–455 Xu et al. demonstrate the electron transfer between Au nanoparticles and perovskite MAPbI3 at a single nanoparticle level by plasmon resonance Rayleigh scattering spectroscopy.456 The most typical and widely used is that embedding plasmonic materials into the ETL of PSCs.457 Zhang et al. incorporated Au nanoparticles into m-TiO2 films and demonstrated a 34.2% improvement in PCE than that of control PSCs, which is associated with effective photon management as a result of the Au nanoparticles.458 Very recently, Cui et al. report on the scrutiny of carrier dynamics of PSC via sandwiching monodisperse TiO2/Au nanoparticles/TiO2 based plasmonic/dielectric core/shell nanoparticles in the ETL of PSCs.442 Then, they obtained a modified PSC with enhanced JSC and VOC, achieving maximum PCEs of 18.81% and 19.42% in planar and mesostructured PSCs, respectively.
Ag is a promising alternative for plasmonic applications because of its high-quality factor over a broad spectral range, outperforming most metals. Moreover, Ag nanoparticles synthesized through wet chemical steps have the advantages of low cost and large-scale preparation. Many possible shapes and sizes are available, such as triangles, cubes, stars, and rods, due to self-assembly characteristics. The obtained Ag nanostructures can be deposited on the substrate quickly so that the optical properties can be thoroughly studied and utilized.420 As is the schematic display in Fig. 19a, Nourolahi et al. embedded Ag nanoparticles with plasmonic effects at 427 nm into a c-TiO2 layer.459 The calculated normalized electric field intensity for a single Ag nanosphere normal to the incident illumination is exhibited in Fig. 19b. The PCEs of Ag nanoparticle modified PSCs can be improved from 4.57 to 6.15%, which is attributed to the enhanced light-harvesting by plasmonic nanoparticles and decreased electron-transporting lifetime. Another work revealed that the absorption intensity and PCE of PSCs decreased with the increased size of Ag nanoparticles, which is related to the LSPR effect on the absorbance of PSCs.460 The enhanced PCE can be mainly ascribed to the accelerated carrier transfer and enhanced light absorption in the visible region. Hao et al. embedded Ag nanoparticles in TiO2 and show a PCE of 12.23%.461 Further introducing a textured FTO substrate, the modified PSCs could achieve a PCE of 14.01%. They attributed the enhanced PCE to the extended optical pathway in the photoactive layer caused by the textured FTO and the incorporated Ag nanoparticles. In addition to the ETL, the metal nanoparticles with SPR can also be incorporated in HTL for PSCs applications. Ghosh et al. studied plasmonic Ag nanoparticles in PEDOT:PSS-based inverted PSCs with enhanced PCE of 5.58%.462 Similarly, Liu et al. report the incorporated 25/40 nm Ag nanoparticles on transparent polyimide (Fig. 19c) to promote light-harvesting in flexible PSCs with an enhanced PCE of 10.41%.140 In particular, a 61% improvement existed in the JSC compared to that based on the control polyimide substrates. Above all, we can conclude that plasmonic-electrical effects play a significant role in averaged performance improvement.

Introducing the LSPR of metal nanoparticles can also improve the light-harvesting efficiency of thinner PSCs. Reducing the thickness of the perovskite absorber layer while improving light-harvesting efficiency can not only increase the PCE of PSCs but also improve the transparency of the semi-transparent PSCs and increases the mechanical stability of the flexible PSCs. Our group revealed that embedding Au nanostars with SnO2 surface modification by low-temperature electron beam method into the ultra-thin PSCs with 250 nm perovskite layer could exhibit an improved PCE from 15.34 to 18.50%, while the PSCs with 400 nm perovskite layer could exhibit a PCE of 20.06%.450 We also obtained flexible, large area and semi-transparent PSCs based on Au nanostar modification. The flexible PSCs exhibited a high PCE of 15.33% and more than 90% of their initial PCE values after 100 bending cycles. Liu et al. incorporated plasmonic 11 nm Ag nanoparticles into ultra-thin PSCs (Fig. 19d) through the spray chemical vapor deposition method, as is illustrated from the SEM images in Fig. 19e.463 Their results revealed that Ag nanoparticles could lead to an enhanced JSC and PCE by 22.2% and 22.5%, respectively. Kim et al. developed planar PSCs with plasmonic Ag nanocubes coupled electromagnetically with an Ag back electrode (Fig. 19f).464 The far-field scattering and near optical field from the nanocubes face closest to the perovskite layer are also enhanced extensively by the coupling (Fig. 19g), with an enhanced JSC and PCE (Fig. 19h). More importantly, the absorption enhancements were more remarkable when applying a thinner perovskite layer. Recently, Carretero-Palacios et al. also assessed the possibility of using various plasmonic nanostructures to minimize the thickness of perovskite film.465–467 Hsu et al. systematically investigated the plasmonic effects of Ag nanoplates embedded in PSCs by varying the sizes and shapes (Fig. 19i).468 After embedding Ag nanoplates into the PEDOT:PSS, the increased EQE proved the increased plasmonic scattering effect. Moreover, Lee et al. revealed that embedding 20 nm Au nanoparticles into the Spiro-OMeTAD would simultaneously produce positive and negative effects on the device performance.469 On the one hand, Au nanoparticles produce a near-field optical absorption increase and a reduced resistance of Spiro-OMeTAD for increasing EQE and JSC.469 On the other hand, the Au nanoparticles would locate near the perovskite film to slightly decrease the VOC of PSCs. Above all, these studies revealed that the scattering effect from the incorporated Ag nanoparticles in HTL or substrate could extend the optical pathway of incident light and increase the photon absorption in PSCs. Meanwhile, many studies have emphasized the electrical properties of a metal material and proved the significant inhibition of carrier recombination and promotion for carrier transfer.
Although most of the work also emphasizes the scattering effect and electrical properties of metal nanoparticles, there are still some studies about Ag nanoparticles playing a negative role as trap sites for the free carrier. For example, Yang et al. systematically investigated the effect of Ag nanoparticles (Fig. 19j) on the PCE by integrating the nanoparticles into the TiO2 photoanode layer.470 They found that the plasmonic effect of Ag nanoparticles could enhance the incident light absorption over a wide range. Nevertheless, they also revealed that the low conduction energy level of the Ag nanoparticles could provide some additional trap sites for free carriers (Fig. 19k). This result draws attention to the balance between increased absorbance by the LSPR and the decreased carrier trapping as well as the decreased surface roughness of the TiO2 layer with the Ag nanoparticles (Fig. 19l). Inconsistent with other previous results, some research studies did not focus on the scattering effect of Ag nanoparticles but the influence of electrical properties on device performance. Abate et al. integrated Ag nanoparticles between two c-TiO2 layers and proved that the incorporated Ag nanoparticles in the ETL of the planar PSC device could facilitate the electron–hole separation and promote carrier extraction from the perovskite to the ETL.471 Higgins et al. demonstrate the small amounts of Ag nanoparticles modified PCBM could improve the JSC, VOC, and FF of PSCs for better coverage and improved carrier collection.472 The larger VOC for PSCs with a low concentration of Ag nanoparticles are attributed to the favorable alignment of LUMO levels between MAPbI3 with either Ag nanoparticles or PCBM layer. The author suggests that the enhanced light scattering is negligible due to the small Ag nanoparticle volume and concentrations employed from plasmonic simulations.
In addition to the traditional 0-dimension metal nanoparticles in space, metal nanostructures with different aspect ratios or shapes have multiple and strong plasmonic absorption properties from the visible to the NIR, exhibiting high plasmonic-induced probability.37,473,474 Also, generated “hot spots” around metal nanostructures could provide more robust EM field enhancements than conventional 0-dimension metal nanoparticles, hence improving light-harvesting and interfacial carrier dynamic processes simultaneously. Making the plasmonic nanostructures aspherical could shift their LSPR from wavelengths below 600 nm to the NIR region or achieve a distribution of plasmonic modes across the visible. Such morphology control strategies are highly essential to enhance the optical absorption in the NIR region where the perovskite material has a low response.475 Based on the above light-harvesting mechanism, Batmunkh et al. revealed that integrating the plasmonic Au nanostars (Fig. 20a) in m-TiO2 could exhibit a definite improvement in the EQE value from around 440 to 750 nm, with an enhanced PCE of 17.72% than that of 15.19% for control devices.476 They attributed the increased device performance to enhanced light absorption and suppressed carrier recombination. Ghahremanirad et al. found that the 60 nm Au nanoparticles could help to increase the absorption by 20%.477 Cai et al. demonstrated significant LSPR coupling effects in the self-organized Au nanostructure/MAPbI3−xClx heterostructures (Fig. 20b) using a thermal deposition and annealing process.478 They observed enhanced PL emission and strange PL spectrum splitting (Fig. 20c), which can be attributed to the hot electron energy transfer effect induced by the LSPR coupling (Fig. 20d). From the phenomenology model, the harvested energy can be extracted into the Au nanostructure. This energy transfer process is much faster than the diffusion and relaxation process and provides a new method to increase device performance for all photovoltaic applications. Recently, Ma et al. exploited a bioinspired nanostructure of Au nanorod-nanoparticle dimers (Fig. 20e) with structural darkness to enhance the light-harvesting and performance of PSCs.479 The Au dimer could exhibit a broadband optical absorption with two broad peaks located at 560 nm and 912 nm for the plasmonic resonance480 and the scattering effect (Fig. 20f).481 Ginting et al. incorporated 35 nm Au nanostars with distributed uniformity (Fig. 20g) into the PSCs to enhance the light absorption.176 Further modification with mPEG–thiol, the Au nanostars exhibited a pronounced SPR peak at 630 nm (Fig. 20h). From the simulation, the electric field distribution of Au nanostars positioned at the perovskite/Spiro-OMeTAD interface exhibited much stronger at specific excitation wavelengths of 740 nm (Fig. 20i), which will prolong the optical pathway and provide more photogeneration in perovskite. Differing from conventional metallic nanoparticles, biometric nanoparticles and Au nanostars would produce a geometric singularity and provide a broadband response for light harvesting. The plasmonic contributions and accelerated carrier transfer are believed to improve the JSC, VOC, and FF values. Xu et al. observed more than six-fold enhancement of luminescence from MAPbI3 films by coupling MAPbI3 and Au nanoparticles.482

(c) PL spectra of a bare perovskite film, an Au nanostructure/perovskite sample, and a 150 °C annealed Au nanostructure/perovskite heterostructure sample. Currently, the enhanced photovoltaic performance of PSCs by plasmonic strategies is limited to the short wavelength range corresponding to the extinction wavelength of metal nanoparticles. Thus, developing novel plasmonic nanostructures with predesigned sizes and shapes is urgent. Dong et al. explored the “bipyramid-like” Au nanobipyramid structures (Fig. 21a) with sharp tips and applied them in the HTL of PSCs.483 Considering the electrical property, hot hole injection induced by plasmonic Au nanobipyramids could fill in the interfacial traps and then boost the improvement of the VOC, offering great potential to expand the performance limitation and operation tolerance of the PSCs. Shalan et al. demonstrated that the Au nanoislands (Fig. 21b) on the 10 nm-thick NiO film could increase the PCE of PSCs to 5.1%, which is double that of control devices.484Fig. 21c shows the SEM image of the Au nanoislands and their corresponding model for FDTD numerical simulations. One peak at 830 nm is off-resonance from the absorption of perovskite, while the other peak at 675 nm will help to enhance the EQE. The author verified that the enhanced EQE response at shorter wavelengths is derived from the plasmon electric potential effect. Metal nanorods could exhibit two spectrally separated LSPR bands due to coherent oscillations of conduction band electrons along transverse and longitudinal axes, and these LSPR bands, in particular, the longitudinal mode, can be effectively tuned by varying the aspect ratio.143 Guo et al. demonstrate that the Au nanorods (Fig. 21d) could offer strong light absorption over almost the whole visible-light range for PSC applications.485 It is found that the brightness of the PL mapping image (Fig. 21e and f) of Au nanorods on the ZnO film is much lower than that of the bare Au nanorods, indicating the effective carrier transfer from Au nanorods to the ZnO film. Thus, the plasmon-induced hot electrons could result in reduced carrier recombination for suppressed photocurrent hysteresis.

(c-3) The 1-T-R spectrum and the action spectrum of near-field intensity calculated by the FDTD simulations. Reprinted with permission from ref. 485, copyright 2018, Royal Society of Chemistry; (g) EQE spectra of PSCs modified with Au, Ag, and Al nanoparticles. Inset is the simulated field |E| enhancement distribution of Ge nanosphere embedded between TiO2 and MAPbI3 film. Reprinted with permission from ref. 488, copyright 2019, Elsevier. 
In addition to single metal nanoparticles, Roopak et al. analyzed the surface plasmon effect of various metal (Au, Ag, and Al) nanostructures embedded in PSCs for better light coupling by discrete dipole approximation to evaluate the extinction spectra and plasmon mode induced field intensity.486 Their results revealed that Al nano-oblates have the potentiality of broadband light-trapping into perovskite and give an insight into the improved photovoltaic performance of PSCs due to the plasmon-induced light coupling to the host medium near the band edge. From an experimental point of view, Kakavelakis et al. tried to integrate Au, Ag, and Al nanoparticles in the PEDOT:PSS and observed the highest increase in IPCE (Fig. 21g) for the Ag nanoparticle modified PSCs.487 Zhang et al. disclose that incorporation of an appropriate ratio of Ge nanoparticles onto the m-TiO2 can improve the crystallinity of the MAPbI3 and promote the interfacial properties of perovskite/m-TiO2.488 The space carrier limited current test (Fig. 21h) reveals that the perovskite layer exhibits increased electron mobility by over 5 times with an optimized PCE of 19.6%. Simulated results in Fig. 21i reveal that the Ge nanoparticles have more vital scattering properties than the conventional Au nanoparticles at the shorter wavelength. Above all, although the nanostructure with a smaller size than 100 nm remained low concentration for PSCs applications, the incorporation of the plasmonic nanostructures could efficiently improve the PCE of PSCs, with obviously increased JSC. However, in most cases, there is no clear correlation between the LSPR spectrum of the individual nanostructures and the optical absorption of PSCs. The enhanced PCE has thus been ascribed to the combination of enhanced EQE and improved photocarrier collection, which are both related to the presence of the plasmonic nanostructures.151,152
5.3 Metal@dielectric composites
Although exhibiting single or multiple LSPR due to the collective oscillation of free electrons driven by visible or NIR light, these noble metal nanoparticles can react with halogens in perovskite materials. Generally, a coating of dielectric composites on these metal nanoparticles is necessary to eliminate these negative chemical reaction processes. Besides, encapsulating the metal into a dielectric shell has been considered an effective method to avoid parasitic recombination of the photogenerated carriers at the interface of metal/perovskite.489–491 The dielectric materials for plasmonic metal nanoparticles usually include SiO2, TiO2, ZnO, NiO, PbS, etc.492–494 According to the previous calculations in Section 5.1, it can be concluded that the plasmonic resonant frequency strongly depends on the dielectric environment. The resonant frequency of plasmonic metal nanoparticles will redshift as εm increases. Thus, encapsulating the metal nanoparticles into a dielectric shell has been considered as an effective strategy for SPR enhancement in PSCs. Zhang et al. previously explored that incorporating Au@SiO2 core–shell nanoparticles into PSCs to enhance the radiative decay of excitons and increase the reabsorption of the emitted radiation.419 The 80 nm Au nanoparticles coated by an approximate 8 nm SiO2 shell could exhibit an absorption peak at ∼550 nm, as is shown in Fig. 22a. The slightly red-shifted extinction spectrum is expected from the change in the local refractive index, which slightly shifts to longer wavelength upon addition of the SiO2 shell. As a result, the Au@SiO2 embedded PSCs (Fig. 22b) exhibited enhanced JSC and PCE. Cui et al. reported that the Au/SiO2 core/shell nanospheres and nanorods could lead to an inverted MAPbI2.85Br0.15 based PSC (Fig. 22c) with an optimized PCE of 13.7%.495 Ma et al. revealed that incorporating Ag@SiO2 core–shell nanocubes with sharp corners into the PSCs could achieve an optimized PCE of 17.22%, ascribed to both the enhanced local electromagnetic field (Fig. 22d) and the inhibition of carrier recombination induced by the Ag@SiO2 nanocubes.213 Similarly, Wang et al. revealed that Ag@SiO2 modified PSCs could exhibit an increased PCE from 12.23% to 14.61%, originated from the enhanced JSC from 20.23 to 23.04 mA cm−2.496 Wang et al. designed core-ultrathin shell Ag@SiO2 nanowires (Fig. 22e) in PSCs to avoid pinholes between grains and improved surface coverage, achieving enhanced PCE of 18.03% with negligible photocurrent hysteresis.497 These concluded that the structural advantage and LSPR effect of Ag@SiO2 nanowires could improve light-harvesting, accelerate electron transmission, promote electron–hole separation, and suppress carrier–carrier recombination. Aeineh et al. enhanced the PCE of PSCs by depositing Au@SiO2 nanoparticles between the c-TiO2 and the m-TiO2, achieving a higher EQE response in the whole wavelength range.498

The light-harvesting efficiency of the MAPbI3−xBrx coated compact-NiO/m-Al2O3 layer with Au@SiO2 nanorods is also presented. Embedding the core–shell nanoparticles in the HTL will also promote photovoltaic performance.499 Chandrasekhar et al. improved the PCE using plasmonic Au@SiO2 core–shell nanoparticles between the PEDOT:PSS-based HTL and the perovskite layer.500 Tang et al. demonstrated that Au@SiO2 core–shell nanorods are beneficial for the enhanced light absorption and EQE across a broad range of wavelengths, which is related to the increased cross-sectional scattering and spectrally absorbing energy density.501 A quasi-static model is used to study the SPRs of Au@SiO2 core–shell nanospheres by varying shell thickness and core radius in the perovskite layer.502 Theoretical modeling indicates that a core@shell with a thickness of 20 nm@1 nm could provide enhanced absorption in the orange-red region of the electromagnetic spectrum. Then they demonstrated ∼20 nm-sized Au@SiO2 core–shell nanoparticles with plasmonic effects could help to achieve an improved PCE of over 18%. Usually, the large concentration of Au nanoparticles would cause worse device performance. The key feature of this approach is taking advantage of the SPR at a lower concentration. Zhang et al. found that the SPR of high aspect ratio Au nanorods@SiO2 integrated into perovskite/Spiro-OMeTAD interface could lead to an overall enhancement of light absorption and carrier separation, resulting in an optimized PCE of 17.39%.503 This work further supports that Au nanorods have an effective SPR effect. Similarly, Wu et al. prepared planar PSCs by employing Au@SiO2 nanorods at the interface between the PEDOT:PSS and MAPbI3.504 Both experiment and simulation results proved that the efficiency enhancement came from the LSPR of Au@SiO2 nanorods, which synchronously improved the incident light-trapping as well as improved the carrier transport and collection characteristics.
Embedded inside of the PSCs, the insulating property of SiO2 will adversely affect the carrier transport. Developing semiconductor materials with high electron mobility as the shell layer has far-reaching significance for improving the LSPR application of metal nanoparticles and enhancing the photovoltaic performance of PSCs. In 2015, Snaith and co-authors incorporated temperature-sensitive core–shell Ag@TiO2 nanoparticles (Fig. 22f) into PSCs to achieve a best PCE of 16.3%.420 Fan et al. reported that the Au@TiO2 nanorod modified PSCs could deliver an optimized PCE of 20.10%.505 The mechanism regarding the plasmonic enhancement effect indicates better carrier separation/transfer as well as facilitated carrier transport in the presence of plasmonic particles. The above works proved that the plasmonic enhancement mechanism of the Au@oxide composite is associated with improved exciton generation rate and enhanced exciton dissociation probability induced by the LSPR effect.506 In addition to the cladding of metal core–shell structure, Yu et al. designed a unique ETL using Ag nanoparticles and three-dimensional orchid-like TiO2 nanowires (OC-TiO2 NWs) to increase the carrier extraction efficiency of PSCs.507Fig. 22g represents the transition of the scaffold structure from a TiO2 nanoparticle-based scaffold to an OC-TiO2 NW-based one and finally to the case of SiO2@Ag@OC-TiO2 NWs. UV-visible absorption spectroscopy indicates the higher absorption of light from the introduction of the plasmonic nanoparticles. Consequently, the SiO2@Ag@OC-TiO2 NW modified PSCs exhibited a high PCE of 15.09%.
In addition to Au@oxide, Li et al. introduced 1.5% Ag to the nano-TiO2 composites into the compact layer of PSCs to strengthen the optical absorption in the visible light region and enhance the electron injection efficiency in the PSCs.508 Luo et al. reported a plasmonic PSC by integrating Au@TiO2 core–shell nanoparticles into porous TiO2 and perovskite photoactive layers.509 Yao et al. developed the metal–inorganic semiconductor–organic semiconductor nanostructure of Ag@TiO2@Pa nanoparticles (Fig. 22h) for PSCs applications to achieve an optimized PCE of 20.2% (Fig. 22i).510 The modification mechanism (Fig. 22j) revealed that electrons transfer from both the photoactive layer and plasmonic excitation to the Pa shell, upon which the electrons are then collected through the fullerene channel. They conclude that the Ag@TiO2@Pa nanoparticles can help to activate efficient plasmon–exciton coupling and eliminate the carrier accumulation, thus facilitating exciton dissociation and reducing the monomolecular recombination in PSCs. Hao et al. synthesized Au@PSS core–shell tetrahedra nanostructures by a seed-mediated growth method and incorporated them into PSCs to improve light absorption of the perovskite photoactive layer via the SPR effect.511 Their results indicate that both the use of a Au tetrahedra core and the ultrathin PSS shell is beneficial for generating a strong local field and thus inhibiting exciton quenching. Qin et al. proved that core–shell Au@CdS nanoparticles could trigger nucleation of the perovskite precursor for high-quality perovskite films with preferred orientation through producing the Au@CdS–PbI2 intermediate for PSC applications.493 Moreover, the light-induced holes could cross barriers at the perovskite/Spiro-OMeTAD interface with the help of the LSPR of Au@CdS, resulting in an enhanced PCE exceeding 20%.
5.4 Metal alloys
Compared to conventional single metal nanostructures with a narrow plasmon resonance band, the metal alloys could exhibit multiple broader and stronger plasmon resonances that can be tuned by adjusting structural dimensions to match the absorption band of the perovskite layer.512 Fu et al. used rationally-designed Au@Ag core–shell nanocuboids (Fig. 23a) with four LSPR peaks (Fig. 23b) as plasmonic inclusions to achieve panchromatic thin PSCs.513 The IPCE results (Fig. 23c) demonstrated that the inclusion of the plasmonic Au@Ag core–shell nanocuboids could be a promising strategy to increase the utilization of low-energy photons and enhance the light-harvesting of PSCs. Lu et al. improved the light absorption and electron transfer in PSCs by incorporating plasmonic Au–Ag alloy popcorn-shaped nanoparticles with broadband optical absorption enhancement around the band edge between 720 and 820 nm.475 Sun et al. applied AuAg@AuAg core–shell alloy nanocrystals (Fig. 23d) into the PEDOT:PSS layer for PSC applications with a champion PCE of 16.76% (Fig. 23e).514 Detailed characterization suggested that the significant increase of device performance is mainly attributed to better light-harvesting by the scattering effect of AuAg@AuAg core–shell alloy nanocrystals and better carrier transport of the modified PEDOT:PSS layer. Chen et al. utilized Cu–Ag alloy nanoparticles with light scattering capabilities and electrical conductivities to achieve PSCs with a remarkable PCE of 18.89%.515 Moreover, the alloy nanoparticle in PSCs could induce 80% and 200% improvements in long-term stability and photostability, respectively. The absorption spectra of AgAl (3 nm)/Au (2 nm) composite nanoparticles reveal an enhanced light absorption by the plasmon resonance and reduced contact barrier at the metal electrode (Fig. 23f).516
 Fig. 23  (a) Optical image of the Au@Ag core–shell nanocuboids. (b) Extinction spectra of the Au@Ag nanocuboids with varied thickness in aqueous solution. (c) Wavelength dependent IPCE and relative IPCE enhancement ratio of the PSCs in different configurations. Reprinted with permission from ref. 513, copyright 2017, Elsevier; (d) TEM image of Au/Ag alloy nanocrystals. (e) Cross-sectional SEM image of the device. Reprinted with permission from ref. 514, copyright 2018, Elsevier; (f) schematic energy levels of each layer in PSCs. Reprinted with permission from ref. 516, copyright 2018, Elsevier; (g) TEM image of the 5 nm SiO2 coated bimetallic nanostructures. (h) The normalized absorption curves of MAPbBr3 sensitized TiO2 films and the Au@Ag nanostructures. (i) Decay kinetics at 530 nm for the reference and Au@Ag nanostructures incorporated samples. Reprinted with permission from ref. 517, copyright 2017, Royal Society of Chemistry. (j) The deposition process for embedding metallic nanostructures in a TiO2 electron layer. (k) A schematic figure of the light trapped PSC via the light scattering effect. (l) The UV-vis curves were obtained for the different metallic nanostructures. Reprinted with permission from ref. 444, copyright 2017, Royal Society of Chemistry. 
Using the same strategy with dielectric coating, Zarick et al. incorporated SiO2 coated Au/Ag bimetallic nanostructures (Fig. 23g) with distinct plasmon resonances centered at 540 nm and 615 nm (Fig. 23h) into the m-TiO2 layer to enhance the PCE of PSCs.517 Lifetimes obtained from photobleaching (Fig. 23i) suggested that the nanostructures promoted efficient electron injection into TiO2. As a result, the Au@Ag nanostructure modified PSCs demonstrated improved light-harvesting and increased PCE by 26%. Deng et al. revealed that the introduction of Au@Ag@SiO2 core–shell nanocuboids could dramatically increase the JSC from 20.87 mA cm−2 to 23.76 mA cm−2, ascribed to the enhanced light absorption and carrier separation ability of Au@Ag@SiO2 core–shell nanocuboids added in perovskite film.518 Han et al. showed that the photovoltaic performance of the quasi-2D PSCs can be improved dramatically by introducing a layer of SiO2 coated Au/Ag-alloyed nanoprisms.519 To take full advantage of alloy materials, our group synthesized Au, Ag, and Au–Ag nanoalloy with different sizes and embedded them into TiO2 nanostructures (Fig. 23j and k) by the low-temperature radio frequency magnetron sputtering method for PSC applications.444Fig. 23l revealed that the absorption spectra of the different metallic nanostructures exhibited multiple broader and stronger plasmon resonances. Above all, comprehensive studies indicate that metal alloys and their combination with dielectric coating can further reduce the series resistance, enhance light absorption, and boost exciton dissociation at the interface.446
5.5 Other nanostructures
The all-dielectric approach in nano-photonics could also provide enhanced photoluminescence and light-harvesting in PSCs. Recently, Tiguntseva et al. showed that the direct laser deposition of Si nanoparticles on the perovskite film (Fig. 24a) is preferable for PL and absorption enhancement.520 They observed that the photoluminescence intensity was enhanced by 50% from a perovskite layer with Si nanoparticles and by 200% for a nanoimprinted metasurface with Si nanoparticles. Furasova et al. improve the performance of PSCs by the incorporation of resonant Si nanoparticles at an interface of MAPbI3 and m-TiO2 (Fig. 24b and c).521 They concluded that the increased JSC is originated from light absorption around Si nanoparticles (Fig. 24d), while the improved FF and the VOC related to improved crystallinity of the perovskite layer in the presence of Si nanoparticles. Aeineh et al. revealed that integrating SiO2 nanoparticles of 50 and 100 nm sizes into the m-TiO2 layer could affect the ion accumulation at the perovskite interface and provide an additional beneficial effect, leading to enhanced photocurrent and reduced J–V hysteresis.522 The increment is in agreement with the theoretical predictions based on light scattering induced by dielectric SiO2 nanoparticles. Hwang et al. also reported the use of size-controlled SiO2 nanoparticles with an optimized diameter of 50 nm as a scaffold layer in PSCs, as shown in Fig. 24e.523 It appears that the pore size formed between the SiO2 nanoparticles with diameters of 15 and 30 nm was too small for the perovskite to infiltrate into the scaffold layer. The optimized PSCs based on 50 nm diameter SiO2 nanoparticles could exhibit a PCE of 11.45%, higher than that of 10.29% for control PSCs (Fig. 24f). Yin et al. utilized submicron TiO2 to improve light absorption by optimizing the proportion of submicron scattering TiO2 in the mesoporous layer.524 With the help of investigating the corresponding light-trapping mechanisms through the absorption spectra and FDTD simulation, they obtained an improved PCE by over 5% through compromising the carrier transportation loss and scattering-based light absorption enhancement. Mali et al. demonstrated that Au decorated TiO2 plasmonic nanofiber-based PSCs could achieve a PCE of 14.92%, which is significantly higher than conventional m-TiO2 as well as TiO2 nanofiber based PSCs. The time-resolved photoluminescence spectroscopy and solid-state impedance spectroscopy results revealed that the Au decorated TiO2 nanofiber-based PSCs exhibited a lower carrier recombination rate. They predicted that the resonant Si nanoparticles could be applied not only in PSCs but also in other optoelectronic devices, owing to the overall enhancement of light localization and optical field induced by Si nanoparticles. These works elucidate the subtle interactions between light and matter in plasmonic photovoltaic composites.
 Fig. 24  (a) Colored SEM image of a perovskite film with Si nanoparticles on the surface. Reprinted with permission from ref. 520, copyright 2017, Royal Society of Chemistry; (b) band alignment and (c) cross-sectional bright-field STEM image of a complete PSC. Reprinted with permission from ref. 521, copyright 2018, Wiley-VCH; (d) experimental extinction (1-T) spectrum of the PSCs with and without Si nanoparticles. (e) UV-vis spectra of the perovskite layer containing various diameter SiO2 nanoparticles, inset is the magnified cross-sectional SEM images of scaffold layers containing SiO2 nanoparticles. (f) J–V characteristics of the PSCs with scaffold layers formed of TiO2 and SiO2 nanoparticles. Reprinted with permission from ref. 523, copyright 2014, Royal Society of Chemistry. 
In summary, the influence of the SPR effect of metal and its composite nanoparticles on the performance of PSCs has been explored from both experimental and theoretical perspectives. The above studies illustrate the complex and delicate interactions between light and matter in plasmonic PSCs. These results clearly show that the effect of nanoparticles with different morphology and concentration on the performance of the device is not the same, but the mechanism is to use the plasma resonance effect to improve the light absorption of the perovskite layer in different wavelengths and promote the exciton dissociation and carrier transfer, so as to improve the performance of the device.439,525 The incorporation of plasmonic nanostructures can affect not only the light absorption but also impact positively or negatively the electrical properties of PSCs. Despite that, there is still no clear correlation between the LSPR effect of the incorporated nanostructures and optical absorption in the perovskite layer in most cases. The improved PCE has thus been ascribed to the combination of enhanced EQE response and better carrier collection, which are both related to the presence of the plasmonic nanostructures. Based on the systematic and hierarchical analysis, we believe that there is more space for increasing the light absorption and efficiency of PSCs by a series of LSPR controlled by the metal and its alloy, semiconductor material, structure, and nanoparticle geometry, which is mainly due to the unique advantages, such as ultra-thin, device structure diversity and wide accommodation of dielectric and metal materials in the device structure (Table 4).

6. Texturing of the interior for light-trapping
Although preparing nanoparticles for LSPR applications has been applied as a practical modification effect for reducing energy loss, it is hard and challenging to control the sizes, shapes, and monodispersity with excellent distribution in the PSCs. By tuning the geometrical factors of periodic microstructures or nanostructures, the resonance wavelength of the surface plasmon polariton modes can be tuned readily within the visible to the NIR spectral range.530–534 These periodic structures are also easier to model, facilitating researchers to study from the point of view of the simulation.535,536 That is to say, one promising strategy for enhancing the performance of PSCs is to use periodic nanostructures, also called texturing, which can usually fulfill the momentum mismatch conditions and can interact with light in unusual ways that can serve as a new platform for improving the light-trapping and current density of PSCs.83,426,537,538 Additionally, some typical textured substrates, for example, the textured c-Si substrate, not only enhance the light absorption of the device but also induce the growth of perovskite films with large crystal grain size, which are beneficial for the efficiency improvement.12,426,539 In 1974, Redfield reported that normal incident light could be reflected from an angled back reflector and then undergo a total internal reflection at the surface to excite the waveguide mode.540 The light trapping could be achieved by the micro texturing of the front surface of the waveguide layer.
From a theoretical point of view, Haque et al. introduced TiO2 semispheroidal geometries with honeycomb periodicity in PSCs.541 The geometry of the photonic structures is a hexagonal array of the semi-spheroidal dome (Fig. 25a) or void (Fig. 25b) elements. Their results indicate that the optimized void structure could enable a PSC with a perovskite thickness of 250 nm to supply a JSC of 28.62 mA cm−2, which is 10.3% higher than that of 25.95 mA cm−2 for conventional PSCs with a perovskite thickness of 500 nm (Fig. 25c–f). When patterned on the front side of PSCs, the wave-optical micro-features yield up to 21% and 27% photocurrent enhancement in PSCs with perovskite thickness of 500 nm and 250 nm, respectively, which are close to those predicted results by Lambertian limits. Also, such features show the potential of the encapsulation role in preventing UV-induced degradation. Recently, Tockhorn et al. investigated the effect of different shallow nanotextures on the optical and electrical properties of PSCs from the perspective of experiment and theory simulation.542 They established two hexagonal sinusoidal nanotextures with a period of 750 nm, which have a negative (cos−) and a positive (cos+) directed cosine lattice and feature heights of 220 and 380 nm, respectively. They also fabricated an inverted pyramidal structure arranged in a square lattice with a period of 1250 nm and a structure depth of about 600 nm and hexagonal pillar-like nanotextures with a structural period of 750 nm and a feature height of around 350 nm (Fig. 25g). They revealed that the EQE-integrated photocurrent values of the planar device (22.1 mA cm−2) could be increased by 0.6–1.0 mA cm−2 by different nanotexture modification. Specifically, the devices with sinusoidal nanotextures reached 23.1 mA cm−2 (cos−) and 22.9 mA cm−2 (cos+), while the inverted pyramids reach 23.1 mA cm−2 and the pillar-like nanotextures reach 22.7 mA cm−2. The simulated optical absorption and reflectance spectra they conducted for the planar and cos− layer stacks are exhibited in Fig. 25h and i, proving an excellent qualitative agreement between the simulated and measured data. For the planar solar cells (Fig. 25h), the measurements show less intense interference fringes than the simulations, which could be related to the roughness at the back of the perovskite layer. For the cos− based nanotexture (Fig. 25i), they observed a qualitative agreement between simulation and experiments. The reflection losses are reduced both for experiments and simulations over a wide range of wavelengths from 300 to 730 nm. The reflection remains low and almost constant, from 400 to 730 nm. Due to these low reflection losses, a high absorption level from 400 to 730 nm can be reached in the perovskite films. The summarized current densities for all PSCs (Fig. 25j) and the simulated parasitic absorption and reflection losses prove that the JSC can increase for all nanotextures. In this section, we introduced various fabrication methodologies of periodic nanostructures from the points of anti-reflection and scattering properties in the interior of the PSC for reducing the energy loss and achieving sufficient light-trapping. In particular, the “interior” indicates the internal part of PSCs, usually including the transparent conductive substrate, the ETL, the perovskite photoactive layer, the HTL, and the electrode. It is contrary to the exterior of PSCs, which is usually placed on the outside of the device.
 Fig. 25  Sketch of both types of light-trapping structures. The parameters including R, RZ, p, tTCO and tETM for the structure (a) and the structure (b) for optimization are indicated by the arrows. (c and d) Absorption spectra attained with the two kinds of optimized light-trapping structures, composed of half-spheroidal domes (green lines), and with the reference cases of a cell with an optimized anti-reflection coating (blue lines), for perovskite absorber layers with distinct thickness: (c) 250 nm and (d) 500 nm. (e and f) Absorption spectra attained with the optimized light-trapping structures (green lines), and with the planar reference anti-reflection coating cases (blue lines), for perovskite layers with a thickness of (e) 250 nm and (f) 500 nm, respectively. Reprinted with permission from ref. 541, copyright 2019, Elsevier; (g) schematic figure of the PSCs with different nanotextured substrates. Measured 1-R and EQE spectra and simulated absorption and reflection profiles for planar substrate (h) and cos− nanotexture (i); (j) the bar diagram visualizes the integrated current densities in the different layers of the simulated layer stack for planar interfaces and the different nanotextures in their respective colors. Reprinted with permission from ref. 542, copyright 2020, American Chemistry Society. 
Texturing the glass or transparent conductive oxide substrates has been considered an effective strategy for changing the surface morphology to improve light trapping.543–546 Shi et al. used textured FTO substrates to enhance the efficiency of thin PSCs by 22%.547 Wang et al. constructed crater-like architecture substrates for ultra-thin PSCs that synergize the merits, including good broad-band, enhanced light-trapping, and excellent separation/collection of photo-generated carriers (Fig. 26a and b).548 The crater-like architecture-based sample with darker color indicates stronger absorption than the traditional sample (Fig. 26c). In particular, they also performed the angles of incidence-dependent maximum power density measurement from −90° to 90° to express the light-harvesting capability of the crater-like architecture (Fig. 26d and e). Compared to the PSCs with a planar surface, crater-like architecture based PSCs could exhibit an increased Pmax as the angles of incidence increases from 0° to ±75°, indicating the pronounced light-harvesting capability particularly at high angles of incidence (Fig. 26e), which is suitable for harvesting sunlight during a day without applying additional solar tracking systems. Kim et al. used a wet chemical etched FTO substrate to improve the photocurrent density by up to 24.4% for PSCs.549 Oh et al. incorporated hexagonal array micro-convex nanostructure-patterned glass substrates (Fig. 26f) with an intense light scattering in PSCs to increase the current density from 19.45 to 20.92 mA cm−2.550
 Fig. 26  (a) Schematic diagram of the structure of the PSCs. (b) The cross-section SEM of the PSCs. (c) The photo of the perovskite films grown on a planar substrate and cratered substrate, respectively. (d) Schematic of incident angle-dependent power generation over a day. (e) Incident angle dependence of generated maximum power of planar and cratered substrate-based PSCs (MAPbI3: ∼200 nm). Reprinted with permission from ref. 548, copyright 2019, Elsevier; (f) PSCs on HAMC-patterned glass. Reprinted with permission from ref. 550, copyright 2018, Elsevier; (g) schematic diagram of nanosphere lithography for 2D TiO2 nanodisk array and assembly of PSCs. (h) SEM images of 2D nanodisk array with p = 500 nm; the inset images are cross-sectional, tilted views of the samples. (the scale bar = 1 μm). (i) Electromagnetic energy density distributions of electromagnetic wave diffracted by the TiO2 layer with flat and 2D nanodisk array morphologies. Reprinted with permission from ref. 555, copyright 2019, Elsevier; (j) schematic illustrations of preparing silicon master and patterning m-TiO2 layer with conical shaped moth-eye structures. (k) Top-view and the corresponding cross-sectional SEM images of TiO2 nanobowl array. (l) SEM image of 3-D nanopatterned m-TiO2. Reprinted with permission from ref. 560, copyright 2016, Elsevier. 
The inorganic ETL with periodic nanostructures can also be integrated for better light-harvesting.551 Except for TiO2 nanoparticles for the mesoporous structured devices, TiO2 nanostructures with a 3D morphology also attract extensive attention.552,553 Usually, the conventional PSCs employ the compact/TiO2 nanoparticle composite layer as the ETL. However, the nanoparticles with small size could induce a poor light scattering ability, and the incident light almost passes through the ETL without changing the direction. Kim et al. report on the nanostructure and dynamics of electron transport characteristics in PSCs by incorporating oriented hemisphere TiO2 arrays with a size of 300 nm, 700 nm, 1100 nm, and 1400 nm.528 By adjusting the size of the polystyrene bead in the imprinting process, they obtained an improved PCE from 10.5 to 15.2%. Jun Hyuk Moon's group studied the effect of the inverse opal TiO2 structure on the photovoltaic performance of the PSCs.554 As a result, they found that a 600 nm thick inverse opal TiO2 structure could help to exhibit a maximum PCE of 17.1%. They also introduce TiO2 ETLs with a thin-film 2D photonic crystal structure in PSCs by using a polystyrene sphere monolayer as the physical mask during the ion etching to pattern the TiO2 scaffold (Fig. 26g and h).555 The field distribution indicates a higher confinement of light in the TiO2 photonic crystal nanodisk region and higher forward scattering (Fig. 26i). Furthermore, they find that the mixed cavities of sizes within 100 and 200 nm between FTO and mesoporous TiO2 enables complete absorption of short-wavelength photons (λ < 500 nm) to long-wavelength photons (500 nm < λ < 780 nm), leading to broadband absorption of short- and long-wave-length photons in visible light.556 The bio-inspired moth-eye nanostructure is expected to reduce the reflection in PSCs. Kang et al. proposed the nanopatterned m-TiO2 layers with 500 nm moth-eye pattern with well-ordered periodic 2D hexagonal symmetry for enhancing the light-harvesting by using soft nanoimprinting lithography and polydimethylsiloxane (PDMS) stamping methods in Fig. 26j.557 The results indicate that light absorption in the 2-D photonic-structured PSCs is more favorable for PSCs having a perovskite thickness of less than 300 nm. Chen et al. observed that the integrated absorption efficiency reaches 65.7% over the wavelength range from 350 to 800 nm at normal incidence in TiO2 nanobowl based PSCs with a 100 nm thick perovskite layer,558 indicating that the significantly enhanced light absorption in the long-wavelength region for the collective impact of the different photon and plasmonic modes and their mutual coupling. Zheng et al. proved that 220 nm TiO2 nanobowl (Fig. 26k) could enhance light absorption, improve pore-filling of perovskite crystals, and increase interface contact for rapid electron extraction in PSCs.559 Jang et al. illustrated that the incorporation of 3-D flower-shaped nanostructures into m-TiO2 ETLs (Fig. 26l) could lead to PSCs with a 10.3% improvement of average PCE owing to increased light-harvesting and carrier collection.560
In the texting structure applied in PSCs, zinc oxide (ZnO) is another typical n-type semiconductor due to its higher carrier mobility and electrical conductivity and capable electrons transporting the collecting effect. Significantly, it can be easily changed into a nanorod array by solution processing techniques such as chemical bath deposition. The researchers found that ZnO nanorod arrays have distinct light scattering characteristics for light-trapping in PSCs. For example, Amassian et al. reported hydrothermal growth of nitrogen-doped ZnO nanorods in hysteresis-free PSCs.561 Zheng et al. reported that a compact and even ZnO:I nanopillar could exhibit fast electron extraction and improved light-trapping effect.562 Tan and co-workers revealed that the optical reflection could be dramatically reduced by using a cascaded SnO2/TiO2–Cl as an ETL and an additional anti-reflective coating on the front glass side in PSCs, obtaining a record-high JSC of 26.1 mA cm−2.552 In the above, we can conclude that the microstructure of the ETLs can act as light scattering centers giving higher current density and reduced recombination effects.529 Considering the low cost, high thermal, and chemical stability of semiconductor oxide-based nanomaterials (TiO2, SnO2, ZnO, etc.), the textured approach paves the way toward an efficient and cheap method for improvement of PSCs.
Designing textured perovskite films rather than using planar-architecture can indeed optimize the photovoltaic performance of PSCs.563,564 Recently, researchers proved that textured perovskite film could exhibit enhanced light-trapping effect with the extended optical pathway from the perspective of experiment and theory simulation.11,12,461,565–571 For the textured perovskite, some are periodic, and some are randomly arranged. Researchers have developed a variety of methods to pattern the randomly arranged perovskite films.572–574 The earliest strategy is to use a laser to etch the substrate and then coat the substrate with a perovskite film.570,573,574 Another approach is to use a laser or focused ion beam to directly process the surface of the perovskite to prepare a perovskite film with a specific pattern or texture. In recent years, nanoimprint technology has been usually applied to texture the perovskite films.575 By heating and pressurizing, researchers can make the perovskite film come into close contact with a substrate with a specific pattern (such as PDMS), and in situ prepare a perovskite film with specific rules and shapes. PDMS, etc. can uniformly print its texture structure on the surface of the receptor (such as perovskite film) to produce a large-area array of specific microstructures.576 As for the periodic ones, Song's group constructed mimicking whispering-gallery structure on the perovskite photoactive layer for anti-reflection and light-harvesting in PSCs, achieving an improved PCE of 19.80%, which is 29.4% higher than that of 15.30% for control PSCs.81 The schematic fabrication process of imprinted PSCs and the morphology of whispering-gallery structured perovskite film by AFM are depicted in Fig. 27a and b. Brittman et al. imprinted nanophotonic patterns into halide perovskite films by using soft lithography for perovskite films. They revealed that the choice of solvent (DMF and DMSO) would strongly influence the resulting morphology of the patterned perovskite (Fig. 27c and d).576 They also imprinted other nanoscale structures, including inverted pyramids (Fig. 27e) and hole arrays (Fig. 27f). Their results revealed that controlling the crystallization dynamics of the film could tune the morphology of these structures and be used to extend to other solution-processable halide perovskites. Schmager et al. obtained enhanced absorption by coupling incident light to quasi-guided modes by texturing the perovskite layer with a period of P = 480 nm (Fig. 27g) using thermal nanoimprint lithography.563 Compared to the control film, the photo-luminescence intensity of the nanoimprinted perovskite layers could be enhanced by 80%, leading to a relative increase in PCE by 2% with increased JSC from 19.1 mA cm−2 to 19.4 mA cm−2. The PCE improvement originates from the enhanced EQE of the nanophotonic PSC in the weakly absorbing spectral region of the perovskite absorber (>680 nm). Recently, Song's group developed a facile and straightforward strategy to introduce a large area grating structure into the perovskite layer by utilizing commercial optical discs (CD-R and DVD-R).577Fig. 27h and i show the AFM images of the perovskite film imprinted by optical discs with a grating structure of ≈1.5 and ≈0.75 μm in the period, ≈1.0 and ≈0.5 μm in line width, and ≈0.1 μm in height. The constructed diffraction grating on the perovskite photoactive layer realizes nanophotonic light-trapping by diffraction and effectively suppresses carrier recombination, leading to higher PCE and JSC of 19.71% and 23.11 mA cm−2. Incident light angle versus JSC curves (Fig. 27j) show that the diffraction-grating perovskite devices would obtain high JSC at incident light angle ≈15° and ≈25°, respectively. These patterned nanophotonic structures tailor the optical properties of the perovskite film and can be effectively used to enhance the photovoltaic performance and reduce the energy loss in PSCs.
 Fig. 27  (a) Schematic of whispering-gallery structured perovskite film and light-trapping setup with whispering-gallery structure. (b) AFM image and line profile of whispering-gallery structured perovskite film. Reprinted with permission from ref. 81, copyright 2018, Elsevier; SEM (c) and AFM (d) of MAPbBr3 films prepared from a mixture of DMF and DMSO as the solvents. The nanophotonic structures of (e) inverted pyramids, (f) array of square holes imprinted into MAPbBr3. (g) AFM image of a nanoimprinted perovskite layer with a period of P = 480 nm. Reprinted with permission from ref. 563, copyright 2019, Elsevier; (h) and (i) are AFM images of perovskite films with different diffraction grating imprinted by a commercial CD and DVD. (j) Incident light angle versus JSC of PSCs. Reprinted with permission from ref. 577, copyright 2018, Wiley-VCH. (k) SEM images of the cross-sectional SEM image of CsPbBr3 based inverse opal. Reprinted with permission from ref. 579, copyright 2017, Wiley-VCH; (l) UV-vis absorption spectra of pristine perovskite and coral perovskite films, the inset shows the top views of the two films. Reprinted with permission from ref. 580 copyright 2019, Elsevier; (m) SEM images of the textured perovskite. Reprinted with permission from ref. 581, copyright 2016, Wiley-VCH. 
A template-assisted method is another effective method for preparing regularly periodic perovskite films. Hörantner et al. guide the growth of perovskite crystals through a predominantly transparent and highly ordered metal oxide honeycomb structure.578 As a result, they achieved an average visible transmittance through the active layer of around 37%. Zhou et al. demonstrated a rational strategy for fabricating all-inorganic CsPbBr3 perovskite inverse opal films via a template-assisted, spin-coating method.579 As is demonstrated in Fig. 27k, CsPbBr3 inverse opal inherits the highly ordered macroporous inverse opal structure with a clear hexagon structural unit, indicating that the infiltration and template removal method applied here does not destroy the ordered structure arising from the template. Tunable optical response performances visible to the naked eye coming from the novel optical properties of photonic bandgaps of CsPbBr3 inverse opal are witnessed, which can be effectively regulated through adjusting the composition and the irradiation angles. Lu et al. also grew a novel coral-like perovskite nanostructured layer by the facile surface modification with dimethylformamide/isopropanol as a co-solvent to enhance the light-harvesting efficiency and thus augment the photocurrent density (Fig. 27l).580 As a result, they obtained optimized PSCs with an impressive PCE of 19.47%, higher than that of 17.19% for the control device. These works report on a facile route to fabricate nanophotonic PSCs with enhanced current generation by employing thermal nanoimprint lithography.
As for the randomly arranged textures, Cheng's group formed a randomly dispersed perovskite morphology (Fig. 27m) through a thin mesoporous TiO2 seeding layer and a gas-assisted crystallization method.581 The textured morphology allows for significant improvements in the light-harvesting and charge extraction performance of the PSCs. The performance gains concomitant with this textured MAPbI3 morphology provide insights into the perovskite microstructure for photovoltaic performance enhancement. Wang's group fabricated an intriguing maze-like MAPbI3 film featuring a bilayer structure with a gas-assisted method.570,582 The maze-like MAPbI3 film enables the construction of ETL-free PSCs with a PCE of 18.5% and negligible hysteresis, which is related to the higher crystallinity and superior light-harvesting capability of the textured perovskite film. Wang et al. prepared perovskite films with ultrawide annealing windows and spontaneous growth of the surface texture by using tetramethylene sulfoxide as a Lewis base ligand in the perovskite precursor solution.537 The stable intermediate state formed by N-methyl-2-pyrrolidone could change the crystallization process and promote the formation of a surface texture. The valleys within 650–800 nm of the EQE spectrum were eliminated due to the spontaneously formed surface texture, which enhanced the photocurrent density and impressive PCE of 21.14%. The above work based on randomly arranged textures could provide concepts for designing perovskite absorbers and lays a foundation for further realization of higher photovoltaic performance and efficient light-harvesting in PSCs.
Besides the above-mentioned functional layer, the interface between the HTL and the metal back electrodes can also be patterned or textured in nanoscale for enhancing the optical absorption and reducing the energy loss in PSCs.440 Such approaches have been widely studied in DSSCs, yielding increased absorption due to the plasmonic near-field enhancement and scattering-induced guided cavity modes.141,422 Considering to apply texture in PSCs, Ginting et al. recently presented a straightforward fabrication process of an inverse micro-cone array structure textured on flexible transparent conductive electrodes via a micro-imprinting process.177 The flexible inverse micro-cone array films could exhibit a high total transmittance of about 93% and a haze of about 95%, demonstrating the promising strategy for improving the light-harvesting efficiency in PSCs in low/high-latitude locations. Long et al. found that a molecule/polymer composite HTL with a periodic microstructure for morphology replication of a corrugated Au electrode could enhance light harvesting in perovskite film.583 They identified that the cavity was the combined function of a Fabry–Pérot interferometer and LSPR for light-trapping. Wei et al. incorporated bioinspired moth-eye nanostructures into the metal back electrode via a soft imprinting technique.584 The experimental and theoretical characterization verified the broadband polarization-insensitive light scattering and surface plasmonic effects due to the patterned metal back electrode.83
In the section summary, texturing structures with dimensions comparable to the wavelengths of sunlight are considered preferential light-trapping approaches to reduce energy loss in PSCs. From the current studies, we have noticed that high refractive index materials have a better effect on the front-end light-trapping structure and large-scale methods are available for manufacturing large areas of metal patterns using template and photolithography methods. However, we should notice that directly texturing the surface of the thin semiconductor layer may degrade the material quality and increase the surface area, resulting in increased recombination losses. The minimum thickness of the cell is also limited due to the surface textures. Using this technique, it is needed to tailor the thickness of the semiconductor layer down to one hundred nanometers. Moreover, exploring the texturing technology of non-perovskite absorption layers (such as SnO2 based ETL and NiO based HTL) will simultaneously maintain effective carrier transmission and achieve sufficient optical absorption characteristics (Table 5).
