7. Anti-reflection coating for photon recycling
Currently, applying anti-reflection coating outside the PSCs has been considered as the most efficient strategy for increasing the optical path length and thus enhancing light absorption in the perovskite layer.588,589 Additionally, the anti-reflection layer can be utilized to protect the PSCs from external damage, such as heat, UV irradiation, and acid in harsh outdoor environments.590–592 As is carefully calculated by Wang et al.,593 for a 350 nm MAPbI3 perovskite layer, with the HTL and ETL being PEDOT:PSS and PC61BM, respectively, only 65% of the incident solar light can be utilized by the perovskite layer. The remaining 35% loss is shared by transmission loss in the ITO and the reflection loss at the air/glass interface. For perovskite/Si tandem configurations, the most considerable optical loss is caused by total reflection (more than 17%), based on the optical simulation by Jiang et al.130 As for PSCs, the anti-reflection layer should have excellent transmittance and negligible absorption at 300–800 nm to maximize the transmittance. To obtain an ideal anti-reflection layer, we should develop an anti-reflection layer exhibiting useful broadband and omnidirectional efficiency, low polarization sensitivity, and protection ability in the entire wavelength region absorbed by the perovskite.594 Until now, there are only a few materials with lower reflection index (n) and high optical transparency that can be fabricated as the anti-reflection layer, such as MgF2 (n = 1.39) and PDMS (n = 1.40).595 For example, periodic nanostructures consisting of PDMS nanocone arrays have been widely used as an anti-reflection layer at the air/glass interface of both perovskite single-junction,585 and tandem solar cells,596 in which the PDMS layer could exhibit a strong light-scattering effect from the synergistic effect of the appropriate pyramid size and the uneven random pyramid distribution. Usually, the anti-reflection layer made of many different structures exhibiting higher anti-reflection can be realized using various technologies, including electron-beam, nanoimprint, soft lithography, and 3D printing technologies.597,598 Anti-reflective coatings made of disordered assemblies of microscale transparent structures imprinted at the air/glass interface of solar cells have been studied far and wide.157,599 There are some different types of anti-reflective coating, including (a) textured structure in the scale of the micrometer, in which the light follows ray optics; (b) textured structure in the scale of a nanometer, in which light follows wave optics; (c) the random texture; (d) the periodic texture. Some biomimetic ones like leaf, and flower epidermis usually exhibit complex structures.161,591
In terms of textured structure in the scale of the micrometer, photonic microstructures placed at the glass side of PSCs are currently one of the practical solutions to achieve PCE enhancement.600 Recently, Min-cheol Kim et al. proved that 300 nm moth-eye structured PDMS films could reduce light reflection and therefore enhanced the PCE of ∼21%.601 The schematic illustrations and comparison of the anti-reflective effect for the 300 nm moth-eye PDMS film and bare glass are exhibited in Fig. 28a–c. Detailed measurements of the light absorption, EQE, and J–V curves revealed that the structured coatings provide broadband improvements in the generated current due to the suppression of reflected light at short wavelengths and the increment of the optical path length at longer wavelengths. Jae Su Yu's group prepared multifunctional inverted micro-pyramidal structured PDMS anti-reflection layers (Fig. 28d) via facile soft lithography for PSC applications.178 The anti-reflection layer could exhibit a higher solar weighted transmittance and a lower solar weighted reflectance than those of the bare glass. Additionally, it showed a much higher average haze ratio value of ∼93.1% (Fig. 28e), leading to the improved JSC and PCE values of 21.25 mA cm−2 and 17.74%. Furthermore, the modified device has a slower degradation rate under light irradiation and a superhydrophobic behavior in environmental conditions. Similarly, Manzoor et al. demonstrated that the application of a PDMS layer could induce the increase of JSC by 1.9 mA cm−2.587 Akshit Peer et al. demonstrated that a periodically structured microlens array could show absorption enhancement (Fig. 28f) at all wavelengths, with noticeable enhancements at 440–480 nm, 550–650 nm, and beyond ∼780 nm for the PSCs.602 Simultaneously, an external microlens array on the air–glass side of the OLED could also induce enhanced emission intensity >100% by outcoupling trapped modes in the glass substrate. Except for the PDMS layer, Bacal revealed that a ∼30% average relative increase in the stabilized power output could be achieved by the optimal poly(methyl methacrylate) as an anti-reflective coating.
 Fig. 28  Schematic refractive index profiles of the (a) ITO/glass substrate and (b) moth-eye PDMS film/ITO/glass substrate. (c) Photos demonstrate the anti-reflective effect of the 300 nm moth-eye PDMS film. Reprinted with permission from ref. 601, copyright 2019, Springer Nature; (d) 30°-tilted SEM images of the IMPS-PDMS. (e) Measured total transmittance spectra of bare glass, flat-PDMS/glass, and IMPS-PDMS/glass. Reprinted with permission from ref. 178, copyright 2016, Royal Society of Chemistry; (f) cross-section SEM image of a free-standing h-CPP. The inset represents a closer view of the h-CPP; the scale bar in the inset corresponds to 100 μm. Reprinted with permission from ref. 157, copyright 2019, Wiley-VCH; (g) absorption as a function of wavelength for PSCs with μLA of a ∼700 nm and h ∼ 800 nm. Reprinted with permission from ref. 602, copyright 2017, The Optical Society; (h) SEM image of the replica on glass, and etched Si wafer was used as a master, (i) absorption and reflection in the 200 nm perovskite layer, (j) absolute value of the electric field with super-positioned energy flow flux and absorption profiles at a wavelength of 609 nm, (k) corresponding integrated electric field of the p-ARFE cell. Reprinted with permission from ref. 604, copyright 2016, IOP Publishing. 
As for the textured structure in the scale of a nanometer, in which light follows wave optics, Martínez-Denegri et al. previously combined the periodically corrugated surface (Fig. 28g) from the photonic fibre plate with a planar interface on the opposite side of the PSCs.157 The top interface of the layered medium is formed by a periodic array of half-cylinders, which they named a half-cylinder photonic plate. It can be estimated that 97% of the incident light rays that fall on a given cylinder are directed to another cylinder upon the first bounce on the bottom of the optical medium. Compare to the traditional method where angular randomization leading to an ergodic optical geometry was not confirmed, they confirmed that the enhanced JSC was accurately predicted by a ray optic model, which established ergodicity in the light propagation in the half-cylinder photonic plate structure. Marko Još et al. obtained 1 mA cm−2 increase in the JSC by applying a foil created by UV nanoimprint lithography (Fig. 28h) on the glass side of PSCs.603 The optical simulation results revealed that the improved performance is mainly due to the reduction of total reflection. Mohammad Mahdi Tavakoli et al. reported a PDMS-based nanocone array anti-reflection layer duplicated from the anodized Al foil on the front side of the glass substrate to improve the light transmittance.585 Truong Khang Nguyen et al. proposed PSCs with a top anti-reflection front electrode to generate lensing/anti-reflecting effects and thus resulting in improved absorption.604 By theoretical and experimental analysis, they demonstrated that the optimized PSCs with a 200 nm perovskite layer could exhibit a JSC of 20.2 mA cm−2, with broadband absorption characteristic at multiple visible frequencies (Fig. 28j). Additionally, there is a broad absorption within the ITO/TiO2 layer and zero reflection at a longer wavelength of 609 nm (Fig. 28k), which is associated with the whispering galley mode corresponding to low reflection. Above all, it is theoretically and experimentally demonstrated that the enhanced light absorption factor measured is close to the maximum possible for light-trapping schemes.605
Considering the random texture structure, Huang's group developed a strategy to reduce the refractive index of the silica by adjusting the refractive indices of silica coatings by introducing core–shell PS@SiO2 nanoparticles or PS nanoparticle fillers.599 Thermal decomposition of PS templates enabled the formation of hollow or mesoporous structured silica films to enhance the porosity and hence reduce the refractive indices. The application of structured silica anti-reflective coating in PSCs can effectively reduce specular light reflection in the broad wavelength range, leading to reduced specular Fresnel reflection in the broad wavelength range of 400–800 nm and thereby enhanced PCE of 20.3%, respectively. Furthermore, the high hydrophobicity and chemical inertness of the silica render the substrate a self-protection property against dust and water uptake and excellent long-term stability under outdoor operational conditions.
Except for the typical texture and random structures, approaches to enhance the photovoltaic performance of solar cells by employing the highly transparent biomimetic architectures as anti-reflective or haze surfaces have been widely studied.606–612 Liang et al. optimized the biomimetic leaf-type hierarchical nanostructure for ultra-thin PSC application from a theoretical perspective by the FDTD method combined with the particle swarm optimization algorithm. They proved that the biomimetic leaf-type hierarchical nanostructure could enhance the light absorption by a maximum of 39% at the long wavelength, with an increased photocurrent by 8.4% than that of control devices.161 From the view of the experiment, Kim et al. adopted a fast soft-imprint lithography patterning method (Fig. 29a) to create a transparent anti-reflection layer with microarchitectures of inverted hemispherical architectured-PDMS and hemispherical architectured-PDMS layers.613 The measured total transmittance and reflectance spectra of the biomimetic architectures (Fig. 29b) laminated on bare glass indicate that the inverted hemispherical architecture could exhibit superior transmittance and average reflectance value of 89.2% and 6.4%, respectively. Also, the inverted hemispherical architectured-PDMS/glass also exhibited a relatively strong light-scattering property with a higher average haze ratio of ∼38%, which is favorable to the enhanced PCE from 16.21 to 19.00%, with a significantly enhanced JSC from 19.91 to 23.29 mA cm−2. Yoo et al. developed a newly nanopatterned MgF2/SiO2 anti-reflection structure (Fig. 29c) by combining the single-layer MgF2 with a two-dimensional moth-eye structure.612 Because the anti-reflective film can be attached to the glass surface of PSCs and predominantly reduce the surface reflection losses over a broad range of incident wavelengths and angles, the improved light absorption and PCE were easily achieved. Li et al. reported the biomimetic haze film to reduce the reflection loss in PSCs by one-step replication of the surface texture of rose petals with low-cost PDMS (Fig. 29d), which exhibits a high transmission haze of 75% and ultra-high diffusion transmittance of 97%.159 At small incidence angles, biomimetic haze films could significantly enhance the light absorption of PSCs by as much as 2.7 times, leading to a significant photon recycling effect, especially for those photovoltaic devices installed in high latitude areas. Dudem et al. adopted a facile soft-imprint lithography technique to create an anti-reflective film for PSC applications.614 In particular, they applied sandpaper as a master mold to produce sandpaper-aided haze film (Fig. 29e). These microgroove architectures on the surface of sandpaper-aided haze film can help to achieve an optimized PCE of 20.34% for PSCs on account of their linear-gradient refractive-index profile. Furthermore, sandpaper-aided haze film effectively lowered the surface temperature of the device under continuous light soaking, which promises an important role of sandpaper-aided haze film as a heat resistant layer for solar cells. Silver ants with hairs could play a crucial role in reducing the body temperature via enhanced optical reflection and radiative heat dissipation as a passive daytime radiative cooler. Inspired by the hierarchical feature structure of the hairs, Lin et al. fabricated the flexible photonic architectures on PDMS in both PSCs and commercial c-Si solar cells for enhancing the light-harvesting, with increased PCE by 6.75% and 3.23%, respectively (Fig. 29f).615 The above studies demonstrate a facile way to enhance the performance of PSCs with a planar front surface.
 Fig. 29  (a) Schematic diagram of the fabrication procedure for the inverted hemispherical architectured-PDMS and hemispherical architectured-PDMS layers. (b) Measured total transmittance spectra. Reprinted with permission from ref. 613, copyright 2018, American Chemistry Society; (c) cross-sectional SEM images of the MgF2/SiO2 anti-reflection structure with a low refractive index layer. Reprinted with permission from ref. 612, copyright 2020, American Chemistry Society; (d) (d-1) schematic illustration of the fabrication process of the biomimetic haze film, (d-2) digital image of a rose petal mould and the biomimetic haze film replicated from the mould, (d-3) SEM image of the top surface of the rose petal, (d-4) SEM image of the top and the tilted surface of the biomimetic haze film, the scale bar is 30 mm. Reprinted with permission from ref. 159, copyright 2017, Royal Society of Chemistry; (e) photographic image demonstrating the easy lamination and detachable ability of sandpaper-aided haze film. Reprinted with permission from ref. 614, copyright 2019, American Chemistry Society; (f) J–V curves of the PSCs with and without the FPA-PDMS. The insets are the photographs of the control and FPA-PDMS modified PSCs. Reprinted with permission from ref. 615, copyright 2019, Elsevier. 
In the above, Fresnel reflection at the air–substrate interface of a PSC inevitably occurs because of the contrast of refractive index between air and transparent substrates, and it has been one of the essential optical losses for the photovoltaic performance of PSCs. Depositing a cost-effective anti-reflection coating with a quarter-wavelength thickness on the transparent substrate has been considered as one of the most feasible methods to reduce Fresnel reflection-induced energy loss. What needs to be the key consideration for the future is the long-term stability of anti-reflection coatings at a realistic condition, including light irradiation, rainfall, climate change, sandstorm, surface cleaning, etc.
In addition to single solar cells, using PSCs as the top cell of tandem devices is a future application direction of high-efficiency photovoltaic.616 The optics of the interconnection layers is essential for single-junction solar cells and even more critical for multijunction solar cells like perovskite/Si tandem solar cells.617,618 The front contact is part of the junction of the solar cell and must provide lateral charge transport to the terminals for an efficient light coupling while having low optical losses. The interconnection layer of a PSC usually needs to have four functions. First, the interconnection layer must have good conductivity so that the carrier can be transferred efficiently. Second, the interconnection layer must be able to effectively couple the incident light in the solar cell. It can be combined with the diffraction or refraction of incident light to increase the optical path length of the light in the solar cell, thereby improving the quantum efficiency. Usually, this can be achieved by texturing the interconnection layer. Third, the absorption loss of the interconnection layer should be as low as possible, that is, the interconnection layer cannot block the incident light.619 From the result in Fig. 30a and b, a 150 nm thick perovskite top cell usually suffers from reflection loss at short wavelengths (λ < 500 nm) and weak absorption at wavelengths 500 < λ < 730 nm, while the Si-based bottom cell suffers significant reflection losses at wavelengths λ > 800 nm. The integrated reflection loss will induce JSC loss estimated to be 4.1 mA cm−2 for a planar solar cell.620 For overcoming the current loss, Bennett W. Schneider et al. showed that conformal deposition of thin PSCs directly onto the textured front surface of an efficient Si-based solar cell could yield lower reflection losses and induce an improved PCE by 2–4%.620 Using FDTD simulations, Stephen Foster et al. demonstrated that the ideal light-trapping and absorption could be achieved over the 300–1100nm wavelength range by using a photonic crystal in perovskite/Si tandem solar cells.333 Recently, Zhang's group designed anti-reflective foils for perovskite/Si tandem solar cell applications (Fig. 30c) by carrying random-pyramidal textures with three different pyramid size ranges.160 Consequently, laminating the PDMS-based anti-reflection coating onto the front surface of tandem solar cells could induce an increased PCE from 19.38% to 21.93% (Fig. 30d and e). Based on the previous works, they also systematically reviewed the strategies of the reflective layer in the perovskite/Si solar cells in series, aiming to guide further improvement of the tandem devices.66,621 Readers can refer to this section in detail.
 Fig. 30  (a) Absorption curves in front and bottom subcells, (b) front-surface reflectance and the corresponding equivalent reflected JSC values. Reprinted with permission from ref. 620, copyright 2014, The Optical Society; (c) the structure of a tandem solar cell, (d) EQE of the front and bottom cells for the tandem device with and without PDMS anti-reflection coating; (e) J–V curves of the tandem solar cell with and without PMDS coatings. Reprinted with permission from ref. 160, copyright 2019, Elsevier; (f) schematic structure of the PSC devices with PDMS film attached to the top. (g) Photograph of PSCs based on flexible willow glass integrated with a PDMS layer. (h) J–V curves of PSCs with and without PDMS film. Reprinted with permission from ref. 585, copyright 2015, American Chemistry Society. 
The outdoor applications are particularly important for PSCs because the accumulation of water on the front surface would induce decreased performance degradation. Therefore, making the arc hydrophobic helps protect equipment from contamination and degradation. Tavakoli applied a nanocone array on the front side of the glass substrate to improve the optical transmittance and to achieve a water-repelling effect of PSCs (Fig. 30f).585Fig. 30g shows the photograph of a nanocone film attached on the PSC. The J–V curves in Fig. 30h revealed that the JSC was increased from 17.7 mA cm−2 to 19.3 mA cm−2 after attaching the nanocone anti-reflection film on the top side of the solar cell. The fabricated solar cells have reasonable bendability, with 96% of the initial value remaining after 200 bending cycles, demonstrating excellent superhydrophobicity. Tavakoli et al. also showed that the PCE of inverted nanocone substrate-based PSCs could be improved by 37%.586 More interestingly, the PCE of an inverted device could remain more than 90% of its initial value after 200 mechanical bending cycles. In conclusion, the antireflection layer can improve the hydrophobicity and bending resistance of PSCs.
Considering the effect of reflection in practice, one of the most important directions is utilized for building-integrated photovoltaics, for which the colors of building exteriors demonstrate the traditional, cultural, and religious features of cities.622–632 Recently, strategies reported for the color tuning of photovoltaics, such as composition and crystallization tuning,633 photonic crystal,634 plasmonics,635–637 dielectric multilayer films,638–640 and structure design strategies,641,642 have been developed for power-generating windows. For example, Huang's group reported colorful PSCs by preparing the perovskite layer having a combination of a hundred-micrometre size large domain structure and a concentric ring photonics structure in each domain which could generate the vivid color.633 Recently, our group present a study of integrating CsPbBr3 nanocrystals inside of PSCs to induce specific colorful devices under UV illumination.237 We observed green and red fluorescence can be observed for the MAPbI3 based PSCs, which is related to the I−/Br− ions exchange in MAPbI3 layer and CsPbBr3 nanocrystals. Zhang et al. integrated a porous photonic crystal scaffold within the perovskite photoactive layer by employing scalable liquid processing techniques,634 as is illustrated from the schematic structure and cross-sectional SEM image of the PSC in Fig. 31a and b. Tuning the reflectance peak of the photonic crystal by modifying the thickness of the individual layers could exhibit intense colors variable across the visible spectrum. Wang et al. demonstrated efficient bifacial colorful PCSs using inorganic CuSCN as the HTL and ITO electrode with different thicknesses.643
 Fig. 31  (a) Schematic diagram of a photonic crystal-based PSC. (b) Cross-sectional SEM image of a photonic-crystal-based device. Reprinted with permission from ref. 634, copyright 2015, American Chemistry Society; (c) scheme of the structure of narrow-bandwidth reflective filters formed by nonperiodic stacking of alternatively high-refractive-index and low-refractive-index layers. (d) The gradual change of the reflected color of the narrow-bandwidth reflective filters with an angular variation. Reprinted with permission from ref. 641, copyright 2019, American Chemistry Society; (e) device structure of the PSCs with conducting polymer as the top electrode. (f) Photographic image of colorful PSCs. Reprinted with permission from ref. 646, copyright 2016, American Chemistry Society; (g) spectral transmittance curves of the PSCs. Reprinted with permission from ref. 647, copyright 2016, American Chemistry Society. 
The use of a multi-nanolayered reflective color-generated filter by periodically or nonperiodically alternating depositing the low-refractive-index and high-refractive-index is another strategy to tune the color of solar cells without modifying the photoactive layers.641,644,645 Yoo et al. fabricated narrow-bandwidth reflective filters by multilayering high-index TiO2/low-index SiO2 in a nonperiodic manner.641Fig. 31c shows a schematic figure of narrow-bandwidth reflective filters with two different structures (periodic and nonperiodic stacking) of multiple nanolayers. These narrow-bandwidth reflective filters are composed of low-refractive-index SiO2 (1.45) layers and high-refractive-index TiO2 (2.31) layers. Various peak wavelengths and full-width at half-maximum values of narrow-bandwidth reflective filters are demonstrated by manipulating optical interference effects between the high- and low-index layers. The photostability of the PSCs can be substantially improved by the UV blocking effect. Fig. 31d shows the gradual change of the reflected color of the narrow-bandwidth reflective filters with an angular variation. According to the reflectance spectra of the narrow-bandwidth reflective filters, all spectra are blue-shifted when the viewing angle increases. This pearl-like color formation concerning viewing angle was beneficial for use in building-integrated PSCs. Except for the above method, localized resonances in ultrathin metal nano-strip optical resonators consisting of an array of metallic subwavelength nanowires on a transparent substrate could also lead to a sharp peak in a reflection spectrum for a distinctive color generation with angle-insensitive properties up to 60°.635 By using the PEDOT:PSS instead of conventional Au as a top electrode and spectrally selective anti-reflection coating (Fig. 31e), Jiang et al. found that thickness optimization of the PEDOT:PSS allowed tuning the reflectance spectra to obtain colorful PSCs.646 The PCE of these colorful PSCs can reach up to 15% and 13% when illuminated from the FTO side and the polymer side, respectively (Fig. 31f). Lu et al. obtained colorful PSCs exhibiting strong coloration: reddish-orange, orange, yellow, yellow-green, yellowish-green, bluish-green, and blue by embedding precisely controllable Ag/ITO/Ag nanostructures (Fig. 31g).647 These results indicate that crystallization tuning, photonic crystals, plasmonics, and multilayer design could be used as promising strategies for building-integrated photovoltaics.
In the section summary, using a broadband anti-reflective coating to cover the entire spectrum wavelength range acceptable to solar cells can reduce the reflection by more than 70%, thereby improving the PCE and reducing light pollution of PSCs. High refractive index materials have a better effect on the front-end light-trapping structure. Also, an anti-reflection layer can protect PSCs from external impact, heat, ultraviolet radiation, and corrosive acid rain in a harsh outdoor environment. Anti-reflection structure or coatings in PSCs has been widely studied in recent years, especially the biomimetic anti-reflection layer composed of nano and microstructures. However, the production of such biomimetic nanostructures and microstructures now involves complicated and expensive manufacturing processes, such as lithography/patterning and etching processes, as well as sophisticated stripping techniques. In future research, it is necessary to develop industrial means, including laser micro–nano processing and electron-beam lithography, to produce an anti-reflection layer in a large area, which requires more innovation in theory and technology (Table 6).

8. Luminescent solar concentrators for light concentration
Solar concentration technology has been considered as a practical approach to reducing the cost of traditional photovoltaics by decreasing their active area used for generating the same amount of electrical power.648 The representative LSCs possess the superiority for providing adaptability (colors, shapes, transparencies, lightweight, and flexibility) to semi-transparent building-integrated photovoltaics.321,649 Basically, the LSC is a device that embeds a transparent matrix of fluorescent material.165,168 When exposed to sunlight, the fluorescent material absorbs incident radiation, re-emitted at a specific wavelength, and transferred to the solar cell at the edge of the matrix by total internal reflection. A portion of the emitted signal is lost on the surface, while the remaining signal is captured by total internal reflection into the edge attached photovoltaic cell, as shown in Fig. 32a. If the photon input/photon output efficiency is high enough, the electricity cost generated by the LSC may be competitive. The most widely used fluorescent materials in the LSC emission layer are dyes, polymers, quantum dots, transition metals, and RE doped phosphors.650–655 Quantum dots have broadband absorption and efficient emission in the visible region or NIR emission, which match narrow-bandgap photovoltaics in the spectrum.656 To obtain an efficient LSC, researchers need to make efforts to reduce the reabsorption of emitted light in the absorbing medium while maintaining the high photoluminescence quantum yield.
 Fig. 32  (a) Working mechanism of LSCs. Reprinted with permission from ref. 173, copyright 2014, Royal Society of Chemistry; (b) picture of perovskite nanocrystal based LSCs. Reprinted with permission from ref. 179, copyright 2019, Elsevier; (c) design principles of LSCs based on ultrafast narrowband exciton routing; (d) photograph of a 10 cm × 10 cm quasi-two-dimensional perovskite nanoplatelet-based LSC. Reprinted with permission from ref. 172, copyright 2019, Springer Nature; (e) absorption and luminescence spectra of Mn2+-doped CsPbCl3 nanocrystals (4% doping level). Reprinted with permission from ref. 167, copyright 2017, American Chemistry Society. (f) Left is absorption, PL spectra of the undoped and Mn2+/Yb3+ codoped CsPbCl3 nanocrystals. Insets: Schematics of the perovskite crystal structures and photographs of the samples under UV illumination (365 nm). Right is the TEM images of undoped and Mn2+/Yb3+ codoped CsPbCl3 nanocrystals. Reprinted with permission from ref. 654, copyright 2020, Wiley-VCH; (g) photograph of dual LSCs coupled with PSCs; (h) J–V curves for the PSCs coupled with different types of LSCs measured under 1 sun illumination. Reprinted with permission from ref. 660, copyright 2014, Royal Society of Chemistry. 
Compared with the typical PSCs, the advantage of perovskite quantum dot-based LSC is the reduction of the active area of solar cells, which can decrease the utilization of expensive hole transport materials and transparent conductive oxide materials as much as possible, thereby reducing the costs associated with energy conversion.173 The overall physical drawing of perovskite-based LSCs is illustrated in Fig. 32b,179 giving the photovoltaic modules the ability to concentrate the maximum amount of light energy at their edges for power generation.657 Perovskite quantum dot-based LSC configurations can be used in the traditional Si-based solar cells or the PSCs attached at the device edges.174,658,659 Wei et al. used quasi-two-dimensional perovskite nanoplatelets with a large Stokes shift and a high photoluminescence quantum yield (Fig. 32c) to provide optical quantum efficiency of 26% and internal concentration factors of 3.3 for 10 cm × 10 cm LSC panels (Fig. 32d).172 Meinardi et al. demonstrated the suitability of Mn2+-doped CsPbCl3 nanocrystals as reabsorption-free emitters for large-area LSC applications. By Monte Carlo ray-tracing simulations of light propagation using the experimental absorption and photoluminescence spectra, they revealed that the dopant photoluminescence is attributed to the resonance with the vibrational modes of the ligand molecules (Fig. 32e). As a result, the Mn2+-doped CsPbCl3 nanocrystals combined with the polymer-based LSCs could result in high optical quality waveguides, in which scattering losses are less than the weak reabsorption by the matrix material.
Current studies focus mainly on undoped or single ions doped into the host perovskite nanocrystals, while few research efforts have been made on simultaneously doping more than one type of metal ion into the same perovskite host and intra-particle energy transfer processes for higher f photoluminescence quantum yield and wider spectral range.654 Cai et al. report a Mn2+/Yb3+ codoped CsPbCl3 nanocrystal with a unique triple-wavelength emission profile simultaneously covering UV/blue, visible, and NIR spectral regions (Fig. 32f) owing to the separated energy relaxation channels among the intrinsic bandgap, Mn2+, and Yb3+ dopant emissions.654 Both the experimental and simulation results indicate that the internal optical efficiency and external optical efficiency of the codoped nanocrystals increased dramatically compared to the undoped or single Mn2+ doped CsPbCl3 nanocrystals. From the early research work, we can find that most of the LSCs are based on commercial Si-based solar cells. The previous study indicates that the PSCs could exhibit an absorption coefficient of about ten times higher than Si-based solar cells in the visible light wavelength band. Kim et al. prepared a dual LSC panel (Fig. 32g) consisting of a perylene-based DC layer that absorbs violet light and a meso-tetraphenyl-tetrabenzoporphine palladium complex and perylene based UC layer to upconvert the NIR light into higher energy blue light, resulting in both dual panels exhibiting blue light emission.660 The dual LSCs combined with a PSC could exhibit a high average-visible-transmittance of 82%, long-term stability, and a maximum PCE of 7.53% (Fig. 32h).
Above all, as nearly zero reabsorption emitters, the perovskite nanocrystal-based LSCs could reduce the energy loss using smaller photovoltaic modules and enhance the PCE by increasing the scattered light collection. Considering the current status and challenges associated with the rapid developments in the field of building and car integrated photovoltaics, perovskite nanocrystal-based LSCs could provide adaptability to the needs of the architect in that they can be made in a variety of colors, shapes, and transparencies, leading to flexible and lighter weight photovoltaic devices. Although perovskite nanocrystals have the potential to generate low-cost solar energy, LSCs based on perovskite nanocrystals face various challenges, most of which are related to the materials used in the design. Doping trivalent lanthanide ions (such as Yb3+, Y3+, Ce3+, Eu3+) into perovskite nanocrystals is a promising strategy to improve the fluorescence intensity and expand the luminescence spectrum, aiming to improve the collection efficiency and photoelectric conversion of solar energy.
9. Conclusions and perspectives
In summary, we have reviewed the progress on various photon managements and the corresponding mechanisms for reducing energy loss in PSCs, which is an attractive branch, and has achieved fruitful and exciting results. The relationship between the optical management structure and photovoltaic performance of PSCs has received particular attention. The integrated hot topics of photo conversion, tandem, plasma, texturing, anti-reflection, and LSC promise to open up new prospects for finding feasible solutions to reach and go beyond the efficiency limitation of PSCs. After a brief overview of the theoretical basis on photon management, we systematically introduce some studies that have successfully applied PSC devices to harvest more photons and realize photovoltaic transformation. The views summarized and the topics discussed here are expected to deepen the current understanding and technical status of spectral regulation in PSCs. Owing to the limited bandgap of perovskite, photon management will be the key throughout the development of PSCs, and they will soon reach the research outbreak period. This review will be useful to researchers from the fields of chemistry, physics, optoelectronics, biology, and even energy storage.
We identify the following critical issues for future research principles to reduce the energy loss in PSCs.
(1) Evidence reflects that the reduced energy loss achieved from UC drives increased photocurrent, but further study is required to confirm the deep mechanism to distinguish and determine whether the main factor is UC, light scattering, or enhanced perovskite crystallinity. In future research, researchers can think in three ways. Firstly, from the view of the device structure, the UC single-crystal outside of the PSCs, or as a transparent substrate (in situ growth of a transparent conductive film on the surface single-crystal) is the top way to achieve UC applications because it can maintain the high fluorescence efficiency of the bulk material and avoid the negative effects of dielectric properties on photovoltaic performance. Secondly, considering the UC matrix, most reported REs were mainly doped into insulating materials (such as NaYF4, LiYF4, etc.), unable to realize direct photoelectric conversion. For future research, RE doped semiconductor-based UC materials, especially for the perovskite UC nanocrystals, have great potential in achieving effective UC luminescence for reducing energy loss in PSCs. Thirdly, the use of new sensitizers, such as dye sensitization or a new type of quantum dot sensitization, is possible to achieve efficient UC luminescence. Researchers can develop more efficient NIR absorption dyes to achieve effective energy transfer (it should have an emission that overlaps with the UC ion absorption band). Research on quantum dot sensitization and enhanced UC luminescence has not been carried out, and it can be used as a new research field to carry out related research. Fourthly, the UC fluorescence intensity of RE doped UC nanomaterials needs to be further enhanced by combining the precious metal nanostructures with the UC nanomaterials to modulate the incident light synergistically. At least, another entry point is the concentrator, such as at 100 times sunlight, for UC-based PSC applications. The fluorescence of UC materials is excited by high-energy and high-intensity light, which is then utilized by the perovskite. These means for UC enhancement are of great significance for future applications in PSCs.
(2) As for the DC effect, future research can be carried out in the following aspects. Firstly, in order to solve the issues of narrow excitation band, weak absorption, and Yb3+ concentration quenching of DC materials, ions with f–d transition or charge transport state absorption should be selected as co-sensitized ions (such as Tb3+, etc.) to achieve broadband absorption. The co-sensitized ions can efficiently transfer the absorbed energy to the DC ion combination. Secondly, using RE doped perovskite quantum dots (CsPbCl3:Yb3+,CsPb(Cl0.35Br0.65)3:Yb3+) can achieve higher quantum cutting than 100% (193% for the highest now), which can significantly improve the utilization in UV and the PCE of PSCs in future research. Thirdly, aiming at the problems of stability and fluorescence efficiency drop caused by aggregation, the SiO2 coating on the surface of semiconductor nanocrystals can effectively maintain the high fluorescence intensity while improving the environmental moisture stability and fluorescence efficiency. This is of considerable significance to realize the internal integration and external coating of quantum dot materials, not only in PSCs but also in Si-based and thin-film solar cells. Finally, moisture and oxygen exposure can be mostly prevented via suitable encapsulation, leaving high-energy UV exposure as the most crucial susceptibility to overcome in PSCs. The UV-induced degradation is primarily attributed to photocatalytic effects caused by the most commonly used TiO2 in the ETL. Compared with the most commonly used UV filters, using perovskite nanocrystals with high fluorescence quantum efficiency, especially inorganic highly durable perovskite (Cs2AgBiBr6, AgBi2I7, Cs3Bi2I9, Cs3Sb2I9, etc.) nanocrystals, could simultaneously enhance the UV stability by protecting the perovskite from harmful UV radiation, promote the nucleation and crystallization process of the perovskite through good lattice matching, and improve the carrier transport characteristics at the interface between the perovskite and the carrier transport layer.
(3) Considering the research on perovskite-based tandem solar cells, the NIR dyes exhibit narrow absorption spectra and retain serious air stability issues. Recently, the development of organic polymer photovoltaic materials has been rapid. The combination of perovskite and durable organic materials to prepare a “perovskite/organic” hybrid or tandem solar cell can compensate for the absorption of the perovskite in the NIR region. At the same time, organic materials have the advantages of tunable energy band and controllable semiconductor properties, which can be used as an effective carrier transport layer to achieve more efficient electron transport and ultimately improve the photovoltaic performance of the PSCs. This is an auspicious research direction, and researchers from the subject of chemical synthesis and materials engineering can carry out more in-depth exploration from the perspective of molecular chemistry, designing NIR organic materials with high absorption at 800–1100 nm. Also, researchers in physics and electronics can optimize the device structure and internal electrical impedance to achieve the minimum charge loss and improve the optical response of perovskite to the NIR region. The tandem strategy may be the most direct pathway and the fastest route to realizing a practical enhancement in PCE for commercially available PSCs. For all perovskite tandem solar cells, the segregation of halogen ions (I/Br) in wide bandgap-based PSCs is the primary cause of VOC loss, which is an insurmountable gap to cross. Developing and introducing pseudohalogen ions (SCN−, COOH−, PF6−, BF4−) with similar ion radius and excellent chemical stability to dope or substitute the halogen anions (Cl−, Br−, I−) could be a feasible strategy to induce the uniform distribution of elements and reduce the phase separation, and further control the crystallinity and carrier transport characteristics (carrier lifetime, carrier diffusion length). Researchers can focus on exploring the regulatory effects some new pseudohalogen ion doping in wide-bandgap perovskites, which is the key to improve PCE further and reduce energy loss by tandem configuration. In the next research step, with the variety of low-bandgap based optoelectronic materials and the continuous growth of PCE, researchers should pay more attention to commercial-related issues, such as how to realize the flexibility of stacked devices and large-area module manufacturing by screen printing, blade-coating, and vacuum integrated evaporation.
(4) As for the SPR effect, although theoretical studies indicated an effective way to take advantage of the plasmonic nanostructures in photon management strategies, most of the reports showed relatively low or moderate efficiency improvements in PSC. It is essential to intelligently design and implement controlled plasmonic metal nanostructures with the required size, shape, and morphology. Just as importantly, the method of integrating plasmonic metal nanostructures into these PSCs is another major factor to consider. Researchers can further explore the interaction mechanism of metal nanostructures and their surface plasmon effects with excitons and carriers in the active layer to understand better the enhancement mechanism, including electrical and optical properties and their respective ratios. A comprehensive scientific study by associating experimental results with simulation results is crucial for revealing the basic knowledge of the plasmonic effect in PSCs. An unavoidable problem of direct contact of metal nanoparticles in the perovskite photoactive layer makes them act as the carrier recombination centers, limiting the improvement of device efficiency. In future research, coating ultrathin oxide shells (ZnO, SnO2, Nb2O5) on the surface of plasmonic metal nanostructures can effectively avoid the recombination of holes and electrons and expand the application range of metal nanoparticles in the device from far away from the active layer to the inside. Meanwhile, the device structure and film thickness should be optimized by adjusting the resonance absorption spectrum of the nanoparticles and the dielectric properties of the shell layer, aiming to maximize the surface plasmon resonance effect of the metal nanoparticles significantly. There are still many scientific and technical issues related to photonic regulation, interface modification, and optical characterization techniques that distinguish PSCs from traditional photovoltaic devices. Sometimes, researchers need to find a compromise between optical and electrical aspects if we aim at using photon management in PSCs with already high efficiency.
(5) Considering the unique structure-based texturing and anti-reflection effect, we have noticed that large-scale methods are available for manufacturing using template and photolithography methods. In the future, it is necessary to develop low-cost soft lithography methods to manufacture pyramid microstructures based on semiconductor oxides, which involves coating or attaching a material to the external surface of a transparent substrate. The advantages of texturing structures include the realization of PSCs with different colors, which improve the aesthetic appeal of PSCs. Using a broadband anti-reflective coating to cover the entire spectrum wavelength range acceptable to PSCs can reduce the reflection by more than 70%, thereby improving the efficiency of PSCs and reducing light pollution of photovoltaics. In future research, anti-reflection coatings should be cost-effective, thin, and easy to manufacture. Using inexpensive MgF2 and other anti-reflective materials as a high-quality layer on FTO by the physical deposition method will contribute to reducing the optical loss of PSCs. As for the material design for anti-reflection, Reststrahlen materials could exhibit Reststrahlen frequency when they are exposed to radiation close to the wavelength that causes lattice vibration. This change causes the extinction coefficient and Fresnel reflection coefficient of the material to rise, causing a sharp increase in reflectivity, which could, in turn, improve the utilization of photons in PSCs.
(6) Finally, recent results demonstrate the potential of perovskite nanocrystals for LSC-based integrated photovoltaic technologies as well as for other photon management applications relying on low-attenuation long-range optical waveguiding. It is proved that the perovskite nanocrystals cannot be affected by the spectral overlap between the absorption spectrum and the emission spectrum, exhibiting an excellent regulatory effect in traditional photovoltaics. The excellent light intensity tolerance makes PSCs an ideal choice for edge photovoltaics and effectively utilize the light energy concentrated through the LSC. In air conditions, however, perovskite nanocrystals and PSCs are still humidity sensitive. As a result, their luminescence properties will quickly degrade over a couple of days or weeks. Therefore, researchers should consider encapsulating the perovskite nanocrystals with a protecting layer and using them inside a double glass window.
Each photon management strategy reviewed above has its exclusive advantages for reducing energy loss. For example, UC is aimed at NIR light; DC is aimed at high-energy UV light and light stability; scattering and texturing are aimed at visible light that is not fully utilized and ultra-thin semi-transparent PSCs; anti-reflection can minimize the reflection and refraction loss of incident light (widely used in crystalline silicon cells); LSC can help to achieve building integrated photovoltaics and the miniaturization of solar cell modules. We expect researchers to consider the synergistic effect of multiple photon management mechanisms as much as possible, to achieve more efficient optoelectronic applications, including tandem, colorful, flexible, semi-transparent PSCs (Fig. 33), as well as in the field of photodetectors, phototransistors, photoelectrochemical sensors, photocatalysis, and especially light-emitting diodes.
 Fig. 33  Photon management strategies (UC, DC, tandem, SPR, texturing, antireflection) to reduce energy loss for PSCs (tandem, colorful, flexible, semi-transparent) and future multi-field applications (photoelectrochemical sensor, photocatalysis, and light-emitting diodes). The tandem image of a PSC. Table Content:

Cong Chen
	Cong Chen received his PhD degree from Jilin University in June 2019. Previously, he studied and did scientific research at Jilin University focused on perovskites, and quantum dots sensitized solar cells under the supervision of Prof. Hongwei Song. In July 2019, he joined Hebei University of technology as an associate professor. He is the youngest special-term “Yuanguang scholar” in Hebei University of Technology. His main topic of interest is the design of solar cells having high efficiency.

Hongwei Song
	Hongwei Song received his PhD degree in Condensed Material Physics from Changchun Institute of Physics, Chinese Academy of Science (CAS) in 1996. From 1996 to 2000, he worked as a postdoctoral researcher in the Institute of Physics, CAS, Nagoya Institute of Technology, and University of California at Berkeley in turn. Since 2007 he has worked at Jilin University as a full professor. He is currently an editorial advisory board member of Scientific Reports. He has published over 300 scientific papers and two book chapters. His research interests have been focused on the spectral physics of rare-earth ions and their optoelectronic applications.

 	Fig. 6  (a) Energy diagram shows different behaviors schematically among a DC and a downshifting material used to harvest the photo in a solar cell. (b) Optical processes and losses for a downshifting layer placed on top of a solar cell. Absorbed and downshifted photons hit the underlying solar cell directly via total reflection or re-absorption and re-emission. Non-absorbed photons are transmitted without a change of wavelength into the solar cell. Optical losses are due to reflection at the front side and interfaces. Due to isotropic emission, individual fractions of down-shifted photons escape through the front side escape cone and the side of the downshifting layer. Reprinted with permission from ref. 108, copyright 2014, Elsevier; (c) simulated absorbance in the typical organic solar cell without (dashed line) and with (full line) the downshifting layer applied to the front side of the cell. Reprinted with permission from ref. 110, copyright 2015, Elsevier; (d) downshifting for CIGS-based solar cells with a mixture of two organic dyes: (d) the violet organic dye absorbs strongly in the low-wavelength region, the yellow organic dye between 400 nm and 500 nm. (e) Fitting the EQE spectrum with a constant LDS efficiency results in small errors because of a change of ηPL for the short-λ two-step absorption and re-emission process. A wavelength-depended LDS efficiency perfectly fits the experimental data. Reprinted with permission from ref. 108, copyright 2014, Elsevier; (f) schematic of the solar cell structures considered in the simulations DC enhanced UV utilization. (g) and (h) Stand for the results for the structure of the solar cell with nBackground of 1.5. Panels (g) and (h) are the absorption profiles calculated for the PSCs with thicknesses of 250 nm and 500 nm, respectively. Reprinted with permission from ref. 113, copyright 2018, Run.
